Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
12 min
The Option of Choice in Delegated Divorce: Actions That Break or Preserve It
Clarifying when the wife's option remains valid during the choice period.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions break the wife's option of choice (khiyar) in delegated divorce (tafweedh) and explain why intending three talags upon saying 'ikhtari' is invalid.

Core Idea
The wife's option is lost only by actions indicating aversion; the word 'ikhtari' cannot bear the intention of three talags.

In delegated divorce, the husband gives the wife a choice to divorce herself. During the option period, her actions may indicate whether she is still considering or has turned away. This lesson clarifies what actions preserve or invalidate that option, including the nuanced rulings on mounts, ships, and the meaning of 'ikhtari' itself. Understanding these distinctions prevents common mistakes in applying this sensitive area of fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Classify actions into those indicating aversion (i'rad) and those that do not
Identify from the text which actions (like stopping the mount, sitting down, reclining) are deemed non-aversive, and which (like walking or moving the mount) indicate aversion. Note the underlying criterion: the action must show disinterest.
The key is whether the action is attributed to the wife's choice and manifests a desire to leave.
2
Analyze the ship analogy
Understand why the ship is treated like a house: its movement is not attributed to the rider, so moving within it does not break the option. Compare this to the mount, whose movement is under the rider's control.
This analogy extends to modern vehicles like trains or cars where movement is not by the passenger's choice.
3
Examine the objection and answer regarding 'ikhtari'
The objection claims that 'ikhtari' can imply either minor or major separation, like the explicit word 'baa'in'. The answer is that 'ikhtari' means 'I choose myself', which necessarily requires only minor separation (ba'in sughra) because the implication (muqtada) is limited to necessity.
The Hanafi position relies on the principle that a necessary implication has no generality.
4
Derive the ruling for each scenario
From the above analysis, state the ruling for each action: stopping mount = no break; moving mount = break; moving in ship = no break; saying 'ikhtartu nafsi' with intention of three = only one divorce (ba'in sughra).
These rulings are mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ووقف دابة هي راكبتها لا يقطع، وفلكها كبيتها، وسير دابتها كسيرها، وفي اختاري لا تصح نية الثلاث
[١] قوله: ووقف دابة؛ أي سكونها وامتناعها عن السير أو إسكانها ومنعها عن السير.
[٢] قوله: لا يقطع؛ فإنّ هذه الأمور ليس فيها تبدل حقيقة ولا حكماً؛ لعدم دلالتها على الإعراض، فلو كانت قائمة فجلست فهي على خيارها؛ لأنّه دليل الإقبال؛ فإنّ القعود أجمع للرأي، وكذا إذا كانت قاعدة فاتكأت بالوسادة، أو متكئة فقعدت وهجرت الوسادة؛ لأنه انتقال من جلسة إلى جلسة، فلا يدل على الإعراض. وكذا طلب الأب للشورة أو الشهود للإشهاد ليس دليلاً على الإعراض، بل هو لتحري الصواب والتحرز عن الإنكار، وكذا سكون مركوبها، بخلاف سيرها، فإنّ سير الدابة ووقوفها مضاف إليها، فوقوفها في حكم وقوفها، فلا يكون دليلا على الإعراض، وسيرها كمشيها، هو دليل على الإعراض.
[٣] قوله: وفلكها؛ بضم الفاء -: هي السفينة الجارية في البحر أو الساكنة على الشط، وكونه في حكم البيتِ أنه لا يبطل الخيار بانتقالها من جانب إلى جانب منه، ولا تعتبر حركة السفينة؛ لأنه ليس باختيار الراكب، فلا تضاف إليه، بخلاف الدابة، فإنّه يقدر على إيقافه وتسييره. وفي حكم الفلك المركب الدخاني الذي يسير بالمسافرين على الأرض بقوة الدخان، كما أنه في حكمه في حق جواز الصلاة فيه حالة سكونه وحركته، وفي وجوب استدارة المصلِّي إلى القبلة لو اختلفت الجهة بسيرها.
[٤] قوله: لا تصح نية الثلاث؛ علله صاحب الهداية بأنّ الاختيار لا يتنوّع. وأورد عليه: بأنه أيضاً منقسم إلى الأعلى والأدنى، كالبينونة في: أنت بائن. وجوابه على ما أشار إليه في «النهر» وغيره: أن اختيارها إنّما يفيد الخلوص والصفاء، فمعنى قولها: اخترتُ نفسي اصطفيتها من ملك الزوج، وذلك إنما يكون بالبينونة، فصارت البينونة هاهنا مقتضى قدر تصحيحاً للكلام؛ إذ اصطفاؤها نفسها لا يمكن بدونها. ومن المعلوم أن المقتضى لا عموم له، فيقدر بقدر الضرورة، وهو البينونة الصغرى، إذ بها تستخلص نفسها وتصطفيها من ملك الزوج، فلا تصح نية الكبرى؛ لعدم احتمال اللفظ، بخلاف أنت بائن ونحوه، حيث صرح بما يدل على البينونة، فاحتمل العموم والخصوص كليهما.
And the stopping of the mount she is riding does not break (the option); and her ship is like her house; and the moving of her mount is like her walking; and in 'ikhtari' (choose) the intention of three (divorces) is not valid. [1] His saying: 'and the stopping of the mount': i.e., its stillness and cessation from moving, or making it stop and preventing it from moving. [2] His saying: 'does not break': because these matters involve no actual or constructive change, due to their not indicating aversion. So if she was standing and then sat down, she remains on her option, because it indicates turning towards; for sitting is more conducive to reflection. Likewise if she was sitting and then reclined on a cushion, or was reclining and then sat down and left the cushion, because it is a transition from one sitting posture to another, so it does not indicate aversion. Similarly, the father seeking consultation or witnesses for attestation is not evidence of aversion, but rather for seeking correctness and guarding against denial. Likewise the stillness of her mount, unlike its moving, for the movement and stopping of the mount are attributed to it; so its stopping is like her stopping, and does not indicate aversion, and its moving is like her walking, and is evidence of aversion. [3] His saying: 'and her ship' (with dammah on the fa): it is the ship sailing in the sea or stationary on the shore. It being like a house is that the option is not invalidated by her moving from one side to another in it, and the movement of the ship is not considered, because it is not by the choice of the rider, so it is not attributed to him, unlike the mount, for he is able to stop and move it. In the ruling of the ship is the smoky vessel that travels with travellers on land by the power of smoke, just as it is in its ruling regarding the permissibility of prayer in it while stationary or moving, and the obligation of the worshipper turning towards the qiblah if the direction changes by its movement. [4] His saying: 'the intention of three is not valid'; the author of Al-Hidayah justified it by saying that 'ikhtari' (choose) does not admit of variation. An objection is raised against it: that it is also divisible into the higher and lower, like bainunah (separation) in 'you are baa'in'. Its answer, as indicated in 'Al-Nahr' and others, is that her choice (ikhtiyar) only signifies purity and clarity; so the meaning of her saying 'I choose myself' is that she selects herself from the husband's ownership, and that only occurs through bainunah; thus bainunah is here a necessary implication (muqtada) in order to validate the speech, since her selecting herself is impossible without it. It is known that a necessary implication has no generality; so it is estimated to the extent of necessity, and that is the minor bainunah (bainunah sughra), because by it she extracts and selects herself from the husband's ownership. So the intention of the major (bainunah kubra) is not valid due to the wording not bearing it, unlike 'you are baa'in' and the like, where the wording plainly indicates bainunah, so it bears both generality and specificity.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation (sharh) from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the topic of delegation in divorce. It addresses the period of choice (khiyar) given to the wife. The matn states four points: (1) Stopping the riding animal does not break the option, (2) The ship is like the house, (3) Moving the animal is like walking, (4) In 'ikhtari' the intention of three talags is invalid. The sharh explains that actions like stopping the mount, sitting, reclining, etc., do not indicate turning away (i'rad) from the option; they indicate continued attention. On the contrary, moving the mount indicates leaving, thus breaking the option. A ship is treated like a house because its movement is not attributed to the rider; hence changing position within it does not break the option. The issue of intending three talags in 'ikhtari' is addressed by saying that the word 'choice' (ikhtiyar) does not admit of degrees; it only implies one separation. When the wife says 'I choose myself', the implicit meaning is that she extracts herself from the husband's ownership, which necessarily requires at least a minor separation (ba'in sughra). Therefore, intending the major separation (ba'in kubra through three talags) is not valid because the wording does not bear it, unlike the explicit word 'ba'in' which can be interpreted for one or three.

Key Terms
الخيار (al-khiyar): The option or choice given to the wife in a deferred divorce (tafweedh).
الإعراض (al-i'rad): Turning away or showing disinterest, which invalidates the option if evidenced by action.
البينونة الصغرى (al-bainunah al-sughra): Minor separation that occurs after one or two talags, allowing remarriage without a new marriage contract.
المقتضى (al-muqtada): A necessary implication required to make speech valid; in this context, the implication of bainunah when saying 'I choose myself'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a wife given the choice (ikhtari) stands up and walks away, what is the ruling on her option?
A) The option continues because movement is not considered.
B) The option is void because walking indicates aversion.
C) The option is void only if she leaves the house.
D) The option continues only if she sits down again.

Key Takeaway
The wife's option in delegated divorce is invalidated only by actions that clearly indicate aversion; the word 'ikhtari' can only effect a minor separation.

Talaq — Divorce

Wife's Choice (Ikhtiyar) in Tafweed: Conditions for Divorce
Ruling on the wife's pronouncement upon being given the choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions for divorce when a wife is given choice in tafweed (mention of 'nafs' and validity of present tense) and explain the Hanafi rationale for accepting the present tense.

Core Idea
In tafweed, the wife must specifically choose herself (with 'nafs') and the present tense suffices because choice is an internal state.

When a husband says 'ikhtari' (choose) to his wife, her response must be precise. This passage from the sharh clarifies two key points: she must explicitly mention her 'self' (nafs) in her reply, and using the present tense (akhtaru) is valid because it reports an ongoing internal state, not a mere promise. Understanding these conditions ensures correct application of talaq rulings in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the wife's response to the husband's delegation of choice (tafweed). The ruling is that one baa'in divorce occurs if her response meets the conditions.
Baa'in divorce means the marriage is immediately terminated and cannot be revoked without a new marriage.
2
Locate the condition of mentioning 'nafs'
The passage states that the word 'nafs' (self) must be present in the wife's statement or what stands in its place. Without it, no divorce occurs.
Example: If she says merely 'ikhtartu' without 'nafsi', the divorce does not take effect.
3
Understand the problem with the present tense
Normally, a present tense statement is considered a promise (wa'd) and does not effect talaq. However, here the present tense is accepted because choice (ikhtiyar) is an internal state of the heart that can be reported as ongoing.
In contrast, if the husband says 'divorce yourself' and she says 'I divorce myself' in present tense, it is invalid without custom or intention of insha.
4
Follow the qiyas vs. exception argument
Analogy (qiyas) would reject the present tense because it is a promise, but the ruling departs from analogy due to the nature of ikhtiyar. The present tense is literally used for the present state, and since the choice is continuous, the report is truthful.
This is a case where a hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) or istihsan overrides the apparent analogy.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that both past and present tense are valid provided 'nafs' is mentioned. Some scholars might not require 'nafs' if the context is clear, but the Hanafi school insists on its mention.
Authority: Al-Fath (Fath al-Qadir) is cited as support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل تبين إن قالت: اخترتُ نفسي، أو أختارُ نفسي، وشُرِطَ ذِكْرُ النَّفْسِ من أحدهما.
[١] قوله: بل تبين؛ أي تصيرُ المخيرة بائنة بواحدة بائنة إن قالت في جواب التخيير: اخترتُ نفسي بصيغة الماضي، أو أختار نفسي بصيغة المضارع، سواء ذكرت أنا أو لا، والقياس يقتضي أن لا يعتبر قولها بصيغة المضارع؛ لأنه وعد، ولهذا لو قالت: أنا أطلق نفسي في جواب: طلّقي نفسك لا يقع الطلاق به ما لم يتعارف أو تنو الإنشاء. وإنما اعتبر المضارع هاهنا؛ لأنّ المضارع حقيقة في الحال، مجاز في الاستقبال على أحد المذاهب، وقيل: بالعكس، وقيل: وهو مشترك، وعلى كل تقدير، يرجح إرادة الحال بقرينة كونه إخباراً عن أمر قائم في الحال، وهو ممكن في الاختيار؛ لأنَّ محله القلب، فيصح الإخبار باللسان عما هو قائم بمحل آخر حال الإخبار. كذا في «الفتح».
[٢] قوله: ذكر النفس؛ أي يشترط لوقوع الطلاق هاهنا ذكر لفظ: «النفس» أو ما يقوم مقامه من أحد الزوجين، فلو قال: اختاري فقالت اخترت، لا يقع به شيء؛
Rather, she becomes baa'in if she says: 'I have chosen myself' (past) or 'I choose myself' (present), and the mention of 'self' (nafs) is required from either of them. [1] His saying: 'rather, she becomes baa'in': i.e., the woman given choice becomes baa'in with one baa'in divorce if she says in response to the delegation: 'I have chosen myself' in the past tense, or 'I choose myself' in the present tense, whether she mentions 'ana' (I) or not. Analogy (qiyas) would require that her statement in the present tense not be considered, because it is a promise; for this reason, if she says 'I divorce myself' in response to 'divorce yourself', the divorce does not occur unless it is customary or she intends creation (insha'). However, the present tense is considered here because the present tense is literal for the present state and metaphorical for the future according to one school; it is said the opposite, and it is said it is shared. In any case, the intention of the present is preferred by the context that it is an report about something existing in the present, and that is possible in choice (ikhtiyar) because its place is the heart, so it is valid to inform verbally about what is present in another place at the time of reporting. This is from 'Al-Fath'. [2] His saying: 'mention of nafs': i.e., it is necessary for the occurrence of divorce here to mention the word 'nafs' (self) or what stands in its place from either of the spouses. So if he said 'choose' and she said 'I choose', nothing occurs.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling for the wife's response when given the choice (ikhtari). The author clarifies that the wife becomes irrevocably divorced (baa'in) only if she uses the word 'nafs' (self) in her response, either in the past tense 'ikhtartu nafsi' or the present tense 'akhtaru nafsi'. The present tense is considered effective here despite the general principle that present tense statements are promises (wa'd) not effective for divorce. The reason is that the present tense can be used for an ongoing state, and the choice is an internal state of the heart which she reports. Therefore, the divorce takes effect immediately. The mention of 'nafs' is a condition because the mere word 'ikhtartu' (I chose) does not specify what she chose; it needs to be connected to herself to indicate she is taking herself out of the marriage.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs): The word 'self' that must be mentioned in the wife's statement for the divorce to occur.
ماضي (madi): Past tense form of the verb, which is valid for divorce (e.g., 'ikhtartu').
مضارع (mudari'): Present/future tense form; considered here for immediate effect because it reports an ongoing state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'ikhtari' (choose). She replies 'akhtaru nafsi' (I choose myself). According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) No divorce occurs because the present tense is only a promise.
B) One baa'in divorce occurs because the present tense is valid here.
C) Three talags occur because she said 'nafs'.
D) The divorce is suspended until she repeats it in the past tense.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband gives his wife the choice (tafweed), her divorce is effective only if she explicitly mentions her 'self' (nafs) and the present tense is valid because it reports an ongoing internal state of choice.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

Condition of Mentioning 'Self' in Wife's Choice
Ruling on wife's response when given option to choose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a wife's choice (ikhtiyar) to effect a divorce and explain why the mention of 'self' is required and why only a minor irrevocable divorce results.

Core Idea
A wife's choice of herself implies selection from possession, necessitating only a minor divorce, not a major one.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses the scenario where a husband delegates the choice of divorce to his wife. The key question is: what must the wife say for a divorce to occur, and what degree of divorce results? The Hanafi school derives its ruling from the linguistic implications of 'ikhtiyar' and the principle of muqtada (implied element). Let's explore the conditions and rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The husband says 'I give you the option' (takhayyur). The wife responds. The ruling: if she says 'I chose myself' or 'I choose myself', she becomes irrevocably divorced (ba'in) by one divorce.
2
Recognize the condition of mentioning 'self'
The text explicitly states that the word 'self' (nafs) must be mentioned by either spouse. Without it, no divorce occurs. This ensures the choice is directed to her own person.
3
Understand the validity of the present tense
Although the present tense normally indicates a promise, here it is accepted because it can inform of an existing state in the heart. The context makes the present meaning preferred.
The commentary cites 'Al-Fath' for this justification.
4
Analyze the implied element (muqtada) rationale
The wife's statement 'I chose myself' implies she selects herself from her husband's possession. Since that requires divorce, divorce is implied as necessary. But because it is implied, it is restricted to the minimum needed: the minor irrevocable divorce.
5
Distinguish from explicit divorce wording
If the husband says 'you are irrevocably divorced', the wording can bear both minor and major meanings. But here, since the divorce is only implied, it cannot be extended to the major.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل تبين إن قالت : اخترتُ نفسي، أو أخْتارُ نفسي، وشُرِطَ ذِكْرُ النَّفْسِ من أحدهما
[٢] بل تبين إن قالت : اخترتُ نفسي ، أو أختارُ نفسي ، وشُرِطَ ذِكْرُ النَّفْسِ من أحدهما .
وأورد عليه : بأنه أيضاً منقسم إلى الأعلى والأدنى، كالبينونة في : أنت بائن. وجوابه على ما أشار إليه في «النهر» وغيره : أن اختيارها إنّما يفيد الخلوص والصفاء، فمعنى قولها : اخترتُ نفسي اصطفيتها من ملك الزوج، وذلك إنما يكون بالبينونة، فصارت البينونة هاهنا مقتضى قدر تصحيحاً للكلام ؛ إذ اصطفاؤها نفسها لا يمكن بدونها. ومن المعلوم أن المقتضى لا عموم له، فيقدر بقدر الضرورة، وهو البينونة الصغرى، إذ بها تستخلص نفسها وتصطفيها من ملك الزوج، فلا تصح نية الكبرى ؛ لعدم احتمال اللفظ ، بخلاف أنت بائن ونحوه، حيث صرح بما يدل على البينونة ، فاحتمل العموم والخصوص كليهما. [١] قوله : بل تبين ؛ أي تصيرُ المخيرة بائنة بواحدة بائنة إن قالت في جواب التخيير: اخترتُ نفسي بصيغة الماضي، أو أختار نفسي بصيغة المضارع، سواء ذكرت أنا أو لا، والقياس يقتضي أن لا يعتبر قولها بصيغة المضارع ؛ لأنه وعد، ولهذا لو قالت : أنا أطلق نفسي في جواب : طلّقي نفسك لا يقع الطلاق به ما لم يتعارف ، أو تنو الإنشاء.
وإنما اعتبر المضارع هاهنا ؛ لأنّ المضارع حقيقة في الحال، مجاز في الاستقبال على أحد المذاهب ، وقيل : بالعكس ، وقيل : وهو مشترك، وعلى كل تقدير، يرجح إرادة الحال بقرينة كونه إخباراً عن أمر قائم في الحال، وهو ممكن في الاختيار ؛ لأنَّ محله القلب، فيصح الإخبار باللسان عما هو قائم بمحل آخر حال الإخبار. كذا في «الفتح». [٢] قوله : ذكر النفس ؛ أي يشترط لوقوع الطلاق هاهنا ذكر لفظ : «النفس» أو ما يقوم مقامه من أحد الزوجين، فلو قال: اختاري فقالت اخترت، لا يقع به شيء ؛
(١) متصلاً أو منفصلاً في المجلس. ينظر : «فتح باب العناية»(٢ : ١١٤).
(٢) «النهر الفائق» (٤ : ٨٠).
(٣) فتح القدير» (٤ : ٨٢).
Rather, she becomes irrevocably divorced once if she says: 'I chose myself' or 'I choose myself,' and it is conditioned that the word 'self' be mentioned by one of them. [2] (The phrase) 'Rather, she becomes irrevocably divorced once if she says: I chose myself, or I choose myself, and it is conditioned that the word self be mentioned by one of them.' (The commentator) objects to this: that it is also divisible into the greater and lesser, like irrevocability in 'you are irrevocably divorced.' The answer, as indicated in 'Al-Nahr' and elsewhere, is that her choice only benefits purification and clarity, so the meaning of her saying 'I chose myself' is 'I selected myself from the husband's possession,' and that can only be through irrevocable divorce. Thus, irrevocability here becomes an implied element to correct the speech, since her self-selection cannot be without it. And it is known that an implied element has no generality, so it is restricted to the extent of necessity, which is the minor irrevocable divorce, because by it she extracts herself and selects herself from the husband's possession. Therefore, the intention of the greater irrevocable divorce is not valid because the word does not accommodate it, unlike 'you are irrevocably divorced' and the like, where one explicitly states what indicates irrevocability, so it accommodates both generality and particularity. [1] The commentator says: 'Rather, she becomes irrevocably divorced' i.e., the woman given the option becomes irrevocably divorced by one irrevocable divorce if she says in response to the delegation: 'I chose myself' in the past tense, or 'I choose myself' in the present tense, whether she mentions 'I' or not. Analogy would dictate that her statement in the present tense not be considered because it is a promise; therefore, if she says 'I will divorce myself' in response to 'Divorce yourself,' divorce does not occur by it unless customary or she intends creation. The present tense is only considered here because the present tense is literal for the present, metaphorical for the future according to one school, and it is said the opposite, and it is said it is polysemous. In any case, the intended meaning of the present is preferred by the context of it being an information about a matter existing in the present, and that is possible in choice because its place is the heart, so it is valid to inform with the tongue about what is existing in another place at the time of informing. As stated in 'Al-Fath'. [2] The commentator says: 'Mentioning the self' i.e., for the occurrence of divorce here it is conditioned that the word 'self' or its equivalent be mentioned by one of the spouses. So if he says 'Choose' and she says 'I chose,' nothing occurs. (1) Connected or separate in the session. See 'Fath Bab al-'Inaya' 2:114. (2) 'Al-Nahr al-Fa'iq' 4:80. (3) 'Fath al-Qadeer' 4:82.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling when a husband delegates the choice of divorce to his wife by saying 'I give you the option.' The matn states that if the wife says 'I chose myself' (in past tense) or 'I choose myself' (in present tense), she becomes irrevocably divorced by one divorce, provided the word 'self' is mentioned. The commentary addresses an objection: since 'I choose myself' implies separation, it could be interpreted as either a minor or major irrevocable divorce. The answer clarifies that the implied element here is only the minor irrevocable divorce, because the wife's selection of herself from possession only requires the minor divorce; she cannot intend the major because the wording does not accommodate it, unlike explicitly saying 'you are irrevocably divorced.' The commentary then justifies using the present tense despite its usual classification as a promise: in this context, the present tense is preferred as literal for the present because the wife is informing of an existing choice in her heart, and the context supports the immediate meaning. Finally, it emphasizes that mentioning 'self' is essential; without it, no divorce occurs even if she says 'I chose.'

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyar): The act of choosing; here it refers to the wife's selection of herself (divorce) or her husband (remaining married).
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; here it refers to the minor irrevocable divorce (bainuna sughra) which ends the marriage but allows remarriage.
النفس (al-nafs): The word 'self' (nafs) mentioned in the response to indicate the wife's own self as the object of choice.
مقتضى (muqtada): An implied element necessary for the correct meaning of a statement; here the divorce is implied as the means of selection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum requirement for the wife's statement to effect a divorce when she is given the option?
A) She must say 'I chose' (اخترت).
B) She must say 'I chose myself' (اخترت نفسي) or 'I choose myself' (أختار نفسي).
C) She must say 'I am divorced'.
D) She must say 'I choose my husband'.

Key Takeaway
When a wife is given the option, her statement must include the word 'self' to effect a minor irrevocable divorce; any other wording, such as 'I chose' alone, does not count.

Ruling on 'Ikhtari Ikhtiyarah' and Repeated Options
Ruling on specific responses leading to triple divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on 'ikhtari ikhtiyarah' and repeated 'ikhtari', and apply the Hanafi principles to determine the number of divorces, including the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When the husband says 'ikhtari ikhtiyarah', one ba'in divorce occurs without mentioning 'self'.

This lesson examines two special scenarios in Hanafi talaq where the husband uses the phrase 'ikhtari ikhtiyarah' (choose one choice) or repeats 'ikhtari' three times. The ruling on these wordings is exceptional, based on the practice of the Companions (athar as-sahabah), and is restricted to the exact wording. We will explore how grammatical forms and contextual indications determine the outcome, including a notable disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
First scenario: husband says 'ikhtari ikhtiyarah' (choose one choice) and wife responds 'ikhtartu' (I chose). Second scenario: husband repeats 'ikhtari' three times, wife says 'ikhtartu ikhtiyarah' or specifies 'first, middle, last'.
Note the difference in wording and the number of divorces.
2
Determine the ruling for each scenario
For scenario one: one ba'in divorce. For scenario two: three divorces, regardless of whether the wife mentions 'self' or the husband's intention is explicit.
The commentary explains why the 'ta' in 'ikhtiyarah' indicates singularity.
3
Understand the evidence (dalil)
The rulings are based on athar as-sahabah (traditions of the Companions) and are against analogy (qiyas). Therefore, they are strictly limited to the reported wording.
This is a key point: the ruling is exceptional and not extensible.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab dispute
In the second scenario, if the wife simply says 'ikhtartu' (I chose) without specifying, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule one divorce, while Abu Hanifa's view (from context) leads to three. The dispute hinges on whether her general response matches the husband's repeated delegation.
The text states 'فعندهما تقع واحدة' showing the Sahibayn's position.
5
Apply the role of contextual indications (qara'in)
Repetition of 'ikhtari' is a strong contextual indicator that the husband intends multiple divorces, so no explicit intention (niyyah) is required. Without such qarinah, intention would be necessary.
This is a standard Hanafi principle: qarinah can substitute for explicit intent.

Therefore, here there is no need to mention 'self' nor the intention of the husband. So if she says in response: 'I chose one choice,' three divorces occur, and also if she says: 'I chose' only, and in the second there is a difference of opinion: according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) one divorce occurs, because even though he delegated three, she only chose one. Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage covers two special cases where the husband uses the phrase 'اختاري اختيارة' (choose one choice) or repeats 'اختاري' three times. The matn rules: if the husband says 'Choose one choice' and the wife responds 'I chose,' she becomes irrevocably divorced by one divorce, even without mentioning 'self.' If he repeats 'choose' three times and she says 'I chose one choice,' or specifies 'first, middle, last,' three divorces occur without need for intention. The commentary explains that the 'ta' in 'ikhtiyarah' indicates a single action, and since choosing herself can be singular or multiple, the husband's wording clarifies his intent. When he repeats 'choose' three times, it shows he intends multiple divorces, so her corresponding response yields three. However, if she simply says 'I chose' in the second repetition, there is a difference: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), only one divorce occurs. Finally, the commentary addresses why no intention is required: contextual clues (like repetition) replace explicit intention.

Key Terms
اختيارة (ikhtiyarah): A single act of choice; the form indicates one time (ta' marboutah for unity).
تكرير (takrir): Repetition; here the husband repeating 'اختاري' three times indicates his desire for multiple divorces.
آثار الصحابة (athar al-sahabah): Traditions or practices of the Companions; used as legal evidence for this exceptional ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Three divorces.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rulings on 'ikhtari ikhtiyarah' and repeated 'ikhtari' are exceptional, based on Companion practice, and illustrate how grammatical forms and contextual indications determine the number of divorces, with a key intra-madhhab dispute resolved by caution.

The Ruling on the Wife's Divorce in Tafwid: Bain or Raj'i?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on tafwid choice phrase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct Hanafi ruling when a wife says 'I divorce myself' in tafwid and explain why it is bain according to the most authoritative view.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, 'choice' language overrides explicit divorce words, producing an irrevocable divorce.

The Hanafi school recognizes a husband's delegation of divorce (tafwid) to his wife. When she exercises that delegation using phrases like 'I divorce myself' or 'I choose myself with a divorce', a dispute arises: does she get a revocable or irrevocable divorce? This lesson analyzes the intra-Hanafi difference and identifies the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The passage states the most correct view (al-asah) that it is one bain (irrevocable) divorce, but notes that Al-Hidayah and some copies of Al-Jami' al-Saghir report it as raj'i (revocable).
Note the authority weight: the bain view comes from corrected copies of Al-Jami' al-Saghir.
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa argues that three potential divorces are combined in her possession without order, like a group in a place. Priority, middle, and last are invalid, leaving absolute choice, so it becomes as if she said 'I have chosen' (which does not allow return).
The analogy of a group in a place: no order, so no revocability can be attached.
3
Analyze the opposing argument and response
The opposing view claims that because the wife used explicit divorce words (صريح), normally producing raj'i, it should be raj'i. The Hanafi response: her utterance is a response to delegation with 'choice', and choice does not allow return; explicit wording is subordinated to context.
This argument is supported by Al-Jami' al-Kabir and Al-Sadr al-Shahid.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is bain (irrevocable). The view in Al-Hidayah is deemed a scribal error. The correct text of Al-Jami' al-Saghir states: 'she is one, he does not own the right of return'.
Later Hanafi fatwa authorities (e.g., Ibn Abidin) confirm the bain view.

And everything that has no order, speech that is for order is irrelevant. As in Al-Inayah. [2] His saying: 'I divorced'; i.e., I effected divorce upon myself and chose separation. [3] His saying: 'she becomes irrevocably divorced'; i.e., one irrevocable divorce occurs to her. As for it being one, it is apparent. As for it being irrevocable despite her mentioning the word 'divorce' explicitly or implicitly, and it is from the explicit words that produce revocable divorce, it is because her saying that occurred as a response to something that has no indication of return, rather it indicates what does not allow return. And this is consistent with what is in some corrected copies of Al-Jami' al-Saghir of Imam Muhammad—peace be upon him—and his wording: 'If she says, "I have divorced myself" or "I have chosen myself with a divorce," it is one, he does not own the right of return.' End. Al-Sadr al-Shahid said in his commentary: 'Because what exists from the husband's side is not explicit divorce, and she only owns according to what the husband owns, and the husband owns it by the word of choice, and it does not allow return even if she comes with explicit divorce.' As mentioned in Al-Jami' al-Kabir, and it is mentioned in some copies of this book that one revocable divorce occurs, and this is a scribal error. [4] His saying: 'he owns the right of return'; and he explained it with the gist: his saying 'I divorced myself' or 'I chose myself with a divorce' necessitates irrevocability after the end of the waiting period because it is from the explicit words, and what necessitates irrevocability after the end of the waiting period was revocable at the time of occurrence. And objection was raised against it: that what is delegated to her is the immediate choice, not that which is added to after the waiting period. And even if granted, then the waiting period should be obligatory upon her after it requires it, but it is not so. As in Al-Hawashi al-Hamidiyyah. [1] His saying: 'explicit'; i.e., the mentioned divorce explicitly, or implied in 'I divorced.' [2] His saying: 'most correct'; rather it is the correct opinion, nothing besides it except error.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of a wife saying 'I divorce myself' or 'I choose myself with one divorce' in a context where the husband has delegated the choice of divorce to her (tafwid). The correct and relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, according to Abu Hanifa and the verified copies of Al-Jami' al-Saghir, is that this constitutes one irrevocable divorce (bain). The reasoning is that the wife's authority derives from the husband's delegation, and since the husband's word 'choice' (ikhtiyar) does not allow for revocability (raj'ah), even if she uses explicit divorce wording, the result is a bain divorce. Some texts, including Al-Hidayah and certain copies of Al-Jami' al-Saghir, state that it is a revocable divorce, but the commentators in this work firmly assert that this is a scribal error and that the correct view is irrevocable. There is also a reported two narrations: one revocable and one irrevocable, with the irrevocable being the most correct (asah). The passage includes an objection to the reasoning that the divorce becomes revocable if it necessitates irrevocability after the waiting period, but the objection is answered by noting that the delegation is for immediate choice, not deferred. Thus, the mufta bihi (operative fatwa) is that one bain divorce occurs.

Key Terms
طلقت نفسي (tallaqtu nafsi): A phrase used by the wife meaning 'I have divorced myself' in the context of delegated divorce (tafwid).
اخترت نفسي بتطليقة (akhtartu nafsi bitatliqah): A phrase meaning 'I have chosen myself with a divorce' – the wife chooses to divorce herself.
بائنة (bāʾinah): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take the wife back during the waiting period without a new marriage.
رجعية (rajʿiyyah): A revocable divorce where the husband can take the wife back without a new marriage during the waiting period.
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of the power of divorce by the husband to the wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One revocable divorce (رجعی)
B) One irrevocable divorce (بائن)
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce (tafwid), the wife's pronouncement using choice language results in one irrevocable (bain) divorce, regardless of explicit divorce wording.

Dispute on Revocability of Delegated Divorce
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delegated divorce type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi dispute on delegated divorce and state the preferred position that it yields an irrevocable (bāʾin) divorce.

Core Idea
Delegated divorce is irrevocable despite clear wording because the wife's agency overrides the default revocability.

This passage addresses a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when a husband delegates authority to his wife to divorce herself using a clear expression, does the divorce become revocable or irrevocable? The text presents two narrations and ultimately settles on the irrevocable view, supported by a logical objection against the revocable view. Understanding this dispute clarifies the unique nature of delegated divorce and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
The core issue: when a husband says to his wife 'your affair is in your hand for one divorce' and she chooses herself, what type of divorce occurs? Two positions exist: one revocable (rajʿī) or one irrevocable (bāʾin).
The dispute is only about the nature of the divorce, not its validity.
2
Examine the two narrations
The text mentions two narrations: (1) it is rajʿī because the wording is clear (ṣarīḥ), and clear divorce is presumptively revocable; (2) it is bāʾin, and this is more correct (aṣaḥḥ). The gloss even calls the revocable view an error.
The revocable view is attributed to some early Hanafi texts.
3
Analyze the objection against revocability
The revocable view is contradicted by the fact that delegation is for immediate choice. If it were revocable, the wife would have to observe 'iddah immediately after effecting the divorce, but she does not. This shows the divorce is immediately effective as bāʾin, not contingent.
This objection is from al-Hawāshī al-Ḥamīdiyyah.
4
Identify the preferred position
The text and gloss affirm that the irrevocable (bāʾin) view is correct, even stating that no other view is valid. This is the mufta bihi position in later Hanafi scholarship.
Note the strong language: 'بل هو الصواب الذي ليس ما سواه إلا غلطاً' (rather it is the correct, besides which nothing but error).
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
If revocable: husband can take her back during 'iddah without new marriage. If irrevocable: she becomes immediately free, must complete 'iddah, and then may marry others. No reconciliation without a new marriage.
This difference directly affects the parties' rights and options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التفويض والأمر باليد والمشيئة
وقيل : هذا غلط وقعَ من الكاتب، والصواب أنَّه لا يملك الرَّجعة.
وقيل : فيه روايتان :
أحداهما : أنه يقع واحدة رجعية ؛ لأن لفظهما صريح. والأخرى : أنها بائنة ، وهذا أصح.
نفسي بتطليقة يوجب البينونة بعد انقضاء العدّة ؛ لكونه من ألفاظ ا ظ الصريح، وما يوجب البينونة بعد انقضاء العدة كان عند الوقوع رجعياً.
واعترض عليه : بأنّ المفوض إليها الاختيار الناجرُ لا المضاف إلى ما بعد العدة، ولو سلّم فينبغي أن يجب عليها العدة بعد اقتضائها وليس كذلك. كذا في «الحواشي الحميدية».
[ 1 ] قوله : صريح ؛ وهو التطليقة المذكورة صراحة، أو في ضمن طلقت. [٢] قوله : أصح ؛ بل هو الصواب الذي ليس ما سواه إلا غلطاً.
حجامي
Chapter of Delegation, Command by Hand, and Will. It is said: This is a mistake by the copyist; the correct is that he does not have the right of return. It is said: There are two narrations: One: it falls as one revocable because its wording is clear. The other: it is irrevocable, and this is more correct. [The phrase] 'myself with a divorce' necessitates irrevocability after the completion of the waiting period because it is from the clear expressions, and that which necessitates irrevocability after the completion of the waiting period was at the time of occurrence revocable. An objection was raised: that the delegation to her is for the immediate choice, not that which is attached to after the waiting period, and if it is granted, then it would be necessary that she observe the waiting period after effecting it, but that is not the case. Thus in al-Hawashi al-Hamidiyyah. [1] His statement: 'clear' means the mentioned divorce explicitly or implicitly. [2] His statement: 'more correct' rather it is the correct view, beside which nothing but error exists. HJami.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the nature of a divorce effected when a husband delegates authority to his wife using a clear expression like 'your affair is in your hand for one divorce.' The text presents divergent views. First, a claim that the received text is a scribal error and the correct ruling is that no right of return (raj'a) exists, i.e., the divorce is irrevocable. Then it mentions two narrations: one that it is a single revocable divorce (because the expression is clear and thus entails raj'a), and another that it is irrevocable, with the latter being deemed more correct. An objection is raised against the revocable view: the delegation is for immediate choice, not for a divorce contingent on completion of 'iddah. If it were revocable, the 'iddah would start immediately, but that is not the case in the delegated scenario. The gloss then clarifies that the clear expression is explicit about the divorce, and that the 'more correct' is actually the only correct stance. This dispute reflects a tension in Hanafi fiqh between the default revocability of clear divorce expressions and the peculiar nature of delegation of divorce.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation by the husband to the wife of the authority to divorce herself.
رجعية (rajʿiyyah): A revocable divorce where the husband has the right to take back his wife during her waiting period without a new marriage contract.
بائنة (bāʾinah): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately; husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One revocable (rajʿī) divorce
B) One irrevocable (bāʾin) divorce
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
Delegated divorce (tafwīḍ) yields an irrevocable divorce, contrary to the default revocability of clear divorce expressions, due to the wife's independent agency and the immediacy of her choice.

The Linguistic Scope of 'Amr bi-Yadik' and Its Effect on Divorce Count
Rulings on the phrases 'amr bi-yadik' and 'ikhtarī' for divorce delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for the phrase 'amr bi-yadik' in divorce, distinguish between 'amr' and 'ikhtarī', and apply the rule that intention of three is valid only with 'amr'.

Core Idea
When the husband delegates divorce by saying 'your affair is in your hand', the word 'amr' is general, allowing the intention of three divorces.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh work examines two related scenarios of divorce delegation. It clarifies that the phrase 'amr bi-yadik' ('your affair is in your hand') has a broad linguistic scope, making it a kināyah that can encompass multiple divorces if intended. Conversely, 'ikhtarī' ('choose') lacks this generality. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the husband's intention and the wife's response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (1) husband says 'amr bi-yadik fī taṭlīqa' or 'ikhtarī bi-taṭlīqa' and wife chooses herself, (2) husband says 'amr bi-yadik' intending three and wife says 'I chose myself with one'.
Note the difference in the husband's words and intention.
2
Analyse the first scenario
When the husband says 'amr bi-yadik fī taṭlīqa' (or 'ikhtarī bi-taṭlīqa'), he delegates a single divorce. The wife's choice results in one revocable divorce because she is limited to what was delegated.
Revocable means the husband can take her back during the waiting period.
3
Analyse the second scenario
When the husband says 'amr bi-yadik' without specifying a number but intends three, and the wife responds 'I chose myself with one', three divorces occur because the word 'amr' is general and can cover multiple divorces.
The wife's 'one' is interpreted as describing her choice, not limiting the count.
4
Distinguish between 'amr' and 'ikhtarī'
The passage explains that 'amr' is a general noun (like in Quran 82:19), so it can be a kināyah for any number of divorces. 'Ikhtarī' is not general and only allows the intention of a single divorce.
This is a key linguistic distinction affecting legal outcomes.
5
Apply the ruling
Given a new scenario (e.g., husband says 'amr bi-yadik' intending two, wife says 'I choose myself twice'), determine the result using the principles learned.
The general rule: 'amr' admits generality; intention specifies the number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الأمر باليد
(ولو قال : أمرك بيدك في تطليقة، أو اختاري بتطليقة، فاختارت نفسها يقعُ واحدة رجعية (1).
ولو قال : أمرك بيدك ، ونوى الثلاث " ، فقالت : اخترتُ نفسي بواحدة ،
أو بمرة واحدة يقعن
[١]قوله : تقع واحدة رجعيّة ؛ لأنه فوض إليها الأمر بلفظ الطلاق، معقب للرجعة، فاختيارها بعده نفسها لا يقع به إلا واحد رجعي ؛ إذ المرأة لا تملك إيقاع ما لم يفوض إليها.
[۲]قوله : بيدك ؛ أو بيمينك أو بشمالك أو أنفك أو لسانك أو عينيك. كذا في «البحر»).
(۳) قوله : ونوى الثلاث ؛ إنما تصح نية الثلاث في الأمر باليد دون اختياري ؛ لأن الأمر اسم عام يتناول كل شيء ، قال الله : وَالْأَمْرُ يَوْمَذٍ لِلَّهِ (۳)، وإذا كان الأمرُ عاماً صالحاً لكل فعل، فإذا نوى به الطلاق صار كناية عن قوله : طلاقك بيدك، والطلاق مصدر يحتمل العموم والخصوص، فتكون نية الثلاث نية التعميم، بخلاف قوله : اختاري، فإنّه لا يحتمل العموم كما مر كذا في «العناية).
(٤) قوله : يقعن ؛ أي ثلاث تطليقات؛ لأن الاختيار يصلح جواباً للأمر باليد ؛ لكونه تمليكاً كالتخيير، والواحدة صفة للاختيار ، فصار كأنها قالت : اخترتُ نفسي بمرة
Section on Command by Hand. (If he said: 'Your affair is in your hand for a divorce' or 'Choose for a divorce', and she chose herself, one revocable divorce occurs. If he said: 'Your affair is in your hand' and intended three, and she said: 'I have chosen myself with one' or 'with one time', three occur. [1] His statement: 'one revocable occurs' because he delegated to her the matter with the word of divorce, which is followed by the right of return; so her choice of herself thereafter only results in one revocable, since the woman does not have the power to effect what was not delegated to her. [2] His statement: 'in your hand' i.e., or with your right hand, or left, or nose, or tongue, or eyes. Thus in 'al-Bahr'. [3] His statement: 'and intended three' — the intention of three only validates in 'amr bi yadik' and not in 'ikhtārī' because 'amr' is a general noun encompassing everything, Allah said: 'And the affair on that day is Allah's.' And when 'amr' is general, valid for every action, then if he intends with it divorce, it becomes a metaphor for his statement 'your divorce is in your hand', and divorce (taṭlīq) is a masdar that admits generality and specificity, so the intention of three is an intention of generality, unlike his statement 'ikhtārī' which does not admit generality as previously mentioned; thus in 'al-ʿInāya'. [4] His statement: 'they occur' i.e., three divorces, because the choice is a valid answer for 'amr bi yadik' as it is a possession like choice (takhayyur), and the 'one' is a description of the choice, so it is as if she said: 'I have chosen myself with one time'.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the 'Fasl fī al-Amr bi al-Yad' (Section on Command by Hand) of a Hanafi fiqh work. It provides two scenarios. First, if the husband says 'Your affair is in your hand for a divorce' or 'Choose (ikhtarī) for a divorce,' and the wife chooses herself, one revocable divorce occurs. The reason given is that the husband delegated the matter using the word of divorce (lafẓ al-ṭalāq), which is followed by the right of return (rajʿa). Thus the wife's choice can only bring about a revocable divorce because she is limited to what was delegated. Alternative wording for 'your hand' includes 'right hand', 'left', 'nose', etc., as noted from 'al-Bahr' (al-Bahr al-Rāʾiq). Second, if the husband says 'Your affair is in your hand' (without specifying a number) and intends three divorces, and the wife responds 'I have chosen myself with one' or 'with one time', three divorces occur. This is because the word 'amr' (affair) is general, as in the Qur'anic verse, so when he intends divorce, it becomes a kināyah (metaphorical expression) for 'your divorce is in your hand' (ṭalāquki bi-yadik). The word 'ṭalāq' is a masdar that can cover one or many, so his intention of three is a specification of generality. This does not work with 'ikhtarī' because it does not admit generality. Therefore, the wife's use of 'one' is interpreted as describing her choice, not limiting the divorce count, so all three intended divorces fall. This distinction shows how linguistic nuances affect legal outcomes in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
أمر بيدك (amr bi-yadik): A phrase used by the husband to delegate the power of divorce to his wife, meaning 'your affair is in your hand'.
كناية (kināyah): A metaphorical expression that implicitly conveys divorce; here 'amr bi-yadik' becomes a kināyah when the husband intends divorce.
تمليك (tamlīk): Ownership or possession; here the husband's phrase 'amr bi-yadik' is considered a tamlīk, giving the wife ownership of the divorce decision.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'Your affair is in your hand' intending three divorces, and his wife says 'I have chosen myself with one', how many divorces occur?
A) One revocable divorce
B) One irrevocable divorce
C) Three divorces
D) None, because her response does not match his intention

Key Takeaway
The word 'amr' in 'amr bi-yadik' is linguistically general, allowing the husband's intention to specify the number of divorces, unlike 'ikhtarī' which does not admit generality.

Delegating One Revocable Talaq via Amr bil Yad
Ruling on wife choosing herself when delegated one talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling when a husband delegates a limited number of talaq to his wife using phrases like 'amruki biyadiki', and state whether the resulting talaq is revocable or irrevocable.

Core Idea
When a husband delegates one talaq, the wife's choice results in exactly one revocable talaq.

The Hanafi school permits a husband to delegate the power of divorce to his wife. However, the scope of that delegation determines the outcome. In this passage, al-Wiqaya explains the effect when the husband specifies one talaq and the wife chooses herself—she cannot effect more than delegated, and the default talaq is revocable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation phrase and its limitation
Note that the husband says 'Your command is in your hand in one talaq' or 'Choose in one talaq'. The phrase 'in one talaq' explicitly limits the delegation to a single talaq.
Other wordings like 'biyadiki' are interpreted similarly; the key is the limitation.
2
Analyze the wife's response
The wife 'chose herself' (ikhtarat nafsaha). In this context, choosing herself is equivalent to effecting one talaq.
Her response must stay within the delegated scope.
3
Determine the legal effect
One talaq falls immediately. Since the husband did not specify irrevocability, and the default in Hanafi law is revocable (raj‘i), the talaq is revocable.
Revocability means the husband can take her back during the 'iddah without a new contract.
4
Understand the reasoning behind the ruling
The commentary explains: the woman does not possess the power to effect what was not delegated to her. The delegation was for one talaq, so she cannot produce more. Also, the word 'talaq' itself implies revocability unless specified as ba'in.
This reasoning is based on the nature of talaq and the principle of delegation.
5
Note the flexibility of wording
The text mentions that 'yad' (hand) can be substituted with other body parts (right hand, left, nose, tongue, eyes) as metaphorical expressions, as cited from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq.
This illustrates that the core meaning is delegation of authority, not literal body parts.

The reasoning is that the woman cannot effect more than what was delegated, and the husband's delegation is tied to a revocable talaq because the word 'talaq' itself is revocable unless specified otherwise.

رجعية (raj‘iyyah): Revocable talaq, where the husband can take his wife back during the waiting period without a new contract.

Intention of Three Talaq in Amr bil Yad: Generality and the Wife's Choice
Ruling on husband's intention of three talaq when wife chooses once

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband intends three talaq in 'amr bil yad' and the wife responds with 'ikhtartu nafsi bi-wahida', and explain the Hanafi reasoning based on the generality of 'amr'.

Core Idea
In 'amr bil yad', the husband's intention of three talaq is valid because 'amr' is general; the wife's choice with one still triggers three.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in delegated divorce (tafwid). When a husband says 'Your command is in your hand' without specifying a number but intends three talaq, and the wife responds 'I chose myself with one', Hanafi fiqh rules that three talaq fall. The key is the general nature of the word 'amr', which enables the husband's intention to take effect. This ruling is distinct from the case where the husband says 'ikhtari' (choose), where intention of three would not be effective. We will walk through the reasoning, the Quranic proof, and the practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Delegation Phrase and the Husband's Intention
The husband uses 'amruki biyadiki' (your command is in your hand) and intends three talaq internally. Recognize that the delegation does not specify a number, but the husband's intention is to trigger three.
2
Analyze the Wife's Response
The wife says 'ikhtartu nafsi bi-wahida' (I chose myself with one). Note that 'bi-wahida' (with one) qualifies the choice, not the talaq count. The wife's act of choosing is a valid response to the delegation.
The word 'ikhtiyar' (choice) is considered tamlik (granting ownership) like takhyir.
3
Understand the Generality of 'Amr'
The Hanafi argument relies on the generality of the word 'amr'. The Quranic verse (82:19) shows that 'al-amr' encompasses everything. Thus, when the husband says 'amruki biyadiki', it is general and can include all talaq. His intention of three is an intention of generality, not a contradiction.
Compare with 'ikhtari' which is not general; intention of three there would be ineffective.
4
Derive the Ruling: Three Talaq Fall
Since the husband's delegation is general and he intended three, and the wife's choice is a valid answer, three talaq fall regardless of her specifying 'with one'. The specification is on the choice, not on the number of talaq.
Al-Nahr al-Fa’iq confirms this as the accepted position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولو قال : أمرك بيدك، ونوى الثلاث " ، فقالت : اخترتُ نفسي بواحدة ، أو بمرة واحدة يقعن (3) قوله : ونوى الثلاث ؛ إنما تصح نية الثلاث في الأمر باليد دون اختياري ؛ لأن الأمر اسم عام يتناول كل شيء ، قال الله : وَالْأَمْرُ يَوْمَذٍ لِلَّهِ (3)، وإذا كان الأمرُ عاماً صالحاً لكل فعل، فإذا نوى به الطلاق صار كناية عن قوله : طلاقك بيدك، والطلاق مصدر يحتمل العموم والخصوص، فتكون نية الثلاث نية التعميم، بخلاف قوله : اختاري، فإنّه لا يحتمل العموم كما مر كذا في «العناية). (And if he said: Your command is in your hand, and he intended three, and she said: I chose myself with one, or with one time, they fall. As in al-‘Inaya. (4) His statement: 'they fall'; meaning three talaq, because choice (ikhtiyar) is a valid answer for 'amr bil yad' since it is a form of granting ownership like takhyir, and 'one' describes the choice, so it is as if she said: I chose myself with one time, and thus three talaq fall. Commentary
This passage deals with a scenario where the husband delegates using 'amruki biyadiki' without specifying a number, but he intends three talaq in his heart. The wife responds with 'I chose myself with one' or 'with one time'. According to the commentary, three talaq fall. The reasoning distinguishes between 'amr bil yad' and 'ikhtari' (choose). In 'amr bil yad', the word 'amr' is general, as shown by the Quranic verse, and thus can be interpreted to include all talaq. When the husband intends three, his intention is valid because the delegation is general. In contrast, 'ikhtari' is not general and thus intention of three would not be effective. The wife's response 'with one' or 'with one time' is interpreted as modifying the choice, not the talaq count; since the choice is an answer to a general delegation, it triggers three talaq. This is the relied-upon view from al-Nahr al-Fa’iq.

Key Terms
نوى الثلاث (nawa al-thalath): The husband's intention of three talaq when delegating without specifying a number.

6 min
One Ba'in Talaq When Wife Expressly Divorces Herself Once
Ruling on wife saying 'I divorced myself once' after delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a delegated wife uses explicit divorce language and explain why it results in one ba'in talaq.

Core Idea
When the wife owns her matter via delegation, her explicit divorce yields one ba'in talaq, not revocable.

In this passage, we examine a scenario where a husband delegates talaq authority to his wife by saying 'Your command is in your hand.' When the wife uses clear language like 'I divorced myself once,' does it result in a revocable or irrevocable talaq? The Hanafi school holds that it is one ba'in talaq, a ruling based on the nature of delegation itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and delegation
Recognize that the husband delegated authority (tafwid) to the wife. She then explicitly says 'I divorced myself once' or 'I chose myself with one talaq.'
The delegation is key; without it, her words have no effect.
2
Determine the type of talaq
The ruling is one ba'in (irrevocable) talaq. This is the final outcome regardless of the explicit wording.
Explicit wording in ordinary talaq would give revocable, but delegation changes the result.
3
Understand why it is ba'in
The husband's delegation made the wife own her matter (tamlik al-amr). Owning her matter is only realized by a ba'in talaq, not a revocable one, because revocable talaq still leaves the marriage intact during iddah.
The purpose of delegation determines the type of talaq.
4
Understand why it is one
In 'I divorced myself once,' the word 'one' qualifies the omitted word 'tatliqah' (instance of divorce), indicating a single talaq. In 'I chose myself with a talaq,' the 'ta' suffix also indicates unity.
Contrast this with saying 'with one time' which would indicate choice and yield three talaq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالت : طلقت نفسي واحدة، أو اخترتُ نفسي بتطليقة فواحدة بائنة (1) قوله : فواحدة بائنة ؛ أما وقوع البائنة وإن كان جوابها بلفظ صريح يقعُ به الرجعي ؛ لأن المعتبر تفويض الزوج، وتفويضه إنّما كان بالبائن ؛ لأنها به تملك المرأة أمرها لا الرجعي. وأما كونها واحدة فلان الواحدة في قولها : طلقت نفسي بواحدة، صفة المصدر محذوف، وهو طلقة؛ إذ خصوص العامل اللفظي، وهو طلقت يدلُّ على خصوص المحذوف. وفي قولها : اخترتُ نفسي بتطليقة، التاء تدلّ على وحدة الطلاق، بخلاف قولها : اخترت بمرّة واحدة أو بواحدة، فإنّ الواحدة هناك صفة للاختيارة لا للتطليقة. كذا في «الفتح»
And if she said: 'I divorced myself once', or 'I chose myself with one talaq', then one irrevocable (talaq) falls. (1) His statement: 'one irrevocable (talaq)'; as for the occurrence of the irrevocable, even though her answer is with an explicit word that would cause revocable, because the consideration is the husband's delegation, and his delegation was for the irrevocable; because by it the woman owns her matter, not the revocable. And as for it being one, because the 'one' in her statement 'I divorced myself with one' is an adjective for the omitted verbal noun, which is 'tatliqah'; since the specificity of the verbal agent, which is 'tallaqtu', indicates the specificity of the omitted word. And in her statement 'I chose myself with a talaq', the 'ta' indicates the unity of the talaq, unlike her statement 'I chose with one time' or 'with one', where 'one' there is an adjective for the choice, not for the talaq. As in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when the wife responds to delegation by using explicit language: 'I divorced myself once' or 'I chose myself with one talaq'. In such cases, one irrevocable (ba'in) talaq falls. The commentary from al-Fath (Fath al-Qadir) explains why it is ba'in despite the explicit wording: the husband's delegation was for the wife to own her matter, and owning her matter can only be through a ba'in talaq, not a revocable one, because a revocable talaq leaves the marriage intact during the waiting period. The wife does not own her matter fully. As for why it is only one talaq, the word 'one' qualifies the omitted verbal noun 'tatliqah' (one instance of divorce), and in the second phrase 'bi-tatliqah', the 'ta' indicates a single instance. This is contrasted with the previous case where she said 'with one time' where 'one' qualifies the choice, not the talaq, leading to three talaq.

Key Terms
بائنة (ba'inah): Irrevocable talaq; the marriage ends immediately without the husband's right to take her back except with a new contract.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation; the husband granting authority to the wife to pronounce talaq.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One revocable talaq
B) One irrevocable talaq
C) Three revocable talaq
D) No talaq

Key Takeaway
When delegation is for the wife to own her matter, her explicit expression of talaq results in one ba'in talaq.

Time Restriction in Delegation: Excluding Nights and Intervening Days
Ruling on time-restricted delegation in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on delegation restricted to 'today and after tomorrow' and apply the linguistic principle to exclude nights and the intervening day.

Core Idea
When a husband specifies 'today and after tomorrow', the wife's authority is limited to those days alone.

This passage addresses a nuanced scenario in delegation of talaq. The husband says 'Your command is in your hand today and after tomorrow'. The Hanafi school uses a linguistic analysis to determine exactly which times are included. Understanding this reasoning helps clarify how time restrictions operate in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statement and the legal question
The husband says 'today and after tomorrow'. The question is: can she choose during the night of today, the night after tomorrow, or the intervening day (tomorrow)?
2
Understand the Hanafi ruling
The night does not enter, nor does the intervening day. So she can only choose on the day the statement was made and on the day after tomorrow.
3
Examine the linguistic principle
When two similar time periods are conjoined with a similar period between them, the default is that each command is restricted to its own period. So 'today' and 'after tomorrow' are separate, excluding the night and the in-between day.
4
Apply the principle to the scenario
Thus, if she chooses herself during the night or on the intervening day, the talaq does not occur. Only choices made on the specified days are effective.
5
Note the source
This reasoning is cited from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq, a prominent Hanafi fiqh source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال : أمرُكِ بيدك اليوم وبعد غد، لا يدخلُ اللّيلُ فيه (2) قوله : لا يدخل الليل فيه ؛ المراد بالليل جنسه ، فيشمل الليلتين : ليلة مقارنة لليوم وليلة مقارنة للغد، وكذا لا يدخلُ اليوم الفاصل ؛ أي لا يكون لها الأمر باليد في هذه الأوقات، بل يكون في اليوم، وفيما بعد غد، حتى لو اختارت نفسها في الليل أو في الغد لا يقع الطلاق. والوجه في ذلك : أن عطف زمن على زمن مماثل مفعول بينهما بزمان مماثل لهما ظاهر في قصد تقييد الأمر المذكور بالأول، وتقييد أمر آخر بالثاني، فكأنه قال : أمرك بيدك اليوم ، وأمرك بيدك بعد غد، ولو أفرد اليوم لا يدخلُ الليل فيه، فكذا إذا عطفت جملة أخرى. كذا في «البحر»
And if he said: 'Your command is in your hand today and after tomorrow', the night does not enter into it. (2) His statement: 'the night does not enter into it'; what is meant by night is its genus, so it includes the two nights: the night adjacent to today and the night adjacent to tomorrow. Likewise, the intervening day does not enter; meaning she does not have the command in these times, but rather on the day and on after tomorrow, such that if she chooses herself during the night or on the day (in between), the talaq does not occur. The reasoning for this is that conjoining a period of time to another similar period, with a similar period of time between them, is apparent in intending to restrict the mentioned command to the first, and to restrict another command to the second. So it is as if he said: 'Your command is in your hand today, and your command is in your hand after tomorrow'. And if he had mentioned only 'today', the night would not enter, so too when he conjoins another sentence. As in al-Bahr.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific scenario where the husband restricts the delegation to specific times: 'today and after tomorrow'. The commentary explains that the night does not enter the delegation, meaning that the wife cannot exercise her delegated authority during the night of today or the night after tomorrow (the night preceding the day after tomorrow). Additionally, the intervening day (tomorrow itself) is also excluded. The reasoning from al-Bahr al-Ra’iq is linguistic: when someone says 'today and after tomorrow', the default understanding is that they are specifying two separate times, with the period in between excluded. Therefore, delegation only applies on the day of the statement and on the day after the next, i.e., the day after tomorrow. If she chooses herself during the excluded times, no talaq falls.

Key Terms
يدخل الليل فيه (yadkhulu al-laylu fihi): Whether the night period is included in the time restriction of the delegation.
اليوم الفاصل (al-yawm al-fasil): The intervening day (tomorrow) between today and after tomorrow.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in the passage, which of the following times is NOT included when the husband says 'Your command is in your hand today and after tomorrow'?
A) The night of today
B) The day after tomorrow
C) Today
D) The day after tomorrow's night

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when delegation is restricted to 'today and after tomorrow', the night and the intervening day are excluded, and the wife can only exercise her right on the named days.

Wife's Self-Divorce: One Ba'in Divorce
Explains ruling when wife says 'I divorced myself once' for delegated divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife with delegated authority divorces herself using explicit wording, and explain why it results in a ba'in divorce rather than raj'i.

Core Idea
When a husband delegates divorce to his wife, her self-divorce is always ba'in—she cannot own herself with a revocable tie.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in the chapter of delegation (tafwid): the wife says 'I divorced myself once' or 'I chose myself with one divorce.' While the wording is explicit and could normally yield a revocable divorce, the Hanafi school rules it is irrevocable (ba'in). The commentary provides the legal reasoning: the delegation itself was for ba'in because the wife can only own herself through an irrevocable divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The wife says 'I divorced myself once.' The ruling is one ba'in (irrevocable) divorce. Note the result: the marriage terminates immediately and is not revocable.
This is the explicit ruling from the text.
2
Understand the nature of delegation
The husband delegated the power of divorce to the wife. In Hanafi law, this delegation is understood to be for ba'in, not raj'i. Why? Because the wife can only own her own marital status through an irrevocable divorce—she cannot hold a revocable tie over herself.
This is the key reasoning: delegation implies ownership of the divorce matter.
3
Analyze the wording for number
Examine the phrases: in 'I divorced myself once,' the word 'one' qualifies the omitted verbal noun 'divorce.' In 'I chose myself with one divorce,' the ta marks the divorce as single. If the wording were ambiguous (e.g., 'I chose with one instance'), it would not necessarily count as one divorce.
Linguistic analysis confirms a single divorce.
4
Consider the husband's intent
If the husband delegated three divorces but the wife only performed one, it still counts as one. She only executed what she said, and she cannot give more than she owns at that moment.
The delegation's scope does not force the wife to exceed her own pronouncement.
5
Contrast with other schools
Other madhahib (e.g., Shafi'i) may hold that explicit wording such as 'I divorce myself' yields a raj'i divorce. The Hanafi position emphasizes that the delegation controls the type, not the wording.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf with a practical difference in the possibility of revocability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالت : طلقت نفسي واحدة، أو اخترتُ نفسي بتطليقة فواحدة بائنة (1) [1] قوله : فواحدة بائنة ؛ أما وقوع البائنة وإن كان جوابها بلفظ صريح يقعُ به الرجعي ؛ لأن المعتبر تفويض الزوج، وتفويضه إنّما كان بالبائن ؛ لأنها به تملك المرأة أمرها لا الرجعي. وأما كونها واحدة فلان الواحدة في قولها : طلقت نفسي بواحدة، صفة المصدر محذوف، وهو طلقة؛ إذ خصوص العامل اللفظي، وهو طلقت يدلُّ على خصوص المحذوف. وفي قولها : اخترتُ نفسي بتطليقة، التاء تدلّ على وحدة الطلاق، بخلاف قولها : اخترت بمرّة واحدة أو بواحدة، فإنّ الواحدة هناك صفة للاختيارة لا للتطليقة. كذا في «الفتح» (۱) لأنه لما نوى ثلاثاً فقد فوّض إليها الثلاث، وهي أتت بالواحدة فيقعُ واحدة كما لو قال لها : طلقي نفسك ثلاثاً، فطلقت نفسها واحدةً، فتكون بائنة ؛ لأنه ملكها نفسها، ولا تملك نفسها إلا بالبائن. وتمامه في البدائع(۳ : ۱۱۷).
And if she says: I divorced myself once, or I chose myself with one divorce, then it is one irrevocable divorce. (1) [1] His statement: 'one irrevocable divorce' – as for the occurrence of irrevocable divorce even though her response is with an explicit wording by which a revocable divorce would occur, it is because what is considered is the husband's delegation, and his delegation was for irrevocable divorce only; because by it the woman owns her matter, not revocable. As for it being one, because 'one' in her saying 'I divorced myself once' is an adjective of the omitted verbal noun, which is 'divorce'; since the specificity of the verbal operator, which is 'I divorced', indicates the specificity of the omitted noun. And in her saying 'I chose myself with one divorce', the ta indicates the unity of the divorce, contrary to her saying 'I chose with one instance' or 'with one', where 'one' there is an adjective of the 'choice' not of the 'divorce'. Thus in al-Fath. (1) Because if he intended three, he delegated three to her, and she performed one, so it occurs as one, just as if he said to her: divorce yourself three times, and she divorced herself once, then it is irrevocable; because he made her own herself, and she does not own herself except with irrevocable. Its completion is in al-Bada'i' (3:117).
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter of delegation (tafwid). When a husband delegates the power of divorce to his wife, and she says 'I divorced myself once' or 'I chose myself with one divorce,' the result is a single irrevocable divorce (ba'in). The commentary explains why it is ba'in despite the wording being explicit (which usually yields raj'i): because the delegation itself was for ba'in (since the wife cannot own herself except with ba'in). And why it is one: linguistic analysis shows that 'one' qualifies the omitted 'divorce', and in the second phrase the ta marks the single divorce. If the husband had intended three but the wife only performed one, it still counts as one. The Hanafi position is clear.

Key Terms
بائنة (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take his wife back without a new marriage contract.
تفويض (tafwid): The husband's delegation of the power to divorce to the wife, giving her the right to divorce herself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce (tafwid), the wife's self-divorce is always ba'in, regardless of wording, because she cannot own herself with a revocable divorce.

Delegation with Time Conditions: Today vs. Tomorrow
Explains effect of night and rejection in delegation with time conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two time-phrased delegations (tafwid) and determine whether the delegation is one or two based on the presence or absence of an intervening day, and state the effect of rejection.

Core Idea
The gap of a day between two time references creates two separate delegations; no gap makes it one.

A husband may delegate the power of divorce to his wife for a specific time. The phrasing of that time affects whether it is one delegation covering a continuous period or two separate delegations. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the key distinction between saying 'today and after tomorrow' versus 'today and tomorrow', and the consequences when the wife rejects or acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The passage contrasts two phrasings: 'today and after tomorrow' (with an intervening day) and 'today and tomorrow' (no intervening day). Identify the difference in the time intervals.
2
Understand the effect of the night
In the first scenario, the night and the intervening day are excluded from the delegation; in the second, the night is included. Trace why this is based on the number of delegations.
3
Apply the ruling to rejection
If the wife rejects the delegation on the first day, does the second remain? The answer depends on whether it is one or two delegations. For two delegations, rejection of one leaves the other valid; for one, rejection of part nullifies the whole.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf disagrees on one sub-case: when the husband says 'today' and 'tomorrow' as separate sentences. Understand his reasoning and why the majority view (Abu Hanifa/Muhammad) is preferred.
Refer to al-Hidayah for details.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(٤) (۱) لأنه لما نوى ثلاثاً فقد فوّض إليها الثلاث، وهي أتت بالواحدة فيقعُ واحدة كما لو قال لها : طلقي نفسك ثلاثاً، فطلقت نفسها واحدةً، فتكون بائنة ؛ لأنه ملكها نفسها، ولا تملك نفسها إلا بالبائن. وتمامه في البدائع(۳ : ۱۱۷). [1] قوله : وبطل ... الخ ؛ يعني فيما إذا قال : أمرك بيدك اليوم وبعد غد إن ردّت المرأة أمر اليوم وقالت : اخترتُ الزوج، أو لم تختر نفسها ، حتى انقضى المجلس لا يبطل به أمر آخر بل أمر اليوم فقط. فلها أن تختار نفسها بعد غد، وذلك لما عرفت أنّ كلامه هذا يشتمل تفويضين مستقلين، فلا يبطل ببطلان أحدهما الآخر، وهذا بخلاف : أمرك بيدك اليوم وغداً، فإنّه إن ردّته في اليوم يبطل في الغد أيضاً ؛ لكونه تفويضاً واحداً؛ ولذا يدخلُ الليل فيه.
