(۱) لأن الطلاق لا يحتمل التأقيت، أما الأمر باليد فيحتمله فيصح ضرب المدة له غير أن عطف زمن على زمن مماثل مفصول بينهما بزمن مماثل لهما ظاهر في قصد تقييد الأمر المذكور بالأول، وتقييد أمر آخر بالثاني، فصار عطف جملة : أي أمرك بيدك اليوم وأمرك بيدك بعد غد. ولو قال : أمرك بيدك اليوم لا يدخل الليل، بخلاف اليوم وغداً ، فإنه لم يفصل بينهما . لتقوم الدلالة على القصد المذكور فكان جمعاً بحرف الجمع في التمليك الواحد. ينظر: «فتح القدير (٤ : ٩٠ - ٩١). (۲) «الهداية» (٤ : ٩٢).
And if he said: 'Your command is in your hand today and after tomorrow,' the night does not enter into it. (r) [2] His statement: 'the night does not enter into it' – by 'night' is meant its genus, so it includes both nights: the night adjacent to today and the night adjacent to after tomorrow, and likewise the intervening day does not enter; i.e., she does not have the command in her hand during these times, but only on today and on after tomorrow; so if she chooses herself during the night or on the intervening day, the divorce does not occur. The reasoning is that connecting a time to a similar time with a similar time between them is apparent in intending to restrict the mentioned command to the first, and restricting another command to the second; so it is as if he said: 'Your command is in your hand today' and 'Your command is in your hand after tomorrow.' And if he had said only 'today,' the night would not enter, so likewise when another sentence is connected. Thus in al-Bahr. (4) (1) Because if he intended three, he delegated three to her, and she performed one, so it occurs as one, just as if he said to her: divorce yourself three times, and she divorced herself once, then it is irrevocable; because he made her own herself, and she does not own herself except with irrevocable. Its completion is in al-Bada'i' (3:117). (2) (al-Nahr al-Fa'iq 2:371). (3) (Fath al-Qadir 4:89). (4) (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 3:349). [PAGE_296] And the command of today is nullified if she rejects it, and the command after tomorrow remains. And in [the case of] 'Your command is in your hand today and tomorrow,' the night enters, and the command does not remain for tomorrow if she rejects it on her day (1) – because the night becomes subsidiary here, so the whole becomes one delegation; thus if she rejects part of it, the whole is nullified, contrary to the first case – because it becomes two delegations, so if she rejects one, the other remains. [1] His statement: 'And is nullified...' etc.; meaning that if he said 'Your command is in your hand today and after tomorrow,' and the woman rejected the command of today and said 'I choose the husband,' or did not choose herself until the session ended, then only the command of today is nullified, not the other. So she may choose herself after tomorrow. That is because, as you know, this speech comprises two independent delegations, so the nullification of one does not nullify the other. This is contrary to 'Your command is in your hand today and tomorrow,' because if she rejects it on today, it becomes nullified for tomorrow as well – because it is one delegation; for that reason the night enters it. [2] His statement: 'so the whole becomes one delegation' – because he mentioned two times: today and tomorrow, and did not separate them with another day, so that it would become two delegations; it is like his saying: 'Your command is in your hand for two days,' in which the intermediate night enters linguistically and customarily. This ruling includes his saying 'Your command is in your hand today' and 'your command is in your hand tomorrow' as well, and there is a disagreement of Abu Yusuf, mentioned in al-Hidayah (2). [3] His statement: 'the whole is nullified' – because rejection is not divisible and cannot be partial. [4] His statement: 'contrary to the first case' – meaning the first issue, which is when he said 'Your command is in your hand today and after tomorrow.' (1) Because divorce does not admit of time limitation, but the command in the hand does admit it, so setting a period is valid; however, connecting a time to a similar time with a similar time between them is apparent in intending to restrict the mentioned command to the first, and restricting another command to the second; thus it becomes the connection of a sentence: i.e., 'Your command is in your hand today' and 'your command is in your hand after tomorrow.' And if he said 'Your command is in your hand today,' the night does not enter, contrary to 'today and tomorrow,' because he did not separate them, so that the indication of the mentioned intent does not stand, thus it is a conjunction with the letter of conjunction in one single granting. See Fath al-Qadir (4:90-91). (2) al-Hidayah (4:92).
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage discusses the delegation of the power of divorce to the wife with temporal limitations. When the husband says 'your command is in your hand today and after tomorrow,' it creates two separate delegations: one for today and one for after tomorrow. Therefore, the night and the intervening day are not included; she can act only on today and on after tomorrow. If she rejects the delegation of today (e.g., by not choosing or by choosing the husband), only that delegation is nullified and the one for after tomorrow remains. In contrast, if he says 'your command is in your hand today and tomorrow,' the lack of a gap means it is a single delegation covering both days, so the night between them is included. If she rejects it today, the whole delegation is nullified. There is a mention of a disagreement from Abu Yusuf regarding the ruling when he says 'today' and 'tomorrow' separately, as recorded in al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
أمرك بيدك (amruki bi-yadiki): A phrase used by a husband to delegate the power of divorce to his wife, literally 'your command is in your hand'.
تفويض (tafwid): The act of delegating the power of divorce to the wife, which can be restricted by time or conditions.
دخول الليل (dukhul al-layl): The inclusion of the night period within the time of delegation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is also nullified
B) It remains valid
C) It becomes conditional on the husband's consent
D) It automatically becomes a divorce

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the presence or absence of an intervening day between time references in a divorce delegation determines whether it is one or two delegations, with direct impact on the validity of the wife's actions and the effect of rejection.

Delegation of Divorce with Time Conditions: One Delegation or Two?
Analyzing delegation of divorce with time intervals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a single continuous delegation and two separate delegations based on the wording of time conditions, and apply the rule that partial rejection voids a single delegation but not separate ones.

Core Idea
Conjoined time words without a gap create one delegation; separated times create two, so rejection of one does not void the other.

The delegation of divorce (tafwid) allows a husband to give his wife authority to divorce herself. When the husband specifies time periods, the wording determines whether it is one continuous delegation or multiple independent ones. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the distinction by analyzing the phrases 'today and after tomorrow' versus 'today and tomorrow', and the effect of the wife's rejection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's wording
Extract the exact time condition phrase the husband used. Is it 'today and after tomorrow' or 'today and tomorrow'? The wording determines the legal outcome.
Pay attention to the conjunction and the presence of a gap.
2
Determine if the times are contiguous or separated
If the two time periods are separated by another day (e.g., today and after tomorrow), they are considered separate delegations. If they are contiguous (today and tomorrow), they form one continuous period including the night.
Contiguous means the second time immediately follows the first without interruption.
3
Apply the rule for delegation unity
For contiguous times, the delegation is one and indivisible. For separated times, each period is an independent delegation.
The Hanafi school holds that wording without a gap indicates a single comprehensive delegation.
4
Apply the rule for partial rejection
If the wife rejects (or fails to choose) regarding part of a single delegation, the entire delegation is void. If she rejects one of two separate delegations, the other remains valid.
Rejection (radd) cannot be partial: it either accepts or rejects the whole delegation.

And the command of today is invalidated if she rejects it, and the command remains for after tomorrow. And in 'your affair is in your hand for today and tomorrow', the night enters, and the command does not remain for tomorrow if she rejects it during the day. Because the night becomes subsidiary here, so the whole becomes one delegation. If she rejects it in part, the whole is invalidated. Contrary to the first case because it becomes two delegations, so if she rejects one, the other remains. [1] His statement: 'And invalidated... etc.' means when he said: Your affair is in your hand for today and after tomorrow. If the woman rejects the command of today and says: I choose the husband, or she does not choose herself until the session expires, another command is not invalidated by it, rather only the command of today. So she may choose herself after tomorrow. That is because you know that this speech of his includes two independent delegations, so the invalidation of one does not invalidate the other. And this is contrary to: Your affair is in your hand for today and tomorrow, for if she rejects it today, it becomes invalid for tomorrow as well, because it is one delegation; therefore the night enters into it. [2] His statement: 'so the whole becomes one delegation' because he mentioned two times: today and tomorrow, and did not separate them by another day such that that would make two delegations. So it is like his saying: Your affair is in your hand for two days, and the intervening night enters by linguistic and customary usage. And this ruling includes his saying: Your affair is in your hand today, and your affair is in your hand tomorrow, also. And in it there is a difference from Abu Yusuf, he mentioned it in Al-Hidayah. [3] His statement: 'the whole is invalidated' because rejection cannot be divided nor partial. [4] His statement: 'Contrary to the first case' meaning the first issue, which is when he said: Your affair is in your hand today and after tomorrow.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on delegation (tafwid) when the husband specifies time periods. The commentator explains the base text's distinction between two scenarios. First: saying 'your affair is in your hand today and after tomorrow' creates two independent delegations. If the wife rejects the first delegation (by not choosing herself within the session), it does not affect the second; she may still exercise her choice after tomorrow. Second: saying 'your affair is in your hand today and tomorrow' is treated as a single composite delegation covering the two days including the intervening night. Rejection on the first day voids the entire delegation. The proof is linguistic: conjointing two similar times without a gap indicates a single continuous period, whereas inserting a different time (after tomorrow) separates them into two distinct commands. The commentator also notes that this ruling applies when the husband repeats the phrase 'your affair is in your hand' for each day, and mentions that Abu Yusuf held a different view regarding the repeated form. The principle is that rejection (radd) cannot be partial; it either accepts or rejects the entire delegation.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of the power to divorce to the wife.
رد (radd): Rejection by the wife of the delegated power; if she does not choose herself within the session, it is considered rejection.
مجلس (majlis): The session in which the delegation is offered; the wife's choice or rejection must occur within this session.

Key Takeaway

Intention for Number of Divorces in Delegated Talaq
Explaining effect of intention on divorce by delegated wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of divorces that occur when a husband delegates divorce by saying 'Divorce yourself' with various intentions, and explain the distinction between a 'considered single entity' and a 'pure number'.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, the generic command 'divorce' defaults to one; intention for three works as a single entity but intention for two fails as a pure number.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi scenario: a husband tells his wife 'Divorce yourself' (or similar delegation). The number of divorces that actually occur depends on the husband's intention and the nature of the number intended. We will unpack why one revocable divorce is the default, three is achievable by intention, two is invalid for free women, and how the rule changes for enslaved women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation scenario
Recognize that the husband has used a formula of tafwid (delegation) like 'Talliqi nafsaki' (Divorce yourself). This gives the wife the power to pronounce divorce.
This is different from the husband pronouncing divorce directly.
2
Determine the default ruling when no intention is expressed
If the husband does not specify a number, or intends one divorce, one revocable divorce occurs. This is because the word 'divorce' (as a command) is a generic noun that applies to the minimum (one).
3
Analyze the validity of intention for three
If the husband intends three and the wife pronounces three, all three occur. Three is considered a 'single entity' (wahid i'tibari) because it is the complete limit of divorce for a free woman. The masdar (verbal noun) can extend to encompass this whole.
The intention must be present and the wife must pronounce accordingly.
4
Explain why intention for two is invalid for a free woman
Intention for two is a 'pure number' (adad mahd) that cannot be read into the generic word 'divorce'. Two is not a complete unit like three; it is an intermediate number. Thus, even if the husband intends two, only one divorce occurs.
The exception: for an enslaved woman, two is the maximum, so it becomes a considered single entity.
5
Apply the exception for enslaved women
If the wife is an enslaved woman, intention for two is valid because she can only be divorced twice. Two becomes her 'considered single entity' (the complete limit). Therefore, two divorces occur if intended.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter on will/option. And if he said: 'Divorce yourself' and he did not intend, or intended one, and she divorced herself, it occurs as revocable. And if she divorced three and he intended that, it is valid. Intent for two is invalid, unless the married woman is an enslaved woman.
[Commentary]
[1] His statement: 'And if he said... etc.' When he finished with the issues of the two forms of delegation – namely option and command by hand – he began with what relates to the second form, which they call will (mashi'ah) such as 'Divorce yourself if you wish' or 'Divorce yourself', for although the word will is not mentioned, it is definitely intended. [2] His statement: 'it occurs as revocable' meaning one revocable divorce. As for its being revocable, it is due to the presence of the explicit wording by which revocable divorce occurs from his side. And as for its being one, it is because it corresponds to what he intended when he intended one, and because it is the minimum and certain when the husband intended nothing of the number. Similarly, one occurs in the case of intention for two in the case of a free woman because, as was passed earlier, intention of pure number is not considered. [3] His statement: 'and he intended that' meaning the three, and the masculine pronoun is due to interpreting it as the mentioned thing or because the three is a considered single entity. He stipulated this because if he intended nothing or intended less than three, the three do not occur, but one occurs as before. [4] His statement: 'it is valid' because 'divorce' is a command from the verbal noun tawliq, so it is as if he said: perform the act of divorce or cause divorce upon yourself, so the masdar is mentioned. And it has passed that the masdar, like other generic nouns, applies to the minimum with the possibility of the whole, so the intention of three operates on it. [5] His statement: 'it is invalid' meaning the intention of two is not valid, for it is an intention of pure number. [6] His statement: 'in her case' because for an enslaved woman, the husband owns only two divorces over her.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on delegation by way of option (mashi'ah), such as saying 'Divorce yourself if you wish.' The commentator explains that the base text covers the case where the husband says 'Divorce yourself' without mentioning the number. The ruling: if the husband had no specific intention, or intended one divorce, and the wife divorces herself, one revocable divorce occurs. If the husband intended three divorces and the wife pronounces three, then three divorces occur and it is valid. However, if the husband intended two divorces, that intention is invalid for a free woman; only one divorce occurs. The reason is that the word 'divorce' (as a command) is derived from the masdar (verbal noun) which is a generic noun that by default applies to the minimum (one), but can be extended by intention to the whole (three). Intention for two is a pure number (adad mahd) which cannot be read into the generic word because two is not a single whole divisible from the abstract concept of divorce. For an enslaved woman, however, intention for two is valid because her capacity for divorce is limited to two; thus two is the maximum and can be considered a single entity of 'complete divorce' for her. The commentary anchors this in the principle that the masdar, like other genus nouns, covers the minimum and can encompass more through intention, but not arbitrary numbers.

Key Terms
مشيئة (mashi'ah): Will or option; a form of delegation where the husband says 'divorce yourself if you wish' or similar, giving the wife the choice to divorce.
نية (niyyah): Intention of the husband regarding the number of divorces; it can affect the outcome if it is a number that is a 'considered single entity' (wahid i'tibari) like three.
عدد محض (adad mahd): Pure number; a number that is not conceptualized as a single entity in the context of divorce. Two is a pure number because it is not a single complete divorce count like three (which is the maximum for a free woman).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Three divorces occur because the word 'divorce' implies the maximum.
D) No divorce occurs because the intention does not match the pronouncement.

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, the number of divorces depends on the husband's intention: one by default, three with intention, two invalid for free women (valid for enslaved women).

Rulings on Delegated Divorce (Tafwid al-Talaq)
Rulings on husband's delegation of divorce to wife via saying 'ṭalliqī nafsak'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings when a husband delegates divorce to his wife by saying 'ṭalliqī nafsak', including the effect of intention as to number, and evaluate the wife's response 'abantu nafsī' and its legal effect.

Core Idea
The infinitive 'ṭalliqī' is a genus noun; it yields one revocable divorce by default but can carry intention for three.

This passage from the Hanafi commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah' explains the ruling when a husband delegates divorce to his wife using the phrase 'ṭalliqī nafsak'. The key is understanding that the imperative 'ṭalliqī' derives from the infinitive 'taṭlīq', which is a genus noun. This linguistic insight determines how intention affects the number of divorces. The passage also addresses the wife's response 'abantu nafsī' (I have separated myself) and why it falls as a single revocable divorce despite the connotation of irrevocability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act: Delegation (Tafwīḍ)
The husband says 'ṭalliqī nafsak' (divorce yourself) to his wife. This is a form of delegation (tafwīḍ), not a direct pronouncement of divorce. The wife is authorized to effect the divorce on his behalf.
This is distinct from takhyīr (choice) where the husband says 'ikhtārī' (choose).
2
Default Ruling: One Revocable Divorce
If the husband did not intend any number, or intended one, and the wife divorces herself, it falls as a single revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī).
Revocable means the husband can take her back without a new marriage contract.
3
Effect of Intention for Three
If the husband intended three divorces and the wife pronounces three, all three take effect, making the divorce irrevocable. The intention is effective because the word 'ṭalliqī' is derived from the infinitive 'taṭlīq', which is a genus noun (ism jins) that indicates the minimum (one) but can encompass the maximum (three).
Intention of two is a 'mere number' (ʿadad maḥḍ) and is ineffective for a free wife.
4
Exception for a Slave Wife
If the wife is a slave, the husband owns only two divorces over her. In that case, intention of two is valid because two is the maximum possible for a slave.
5
Wife's Response 'Abantu Nafsī'
If the wife, in response to 'ṭalliqī nafsak', says 'abantu nafsī' (I have separated myself), it also counts as one revocable divorce. The word 'abantu' is from the vocabulary of divorce, but the added description of 'irrevocable' (bāʾin) is ignored because the delegation did not include such a specification.
This is contrasted with the word 'ikhtārī' (I choose), which is not a divorce term and produces no effect in this context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في المشيئة
ولو قال: طلقي نفسك، ولم ينو، أو نوى واحدة، فطلقت نفسها تقع رجعية، وإن طلقت ثلاثاً ونواه صح، ونيّة الثنتين لا ، إلا إذا كانت المنكوحة أمة
(ولو قال : طلّقي نفسك ، ولم ينو ، أو نوى واحدة، فطلقت نفسها تقع رجعية، وإن طلقت ثلاثاً ونواه صح ، ونية الثنتين لا، إلا إذا كانت المنكوحة أمة )؛ لأنه واحد اعتباري في حقها ، لأن قوله طلقي معناه : افعلي فعل الطلاق، فالطلاق مصدر قوله : ولو قال ... الخ ؛ لَمَّا فرغ عن مسائل صورتي التفويض ؛ أي التخيير والأمر باليد، شَرَعَ فيما يتعلق بالصورة الثانية المعبر عندهم بالمشيئة نحو : طلقي نفسك إن شئت، أو طلقي نفسك ، فإنّ المشيئة وإن لم تكن مذكورة فيه لكنها مرادة قطعاً. قوله : تقع رجعيّة؛ أي واحدةً رجعيّة، أما كون الواقع رجعياً فلوجودِ لفظ الصريح الذي يقع به الرجعي من جانبه، وأما كونه واحداً فلتطابقه بما نواه فيما نوى واحداً، ولكونه أدنى، ومتيقناً فيما لم ينو الزوج شيئاً من العدد، وكذا يقعُ واحد في صورة نية الاثنين في حق الحرّة لما مرّ سابقا أن نية العدد المحض لا تعتبر بها. قوله : ونواه ; أي الثلاث، وتذكير الضمير باعتبار تأويله بالمذكور أو كونه واحداً اعتبارياً، وإنما اشترط هذا لأنه لو لم ينو شيئاً أو نوى ما دون الثلاث لا يقع الثلاث، بل الواحد كما مرّ. قوله : صح؛ لأنَّ طلّقي أمر من التطليق، فكأنه قال : افعلي فعل الطلاق، أو أوقعي عليك الطلاق ، فصار المصدر مذكوراً ، وقد مر أن المصدر كسائر أسماء الأجناس يقع على الأدنى مع احتمال الكل، فتعمل فيه نية الثلاث. قوله : لا ; أي لا تصح نية الثنتين، فإنّها نية العدد المحض. قوله : في حقها ; فإنّ الأمة يملك الزوج عليها طلاقين فحسب.
(1) أي نوى الزوج الثلاث يقعن عليها ; لأن قوله طلقي معناه افعلي فعل الطلاق، وهو جنس فرد يحتمل الفرد حقيقة، وهو الواحد عند عدم النية والفرد اعتبارياً وهو الثلاث، ويحمل عليه عند النية. ينظر : شرح ابن ملك (ق ۹۹/أ).
( ويقع بأبنتُ نفسي رجعية) ; لأنها قالت في جواب طلقي نفسك، لها إيقاع البائن ، بل مطلق الطلاق، ففي قولها : أَبَنْتُ نفسي ; بَطَلَت صفةُ الإبانة ، وبقى مطلق الطلاق، وهو رجعي
قوله : لفظ فرد ; هو إما بالإضافة وإما بالتوصيف ; أي لفظ مفرد غير دال على الكثرة.

كما إذا قالت في جواب طلّقي نفسك : طلقت نفسي طلقة بائنة، فإنه يقع فيه الواحد الرجعي ويلغو الوصف الزائد على التفويض، وهذا بخلاف الاختيار، فإنّه ليس من ألفاظ الطلاق، ألا ترى أنه لو قال لامرأته : اخترتك أو اختاري ونوى به الطلاق لا يصح.
ولو قالت ابتداءً : اخترتُ نفسي، فقال الزوج أجزت، لا يقع شيء، نعم عرف بالإجماع أنه طلاق إذا حصل جواباً للتخيير، فلهذا لا يقع شيء بقولها : اخترتُ نفسي، في جواب: طلّقي نفسك. كذا في «الهداية»
قوله : وبقي مطلق الطلاق ; يرد عليه : أنه لا ذكر هاهنا في كلامها للطلاق لا مطلقاً ولا مقيّداً، وإنما قالت : أبنت وهو يوجب ثبوت البينونة، وهو لا يستلزم الطلاق لوجوده في صورة الفرقة من غير طلاق.

أوقعت على نفسي الطلاق البائن ; إذ المفوض إليها إنما هو الطلاق لا غيره.
Chapter on Will (al-Mashīʼah). And if he said: 'Divorce yourself,' and he did not intend, or intended one, and she divorced herself, it occurs as revocable. And if she divorced three and he intended [three], it is valid, and the intention of two is not [valid], except if the married woman is a slave-girl. (And if he said: 'Divorce yourself,' and he did not intend, or intended one, and she divorced herself, it occurs as revocable. And if she divorced three and he intended, it is valid, and the intention of two is not, except if the married woman is a slave-girl); because it is an integral unit in her right, because his saying 'divorce' means: perform the act of divorce, for divorce is an infinitive... (commentary continues). And it occurs with 'I have separated myself' as revocable.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from a Hanafi fiqh commentary (sharh) on the chapter of will (al-mashīʼah) regarding delegated divorce (tafwīḍ). The author first states the primary ruling: when a husband says to his wife 'ṭalliqī nafsak' (divorce yourself) without specifying the number, or intending one, and she divorces herself, it counts as a single revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī). If she pronounces three divorces and the husband intended three, the three are valid. However, if he intended two, it is not valid unless the wife is a slave-girl (because a slave-girl can only be divorced twice). The commentary explains that the word 'ṭalliqī' is a command derived from the infinitive 'taṭlīq' (to cause divorce), which is a genus noun (ism jins) that in its absolute form indicates the minimum (one divorce) but can accommodate the maximum (three) through intention. Thus, the husband’s intention of three is effective, but intention of two is 'mere number' (ʿadad maḥḍ) and not recognized. The passage then addresses the case where the wife responds with 'abantu nafsī' (I have separated myself). The commentary clarifies that this is considered a valid divorce response because 'abantu' is from the vocabulary of divorce; the added description of 'irrevocable' (bāʼin) is ignored because the husband’s delegation was to 'divorce' absolutely, not to a specific type. Therefore, it falls as one revocable divorce. The commentary also distinguishes this from the word 'ikhtiyār' (choice), which is not a divorce term unless used in a specific context of taḥyīr. The passage concludes with a critical refinement: an objection that 'abantu' does not mention divorce is answered by noting that in the context of delegation, it implicitly means 'I have made divorce irrevocable upon myself'.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashīʼah): The delegation of divorce by the husband to the wife, often expressed as 'ṭalliqī nafsak in shiʼti' or simply 'ṭalliqī nafsak', where the wife has the choice to divorce herself.
العدد المحض (al-ʿadad al-maḥḍ): A mere number (like two) that is not inherently indicated by the verbal form; intentions of pure numbers are not recognized in this context.
الإبانة (al-ibānah): Irrevocable separation (ṭalāq bāʾin); here, if the wife says 'abantu nafsī' (I have separated myself), the description of irrevocability is ignored and it falls as revocable divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a husband says 'ṭalliqī nafsak' without any intention, and his free wife responds 'ṭallaqtu nafsī thalāthan', what is the result?
A) Three irrevocable divorces.
B) One revocable divorce.
C) No divorce because the wife exceeded the delegation.
D) It depends on whether the wife intended three.

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, the word 'ṭalliqī' as a genus noun defaults to one revocable divorce; intention for three is effective, but intention for two is not, except for slave wives.

Wife's Statement 'Abantu Nafsi' in Response to Delegation
Ruling on wife saying 'abantu nafsi' in response to delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife says 'abantu nafsi' in response to tafwid al-talaq and explain why any description of irrevocability is void.

Core Idea
Delegation to divorce excludes conditions: 'abantu nafsi' yields one revocable divorce.

When a husband delegates the authority to divorce to his wife, the scope of her power is limited to effecting a divorce as delagated. If she adds extra conditions, like irrevocability, those are legally disregarded. This lesson examines this rule through the specific phrase 'abantu nafsi' (I have separated myself) and contrasts it with other responses like 'ikhtiyar' (choice).

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The husband says 'talliqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself), delegating the power to divorce. The wife responds 'abantu nafsi' (I have separated myself).
This is a classic example of tafwid al-talaq (delegation of divorce).
2
Classify the wife's response
Determine that 'abantu' is a kinayah (metonym) for divorce, not a sarih (explicit) word. In Hanafi fiqh, kinayah words are valid for divorce when accompanied by intention.
The proof: if the husband himself says 'abantuki' intending divorce, it is valid.
3
Apply the scope of delegation
The husband's delegation only grants authority over divorce, not over adding conditions like irrevocability. Therefore, any extra description the wife adds is void.
This is a qiyas: just as the husband's direct statement 'abantuki' gives a revocable divorce without qualification, so does the wife's response.
4
Derive the ruling
The voiding of the extra description leaves a simple, absolute divorce, which in Hanafi law is revocable (raj'i).
Thus, the outcome is one revocable divorce, not an irrevocable one.
5
Contrast with 'ikhtiyar'
Note that if the wife responds with 'ikhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself) or similar, no divorce occurs because 'ikhtiyar' is not a word of divorce in this context, unlike 'abantu'.
This reinforces that 'abantu' is a recognized word of divorce.

[1] His statement: 'A singular word' is either by annexation or by description, meaning a singular word that does not indicate multiplicity. [2] His statement: 'And it occurs' means if she said 'I have separated myself' in response to 'divorce yourself', one revocable divorce occurs upon her because 'separation' is among the words of divorce. Do you not see that if he said 'I have separated you' intending divorce, or she said 'I have separated myself' and the husband said 'I have accepted that', she becomes separated; so her response corresponds to his original delegation, and the additional description of irrevocability is disregarded because it is not present in his delegation. Similarly, if she said in response to 'divorce yourself': 'I have divorced myself with an irrevocable divorce', then one revocable divorce occurs and the extra description beyond the delegation is nullified. This is contrary to 'choice' (ikhtiyar) because it is not among the words of divorce. Do you not see that if he said to his wife 'I have chosen you' or 'choose' intending divorce, it is not valid. And if she said initially 'I have chosen myself' and the husband said 'I have accepted', nothing occurs. Yes, it is known by consensus that it is divorce if it occurs in response to (the husband's) delegation of choice (takhyr), so for this reason nothing occurs by her saying 'I have chosen myself' in response to 'divorce yourself'. Thus in al-Hidayah. [3] His statement: 'And the absolute divorce remains' is objected to: that there is no mention here in her speech of divorce, neither absolute nor qualified; she only said 'I have separated' which necessitates establishing separation, and that does not entail divorce because it exists in the case of separation without divorce. The answer: that her saying 'I have separated', since it was in response to 'divorce yourself', its meaning becomes 'I have effected an irrevocable divorce upon myself' because what is delegated to her is only divorce, nothing else.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the wife's statement 'abantu nafsi' when given the delegation to divorce herself. The matn states that if she says this in response to the husband's 'talliqi nafsaki', a single revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) occurs, not an irrevocable one. The sharh expands: 'ibaanah' (separation) is a valid word for divorce, as it is used customarily with the intention of divorce. The husband's delegation only gives her the authority to divorce, not to add conditions like irrevocability. Therefore, any description of irrevocability she adds is void. The commentary then contrasts this with the word 'choice' (ikhtiyar), which is not a word of divorce initially, but becomes divorce only when used in response to a delegation of choice (takhyr). The text then addresses an objection: her statement 'abantu' does not explicitly mention divorce, and separation can occur without divorce (e.g. annulment). The answer: because it was in response to a delegation that only gives authority over divorce, her statement is interpreted as divorcing herself irrevocably, but the description of irrevocability is nullified, leaving a revocable divorce. This is the Hanafi position: the wife is seen as exercising the delegated power, and any extra conditions are ignored.

Key Terms
أبنت (abantu): In this context, 'abantu' is a verbal noun meaning 'I have separated', used as a kināyah for divorce when intended.
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; a divorce where the husband cannot take the wife back without a new marriage contract.
رجعي (raj'i): Revocable divorce; a divorce where the husband can take the wife back during the waiting period without a new marriage.
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation by the husband to the wife to divorce herself or choose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A revocable divorce (raj'i)
C) No divorce occurs
D) Three divorces

Key Takeaway
When a wife responds to a delegation to divorce herself with 'abantu nafsi', the result is one revocable divorce; any attempt to make it irrevocable is null.

When 'I Choose Myself' Does Not Divorce: The Hanafi Rule
Ruling on wife saying 'I choose myself' in response to delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between words of clear divorce (sarih) and words that require intention (kinayah), and apply the Hanafi rule that 'ikhtartu' is not a word of divorce in the context of delegation.

Core Idea
A wife's 'I choose myself' in response to 'divorce yourself' does not count as a divorce because 'choice' is not a word of divorce by convention or custom.

In Hanafi law, the words used to effect a divorce are strictly classified. Some words are sarih (explicit) and effect divorce automatically, while others are kinayah (metaphorical) and require intention. This lesson examines a specific scenario: a husband says 'talliqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself) and the wife responds 'ikhtartu nafsi' (I have chosen myself). Why does no divorce occur? The answer lies in understanding how words acquire their legal efficacy in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the wife saying 'ikhtartu nafsi' in response to the husband's delegation 'talliqi nafsaki'. The ruling is that no divorce occurs.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof given is that 'ikhtartu' is not among the words of divorce. The commentator explains that divorce words must indicate divorce by linguistic convention (wad') as in 'tallaqtu', or by custom (urf) as in 'abantu' or 'harramtu'. 'Ikhtartu' has neither.
The authority cited is Talkhīs al-Jāmi' al-Kabīr.
3
Analyze the basis: wad' and urf
Words of divorce are effective either because their literal meaning indicates divorce (wad') or because customary usage has made them synonymous with divorce (urf). 'Ikhtartu' literally means 'I chose', which does not indicate divorce, and there is no customary usage to that effect except in the specific context of takhyr (delegation of choice).
4
Apply the distinction to the case
Here the husband delegated divorce (talliqi nafsaki), not choice. Therefore, the wife's response of 'ikhtartu' is irrelevant for divorce. She must use a word that is suitable for divorce, such as 'tallaqtu nafsi' or a recognized kinayah with intention.
5
Note the exception: takhyr
If the husband had said 'ikhtari nafsaki' (choose yourself), then her response 'ikhtartu nafsi' would constitute a divorce by takhyr. That is a separate context and not applicable here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وباخترتُ نفسي لا يقع) ؛ لأنه ليس من ألفاظ الطلاق.
[١]قوله : لأنه ليس من ألفاظ الطلاق ؛ ولذا لا يقع شيء إذا قالت ابتداء : اخترت نفسي، وأجاز الزوج، قال شارح «تلخيص الجامع الكبير»: لأن هذا اللفظ لا يصلح للإيقاع ؛ لأنّ إيقاع الطلاق إنما يكون بما يدلّ عليه ،وضعاً، كما في : طلقت، أو عرفاً كما في : أبنت وحرمت ، فإنّ إيقاع الطلاق بالإبانة أو التحريم عند النية متعارف، ولا وضع في اخترت أصلاً ، ولا عرف في إيقاع الطلاق به إلا جواباً للتفويض.
And with 'I have chosen myself' nothing occurs because it is not among the words of divorce. [1] His statement: because it is not among the words of divorce; therefore nothing occurs if she says initially 'I have chosen myself' and the husband accepts. The commentator of Talkhīs al-Jāmi' al-Kabīr said: because this word is not suitable for effecting (divorce), because effecting divorce is only by what indicates it, either by linguistic convention as in 'I have divorced', or by custom as in 'I have separated' and 'I have made unlawful'; indeed effecting divorce by 'ibānah' or 'tahrim' with intention is customary. There is no linguistic (indication) in 'I have chosen' at all, nor any custom in effecting divorce with it except as a response to delegation.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof that the wife's statement 'bikhtarti nafsi' (I have chosen myself) in response to the husband's delegation 'talliqi nafsaki' does not result in divorce. The reason is that 'choice' (ikhtiyār) is not a word used for divorce, neither in linguistic convention (waḍ') like 'ṭallaqtu', nor in customary usage ('urf) like 'abantu' or 'ḥarramtu' (which are kināyāt). The only exception is when the husband delegates choice (takhyr), then her saying 'I have chosen myself' counts as divorce by consensus. But in the case of 'talliqi nafsaki', the delegation is specifically for divorce, not choice. Hence, her saying 'ikhtartu' is irrelevant and no divorce occurs. This is the Hanafi ruling: the word must be either explicit for divorce or a recognized kināyah with intention; 'choice' is neither.

Key Terms
اخترت (ikhtartu): Verb meaning 'I have chosen'; in this context, it is not a word of divorce.
وضع (waḍ'): Linguistic convention; the established meaning of a word in the Arabic language.
عرف (urf): Customary usage; how a word is understood in a particular community or legal context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'talliqi nafsaki' and the wife responds 'ikhtartu nafsi', what is the ruling?
A) One revocable divorce occurs
B) One irrevocable divorce occurs
C) No divorce occurs
D) Three divorces occur

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the word 'ikhtartu' is not a word of divorce; it only effects divorce when used as a response to a delegation of choice (takhyr), not to a delegation of divorce (tafwid).

Binding Nature and Session Limitation in Delegation of Self-Divorce
Ruling on revocation and session limitation for delegation of self-divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that a husband cannot revoke delegation of self-divorce and why it must be exercised in the same session, and apply this to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Delegation for self-divorce is a binding grant of possession, making revocation impossible and the right limited to the session.

When a husband tells his wife 'talliqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself), she gains a powerful right. Can he take it back? Must she act immediately? Hanafi law answers clearly: once delegated, it's hers, and she must decide in that same gathering. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind these two rules.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two rulings for self-divorce delegation
The husband cannot revoke the delegation, and the wife must act in the same session. Identify these as the core rules.
These rulings distinguish self-divorce from other delegations.
2
Recognize delegation as tafwid (empowerment)
Delegation of self-divorce is not a simple agency but a form of tafwid, which includes takhyir (choice) and amr bil yad (command by hand).
Tafwid gives the wife ownership of the right.
3
Analyze the legal bases: ta'liq and tamlik
The delegation is akin to a conditional divorce (ta'liq) such as 'if you enter the house, you are divorced,' which is binding. It is also a grant of possession (tamlik), similar to a gift completed without acceptance.
Both bases make the delegation irrevocable.
4
Apply the analogy to other cases
The same rules apply whether the husband says 'divorce yourself,' 'choose,' or 'your affair is in your hand.' All are tafwid and share the same binding and session-limited nature.
The ruling is general for all types of tafwid.

ولا يصح الرجوع عن طلقي نفسك، ويتقيد بالمجلس، وفي : طلقي ضرتك، وطلق امرأتي خلافهما : أي يصح عنه الرجوع، ولا يتقيد بالمجلس [١]قوله : لأنه ليس من ألفاظ الطلاق ؛ ولذا لا يقع شيء إذا قالت ابتداء : اخترت نفسي، وأجاز الزوج، قال شارح «تلخيص الجامع الكبير»: لأن هذا اللفظ لا يصلح للإيقاع ؛ لأنّ إيقاع الطلاق إنما يكون بما يدلّ عليه ،وضعاً، كما في : طلقت، أو عرفاً كما في : أبنت وحرمت ، فإنّ إيقاع الطلاق بالإبانة أو التحريم عند النية متعارف، ولا وضع في اخترت أصلاً ، ولا عرف في إيقاع الطلاق به إلا جواباً للتفويض.
[٢] قوله : ولا يصح الرجوع ... الخ ؛ هذان الحكمان ليسا بخاصتين بألفاظ المشيئة كما يوهم تخصيص طلّقي نفسك بالذكر، بل هما يعمّان جميع أنواع التفويض من التخيير والأمر باليد، ووجه عدم صحة رجوع الزوج عنها على ما في «الفتح» وغيره :
(1)
إن التفويض تعليق معنى ، فإن قوله : طلقـي نـفـسـك مثلاً، تعليق للطلاق بتطليقها، وقس عليه : أمرك بيدك، والتعليقات عقود لازمة لا يصح الرجوع عنها ، كقوله : إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق ، وأيضاً التفويض تمليك، فإنّ الزوج يُملكُ زوجته بتفويضه، والتمليك لازم لا يصح الرجوع عنه إذا تم بالملك وحده بلا توقف على قبول الآخر، ووجه التقيد بالمجلس أنّ في التفويض بأنواعه الثلاثة معنى التمليك، والتمليك يقتصر على المجلس. [٣]قوله : وفي طلقي ضرتك ؛ يعني لو قال لامرأته : طلقي ضرتك ؛ أي امرأتي الأخرى، أو قال الرجل : طلق امرأتي، يصح الرجوع عنه قبل إيقاع الوكيل الطلاق ولا يتقيّد بالمجلس، فلها أن تطلق ضرتها بعد انقضاء المجلس، وله أن يطلق امرأة المفوّض بعده.
And revocation is not valid from 'divorce yourself', and it is confined to the session. And regarding 'divorce your co-wife' and 'divorce my wife', the opposite applies: meaning revocation is valid from them, and they are not confined to the session. [1] His statement: because it is not among the words of divorce; therefore nothing occurs if she says initially 'I have chosen myself' and the husband accepts. The commentator of Talkhīs al-Jāmi' al-Kabīr said: because this word is not suitable for effecting (divorce)... (repeated from previous). [2] His statement: 'And revocation is not valid...' etc. These two rulings are not specific to the words of will (mashī'ah) as might be understood from specifying 'divorce yourself', but rather they apply to all types of delegation: of choice (takhyr) and command by hand (amr bil yad). The reason for the invalidity of the husband's revocation from them is, according to al-Fatḥ and others: that delegation is a suspension (ta'liq) in meaning, for his statement 'divorce yourself' for example is a suspension of divorce upon her divorcing; and analogously 'your affair is in your hand'; and suspensions are binding contracts from which revocation is not valid, like if he says 'if you enter the house then you are divorced'. Also, delegation is a granting of possession (tamlik), for the husband grants possession to his wife through his delegation, and possession is binding, and revocation is not valid once completed solely by the grant without need for acceptance. The reason for confinement to the session is that in the three types of delegation there is the meaning of possession, and possession is restricted to the session. [3] His statement: 'And regarding 'divorce your co-wife'' means if he said to his wife 'divorce your co-wife', i.e. my other wife, or a man said 'divorce my wife', then revocation is valid before the agent effects the divorce, and it is not confined to the session, so she may divorce the co-wife after the session ends, and he may divorce the delegated wife after it.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings on delegation in divorce. First, when a husband tells his wife 'talliqi nafsaki' (divorce yourself), he cannot revoke this delegation, and the wife must act within the same session (majlis). Second, if he says 'talliqi darataka' (divorce your co-wife) or a man says to another 'talliq imra'ati' (divorce my wife), the opposite applies: the delegator can revoke before the agent acts, and the agent can act even after the session ends. The commentary explains the reasoning: delegation of self-divorce is considered a ta'liq (suspension) and tamlik (grant of possession), both of which are binding and irrevocable. The ta'liq is similar to a conditional divorce like 'if you enter the house, you are divorced' – once the condition (the husband's statement) is made, it is binding and cannot be undone. The tamlik is like a gift that is completed by the grantor alone without acceptance. Because possession is intended to be immediate, it is limited to the session. In contrast, when the husband tells his wife to divorce a co-wife, or tells another man to divorce his wife, that is a standard agency (wakalah), which is not binding and lasts beyond the session. The key distinction: delegation of self-divorce is a form of tafwīd (empowerment) that creates a right in the wife that cannot be revoked, while delegation to divorce a third party is mere authorization (wakalah) which is revocable.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation by the husband to the wife to exercise a right (like divorce or choice).
تقيد بالمجلس (taqayyud bil majlis): Confined to the session; the wife must exercise the delegated right in the same gathering without delay.
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation; the husband's ability to undo the delegation.
تمليك (tamlik): Grant of possession; in this context, the husband grants the wife the right to divorce herself, which becomes her possession.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension; making the divorce conditional on an action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in the passage, if a husband says to his wife 'divorce yourself', which of the following is true?
A) He can revoke at any time before she acts.
B) She must act in the same session, and he cannot revoke.
C) She can act anytime, and he cannot revoke.
D) He can revoke only if she delays beyond the session.

Key Takeaway
Delegation of self-divorce is a binding grant that cannot be revoked and is restricted to the session.

6 min
Revocable Delegation and No Session Limit for Third-Party Divorce
Ruling on revocation and session limitation for delegation to divorce a third party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish delegation to divorce a third party from self-divorce delegation, and apply the Hanafi ruling that such delegation is revocable and not session-limited.

Core Idea
Delegation to divorce a co-wife or another man's wife is a simple agency, revocable and not confined to the session.

What if a husband tells his wife to divorce her co-wife, or tells another man to divorce his wife? These are not grants of possession but mere authorizations. The delegator can revoke, and the agent can act even later. This lesson explores the reasoning and contrast.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the scenarios for third-party delegation
Two scenarios: (1) husband tells his wife to divorce her co-wife; (2) a man tells another man to divorce his wife. Both are delegations to affect a marriage not involving the agent's own marriage.
These are not tafwid but wakalah.
2
Understand the legal nature as wakalah (agency)
The husband authorizes the agent to perform a legal act on his behalf. This is a revocable agency, not a transfer of ownership.
Agency can be terminated by the principal at any time before execution.
3
Note the consequences: revocation and no session limit
The delegator can revoke before the agent acts, and the agent can act even after the session ends. The session limitation does not apply because there is no transfer of immediate possession.
The 'session' concept only applies to tamlik/tafwid.
4
Compare with self-divorce delegation
In self-divorce, the delegation directly grants the wife ownership of the right (tamlik), so it is binding and session-limited. In third-party delegation, the husband retains control, so it is revocable and not session-limited.
The key difference is whether the right is transferred or merely authorized.

رجوع (ruju'): Revocation; the principal's ability to cancel the agency.

Why 'I Choose Myself' Does Not Divorce
Ruling that 'اخترت نفسي' is not a word of divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the phrase 'اخترت نفسي' and explain why it is not a valid divorce word based on the requirement that divorce words be lexically or customarily indicative.

Core Idea
Divorce is only effective by words that indicate it by lexical sense or custom—'I choose myself' does neither.

When a husband delegates the choice of divorce to his wife, her reply 'I choose myself' might seem decisive. Yet the Hanafi school rules that no divorce occurs. Why? The phrase simply does not belong to the class of words that can effect divorce. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and customary criteria that govern valid divorce wording.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the wife saying 'اخترت نفسي' after delegation. The ruling: no divorce occurs (la yaka').
The matn states this clearly.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentary gives the proof: divorce is only effected by words that indicate it either by lexical usage (wad‘) or by custom (‘urf).
This is a qiyas on the general principle of valid divorce words.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'اخترت' has no lexical meaning related to divorce (unlike 'طلقت'), and no customary usage for initiating divorce (unlike 'أبنت' or 'حرمت'). It is only used as a response to delegation, not as a pronouncement.
The absence of both wad‘ and ‘urf means the phrase is unsuitable for īqā‘.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools may treat 'اخترت' as a kināyah that requires intention. In Hanafi law, even with intention it does not work because it is not fit for the purpose.
The dispute highlights the strict Hanafi requirement that the word itself must be capable of signifying divorce.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that no divorce occurs. This is established in Zahir al-Riwayah.
The tarjih is based on the principle that words not wad‘ī or ‘urfī for divorce are ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وباخترتُ نفسي لا يقع)؛ لأنه ليس من ألفاظ الطلاق. قوله: لأنه ليس من ألفاظ الطلاق؛ ولذا لا يقع شيء إذا قالت ابتداء: اخترت نفسي، وأجاز الزوج، قال شارح «تلخيص الجامع الكبير»: لأن هذا اللفظ لا يصلح للإيقاع؛ لأنّ إيقاع الطلاق إنما يكون بما يدل عليه، وضعاً، كما في: طلقت، أو عرفاً كما في: أبنت وحرمت، فإنّ إيقاع الطلاق بالإبانة أو التحريم عند النية متعارف، ولا وضع في اخترت أصلاً، ولا عرف في إيقاع الطلاق به إلا جواباً للتفويض.
(And if she says “I choose myself,” no divorce occurs) because it is not from the words of divorce. He said: because it is not from the words of divorce; hence nothing occurs if she says initially “I choose myself” and the husband authorizes. The commentator of “Talkhis al-Jami’ al-Kabir” said: because this word is not suitable for pronouncement of divorce, for the pronouncement of divorce is only by what indicates it, either by lexical usage, as in “I divorce,” or by customary usage, as in “I separate” and “I forbid,” for the pronouncement of divorce by separation or forbiddance with intention is customary, and there is no lexical usage for “I choose” at all, nor customary usage for pronouncing divorce by it except as a reply to delegation.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This is a ruling statement with a proof based on the principles of Hanafi fiqh. The base matn states that if a wife says “I choose myself” after delegation, no divorce occurs. The commentary explains the reasoning: the phrase “I choose myself” is not a word of divorce. For divorce to be effective, the wording must be either lexically indicative (like “I divorce you”) or customarily indicative (like “I separate” or “I forbid”) when intended. The word “اخترت” is neither lexically nor customarily used for divorce except as a response to delegation, where it indicates acceptance of the authority, not divorce itself. Therefore, when a wife says it, even with the husband’s authorization, no divorce takes place.

Key Terms
اخترت (ikhtartu): I choose/I opt; in this context, it is a response to delegation indicating the wife's selection of divorce, but it does not itself effect divorce.
إيقاع الطلاق (īqā‘ al-ṭalāq): The act of pronouncing and effecting a divorce; requires a word that is inherently or customarily indicative of divorce.
وضع (waḍ‘): Lexical or conventional meaning; a word is wad‘ī if it is linguistically assigned to a specific meaning, like 'طلق' for divorce.
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of the right to divorce from husband to wife. The wife's reply 'اخترت' is a response to tafwīḍ, not an initiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'اخترت نفسك' (choose yourself), and she replies 'اخترت نفسي', does divorce occur according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) Yes, because delegation gives her the power to divorce.
B) No, because 'اخترت' is not a word of divorce.
C) Yes, because she has exercised her choice.
D) No, because she must say 'طلقت نفسي' explicitly.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'اخترت نفسي' does not effect a divorce in Hanafi fiqh because it is neither lexically nor customarily a word of divorce.

Retraction and Session in Tafwīḍ vs. Tawkīl
Distinction between delegation and agency regarding retraction and session restriction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between delegation (tafwīḍ) and agency (tawkīl) in talaq by applying the Hanafi rules on retraction validity and session restriction.

Core Idea
Delegation to divorce oneself is binding and session-bound; agency to divorce another is revocable and not session-bound.

This lesson examines a key distinction in Hanafi talaq law: when a husband says 'divorce yourself' versus 'divorce your co-wife' or 'divorce my wife' to a third party. The legal consequences — whether the husband can revoke and whether the act is limited to the session — differ fundamentally because the first is a binding delegation (tafwīḍ) akin to an oath, while the latter is a revocable agency (tawkīl). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying rulings on talaq by delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage contrasts two types of address: (i) tafwīḍ (e.g., 'divorce yourself') and (ii) tawkīl (e.g., 'divorce your co-wife' or 'divorce my wife').
Delegation applies to the wife herself; agency applies to a third party or for another wife.
2
Extract the two rulings for each
For tafwīḍ: retraction is invalid and the delegation is restricted to the session. For tawkīl: retraction is valid and it is not session-bound.
The session is the gathering where the statement was made.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning
Understand that tafwīḍ is treated as a suspension (ta‘līq) and transfer of ownership (tamlik), making it binding and session-restricted. Tawkīl is a mere agency that can be revoked.
Suspended contracts like 'if you enter the house, you are divorced' are irrevocable.
4
Verify the generality
The commentary states that these rulings apply to all forms of tafwīḍ (choice, command with hand), not only to the phrase 'divorce yourself'.
The same logic applies to 'your affair is in your hand'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ولا يصح الرجوع عن طلقي نفسك، ويتقيد بالمجلس، وفي: طلقي ضرتك، وطلق امرأتي خلافهما: أي يصح عنه الرجوع، ولا يتقيد بالمجلس). قوله: ولا يصح الرجوع... الخ؛ هذان الحكمان ليسا بخاصتين بألفاظ المشيئة كما يوهم تخصيص طلقي نفسك بالذكر، بل هما يعمّان جميع أنواع التفويض من التخيير والأمر باليد، ووجه عدم صحة رجوع الزوج عنها على ما في «الفتح» وغيره: إن التفويض تعليق معنى، فإن قوله: طلقي نفسك مثلاً، تعليق للطلاق بتطليقها، وقس عليه: أمرك بيدك، والتعليقات عقود لازمة لا يصح الرجوع عنها، كقوله: إن دخلت الدار فأنت طالق، وأيضاً التفويض تمليك، فإنّ الزوج يُملكُ زوجته بتفويضه، والتمليك لازم لا يصح الرجوع عنه إذا تم بالملك وحده بلا توقف على قبول الآخر، ووجه التقيد بالمجلس أنّ في التفويض بأنواعه الثلاثة معنى التمليك، والتمليك يقتصر على المجلس. قوله: وفي طلقي ضرتك؛ يعني لو قال لامرأته: طلقي ضرتك؛ أي امرأتي الأخرى، أو قال الرجل: طلق امرأتي، يصح الرجوع عنه قبل إيقاع الوكيل الطلاق ولا يتقيّد بالمجلس، فلها أن تطلق ضرتها بعد انقضاء المجلس، وله أن يطلق امرأة المفوّض بعده. لأن طلقي نفسك ليس بتوكيل، بل هو يمين، لأنه تعليق الطلاق بتطليقها، واليمين تصرف لازم، فلا يقبلُ الرُّجوع، ثُمَّ هو تمليك؛ لأنها تعمل لنفسها، فيتقيد بالمجلس، وأما طلّقي ضرتك، وطلق امرأتي، فتوكيل، فيقبلُ الرجوع، ولا يتقيّد بالمجلس.
(And retraction from “divorce yourself” is not valid, and it is restricted to the session, while in “divorce your co-wife” and “divorce my wife” the opposite of these two: i.e., retraction from them is valid and they are not restricted to the session). He said: retraction is not valid... These two rulings are not specific to the words of will as the specification of “divorce yourself” might suggest, but they apply to all types of delegation: choice, command with the hand. The reason that retraction by the husband is not valid according to “al-Fath” and others is that delegation is a suspended meaning; for example, his saying “divorce yourself” suspends divorce upon her divorcing, and analogously “your affair is in your hand”, and suspended contracts are binding contracts from which retraction is not valid, like saying “if you enter the house, you are divorced”. Also, delegation is a transfer of ownership; the husband transfers ownership to his wife by his delegation, and ownership transfer is binding and retraction is not valid when it is completed by the ownership alone without requiring the acceptance of the other. The reason for restriction to the session is that in the three types of delegation there is the meaning of ownership, and ownership is restricted to the session. He said: and in “divorce your co-wife,” meaning if he said to his wife “divorce your co-wife,” i.e., my other wife, or the man said “divorce my wife,” retraction is valid before the agent pronounces divorce, and it is not restricted to the session. So she may divorce her co-wife after the session ends, and he may divorce the wife of the delegator after it. Because “divorce yourself” is not agency; rather, it is an oath, because it is a suspension of divorce upon her divorcing, and an oath is a binding action, so it does not accept retraction. Then it is a transfer of ownership because she acts for herself, so it is restricted to the session. As for “divorce your co-wife” and “divorce my wife,” these are agency, so retraction is accepted and they are not restricted to the session.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage contrasts two categories: delegation (tafwīḍ) and agency (tawkīl) in the context of talaq. The matn states that from “divorce yourself” retraction is invalid and it is restricted to the session, while from “divorce your co-wife” and “divorce my wife” retraction is valid and not session-bound. The commentary explains the reasoning: “divorce yourself” (and similar tafwīḍ phrases) are not agency; they are a suspension (ta‘līq) and a transfer of ownership (tamlik). A suspended contract is binding, and ownership transfer, once completed without acceptance, is irrevocable. The session restriction arises because the ownership is considered to be transferred only within the session. In contrast, “divorce your co-wife” and “divorce my wife” are agency: the woman acts as an agent for the husband, not for herself. Agency can be revoked and is not restricted to the session. A key point is that delegation (tafwīḍ) is like an oath (yamīn) because it strengthens the husband’s resolve and is a form of commitment.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation by the husband to the wife to pronounce divorce upon herself; treated as a suspension and ownership transfer.
توكيل (tawkīl): Agency; the husband appoints the wife (or someone else) as his agent to divorce another wife, or he appoints a third party to divorce his wife.
تعليق (ta‘līq): Suspension of divorce upon a condition; in delegation, the divorce is suspended upon the wife’s act of divorcing herself.
تمليك (tamlik): Transfer of ownership; the husband gives the wife the right to divorce herself, which is considered a transfer of the power of divorce.
يمين (yamīn): Oath; the commentary states that delegation is like an oath because it strengthens commitment and is binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'طلقي نفسك'. Later, before she does anything, he wants to revoke it. According to Hanafi fiqh, can he revoke?
A) Yes, because it is a form of agency.
B) No, because delegation is binding like an oath.
C) Yes, as long as he revokes within the session.
D) No, because the wife has accepted.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, delegation (tafwīḍ) to the wife to divorce herself is binding and session-restricted, whereas agency (tawkīl) to divorce another is revocable and not session-bound.

The Phrase 'متى شئت' Removes Session Restriction in Talaq Delegation
Ruling on a delegation formula with time qualifier

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'طلقي نفسك متى شئت' and explain why it removes the session restriction using the analogy to absolute agency.

Core Idea
Adding 'whenever you wish' transforms a session-bound tamlik into an unrestricted absolute agency.

In Hanafi fiqh, a husband may delegate the power of divorce to his wife using specific formulas. The default rule is that such delegation (tamlik) is tied to the session in which it is spoken — the wife must accept or exercise it immediately. However, the classical Hanafi masters identified an exception: when the husband adds the phrase 'متى شئت' (whenever you wish), the session restriction is lifted. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning behind this exception through a brief passage from the Hanafi commentary tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Delegation Formulas
The passage contrasts two formulas: simple 'طلقي نفسك' (divorce yourself) which is session-bound, and 'طلقي نفسك متى شئت' which is not. Recognize that the addition of 'متى شئت' is the key variable.
2
Understand the Legal Basis for Session Restriction
The default restriction is because simple delegation is considered a transfer of ownership (tamlik), which requires immediacy to be legally sound. The session is the timeframe for acceptance.
Tamlik resembles a gift that must be accepted on the spot.
3
Analyze How 'متى شئت' Changes the Nature
The phrase 'متى شئت' gives the wife the choice of time. Her will (mashī’ah) expands the permissible period indefinitely. This shifts the legal character from tamlik to absolute agency (tawkīl muṭlaq).
Agency (wakāla) is not restricted to the session unless specified.
4
Derive the Ruling via Qiyas
The Hanafi jurists analogize this qualified delegation to absolute agency. Just as an agent with absolute authority may act anytime, the wife may divorce herself at any future time. The ruling: the delegation is valid and not session-bound.
Qiyas is used because there is no explicit nass on this specific wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وفي: طلقي نفسك متى شئت لا يتقيد به). قوله: لا يتقيد به؛ الحاصل أن صيغ التفويض التي تتقيد بالمجلس؛ لكونه تمليكاً، وإذا قيل: طلقي نفسك متى شئت، فلم يتقيد؛ لأن مشيئتها وسعت الوقت، فكان كالتوكيل المطلق.
(And in “divorce yourself whenever you wish,” it is not restricted to the session). He said: it is not restricted to it; the summary is that the formulas of delegation that are restricted to the session are because they are a transfer of ownership, but when it is said “divorce yourself whenever you wish,” it is not restricted because her will expands the time, so it is like absolute agency.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This is a ruling that modifies the general principle. The matn states that if the husband says 'divorce yourself whenever you wish,' the authority is not limited to the session of delegation. The commentary explains why: the usual delegation (like 'divorce yourself' without a time qualifier) is restricted to the session because it is a transfer of ownership (tamlik) that is considered complete only within that session. However, when the phrase 'whenever you wish' is added, the wife's choice (mashī’ah) is given a wider timeframe—it expands the time, effectively making the authority similar to an absolute agency (tawkīl muṭlaq). An absolute agency is not restricted to the session; it remains valid until revoked or the task is performed. Thus, she may divorce herself at any future time.

Key Terms
متى شئت (matā shi’ta): Whenever you wish; this phrase expands the authority of the wife beyond the session.
مشيئة (mashī’ah): Will; the wife’s will or choice, which when explicitly mentioned, gives her the power to choose the time of divorce.
توكيل مطلق (tawkīl muṭlaq): Absolute agency; an agency without time or session restrictions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'طلقي نفسك متى شئت' (divorce yourself whenever you wish). Two days later, she says 'طلقت نفسي' (I divorce myself). Is this talaq valid according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) No, because delegation must be exercised immediately in the same session.
B) Yes, because adding 'متى شئت' removes the session restriction, so she can divorce later.
C) No, because she must have the husband's permission again.
D) Yes, but only if she says 'اخترت' instead.

Key Takeaway
The addition of 'متى شئت' transforms a session-bound tamlik into an unrestricted absolute agency, making the delegation valid at any future time.

Distinguishing Tamlik from Tawkil: The Hanafi Ruling on Delegated Divorce
Commentary explaining why 'Divorce yourself' is tamlik not tawkil and its session restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tamlik and tawkil in divorce delegation, state the Hanafi ruling on restriction to the session, and apply the rule to different verbal formulas.

Core Idea
When a husband tells his wife 'Divorce yourself,' she owns the divorce; it is tamlik not tawkil, binding and limited to the session unless time-general words are used.

This passage addresses a common scenario: a husband delegates authority to his wife to divorce herself. The Hanafi commentary clarifies that such a delegation is not agency (tawkil) but ownership (tamlik), making the wife the direct actor. This distinction has critical implications for whether the husband can revoke, whether the delegation is restricted to the session, and what happens when words like 'whenever' are used. Understanding this ruling is essential for correct fatwa in marriage and divorce matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its classification
The husband's statement 'طلقي نفسك' (Divorce yourself) is a delegation of authority to divorce. Determine whether it is tawkil (agency) or tamlik (ownership) by asking: who acts for whom? If the wife acts for herself, it is tamlik; if she acts for the husband, it is tawkil.
The commentary states: 'ليس بتوكيل لأن الوكيل من يعمل لغيره لا من يعمل لنفسه.'
2
Understand the Hanafi position: tamlik
According to the Hanafi school, the delegation is tamlik because the wife is acting for herself—she owns the right to divorce herself after the husband's statement. This makes the delegation binding (the husband cannot revoke) and restricted to the session (majlis) by default.
Contrast with tawkil: if the husband says 'Divorce my other wife,' it is tawkil because the wife is acting on the husband's behalf for another woman.
3
Examine the default restriction to the session
A tamlik delegation is restricted to the session in which it was made. The wife must exercise her right to divorce in that same gathering (majlis). If she divorces later, it is not effective unless the husband's words indicated generality of time.
The commentary says: 'لا يتقيد به' refers to the condition; but it is restricted to the session because it is tamlik.
4
Learn the effect of time-general vs. time-specific words
If the husband uses words indicating generality of time (متى 'whenever', كلما 'every time', etc.), the delegation is no longer restricted to the session. Words like إن 'if' or كيف 'how' do not extend beyond the session; they maintain the default restriction.
The commentary explicitly lists which words remove the restriction and which do not.
5
Recognize the oath (yamin) classification
Suspending divorce on a condition is considered a yamin (oath) because it strengthens the commitment. This is supported by a hadith: 'ملعون من حلف بالطلاق' (Cursed is one who swears by divorce). The classification as yamin reinforces the binding nature of such statements.
This classification explains why the husband cannot treat the delegation as a mere promise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : طلقى نفسك متى شئت لا يتقيد به. [١] قوله : لأن ... الخ ؛ بيان لتعليل المسألتين مع وجه الفرق بينهما، وحاصله : إن قوله لامرأته : طلقي نفسك، أو أمرك بيدك، أو اختاري ليس بتوكيل ؛ لأنّ الوكيل من يعمل لغيره لا من يعمل لنفسه، بل هو تعليق وتمليك، بخلاف: طلقي ضرتك أو طلقي امرأتي، فإنّه توكيل بإيقاع الطلاقِ على غير وكيل، والتوكيل لا يتقيد بالمجلس، ويصح الرجوع عنه. [٢]قوله : هو يمين ؛ قال خواجه مسعود في «شرح تلخيص الجامع الكبير»: اعلم أن تعليق الطلاق ونحوه يمين، وهذا لورود الشرع والعرف به ، قال : «ملعون من حلف بالطلاق» (١) ، ويقال : حلف فلان بالطلاق، كما يقال : حلف بالله له ، وإنما سُمّي التعليقُ يميناً ؛ لأنه يتقوى به وجود الفعل أو عدمه. (۱) واليمين : القوّة لغة، وينسب اليمين إلى الجزاء، فيقال : يمين بالطلاق، كما يقال : يمين بالله حالة ؛ لأنه مقسم به ، كاسم الله ؛ لأنَّ المقسم به ما يقصد بذكره تأكيد البر مراعاة لحرمته، وهاهنا كذلك ؛ لأنه يقصد بذكر الجزاء من الطلاق والعتاق تأكيد البر رعاية الحرمة النكاح والمال. [٣] قوله : لأنها ؛ أي المرأة التي فوّض الأمر إليها. [٤] قوله : ولا يتقيّد ... الخ ؛ إلا إذا تقيّدت الوكالة بشيء يقتصر على المجلس كمشيئتها ؛ فإنّ الوكالة إيضاح تتقيّد بالمجلس. كذا في «النهر» و«البحر». [0] قوله : لا يتقيد به ؛ الحاصلُ أنّ صيغ التفويض الذي يتقيد بالمجلس ؛ لكونه تمليكاً على ما ذكرنا تحقيقه إذا اقترنت بكلمةٍ تفيد عموم الوقت : كمتي، وإذا ما ، ومتى ما، وكلّما، وحين ما، وأي وقت ونحوها، فحينئذ لا يتقيد بالمجلس ؛ لوجود ما يدلّ على عموم الوقت. بخلاف: إنّ، وكيف، وحيث وكم، وأين ،وأينما، فإنّ هذه الألفاظ لا تدلّ على عموم الأوقات، وإن دلَّ بعضُها على عموم الأمكنة، فباقترانها لا يبطل ما هو مقتضى التمليك ؛ أي التقيد بالمجلس. أي بالمجلس،
And regarding: 'Divorce yourself whenever you wish' it is not restricted by it [the condition]. [1] His saying: 'because...etc.' is an explanation of the reasoning for the two issues and the aspect of difference between them. The summary: that a man's saying to his wife 'Divorce yourself', or 'Your affair is in your hand', or 'Choose' is not an agency (tawkil) because the agent acts for another, not for himself; rather it is a suspension and ownership (tamlik). Contrary to 'Divorce your co-wife' or 'Divorce my wife', for that is an agency to effect divorce on another, and agency is not restricted to the session and revocation of it is valid. [2] His saying: 'it is an oath' – Khwaja Mas'ud said in Sharh Talkhis al-Jami' al-Kabir: Know that suspension of divorce and similar is an oath, and this is due to the Shariah and custom stating it. He said: 'Cursed is one who swears by divorce.' And it is said: 'So-and-so swore by divorce' as it is said 'He swore by Allah'. It is only called an oath because the existence or non-existence of the act is strengthened by it. And the oath linguistically means strength. The oath is attributed to the consequence; one says 'an oath by divorce' as one says 'an oath by Allah' as a situation because it is sworn by, like the name of Allah. [3] His saying: 'because she' means the woman to whom the affair was delegated. [4] His saying: 'and it is not restricted...etc.' – except when the agency is restricted by something limited to the session like her wish; then the agency is clarified to be restricted to the session. Thus in al-Nahr and al-Bahr. [0] His saying: 'it is not restricted by it' – the summary: that the formulas of delegation are restricted to the session because it is ownership (tamlik) as we mentioned its verification. When it is accompanied by a word indicating generality of time, like 'whenever', 'if', 'whenever', 'every time', 'at the time', 'any time' and similar, then it is not restricted to the session due to the existence of what indicates generality of time. Contrary to 'if', 'how', 'where', 'as much', 'where', 'wherever' for these words do not indicate generality of times, even if some indicate generality of places; by their conjunction the requirement of ownership is not nullified, i.e., restriction to the session. That is, restricted to the session.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from a Hanafi sharh explaining the ruling when a husband tells his wife 'Divorce yourself whenever you wish.' The matn states it is not restricted by the condition, but the commentary clarifies that it is actually restricted to the session because it is considered tamlik (ownership/delegation of authority) rather than tawkil (agency). The key distinction: in tawkil, the agent acts for the principal; in tamlik, the woman acts for herself. Thus 'Divorce yourself' gives her ownership over the divorce, making it binding (no revocation by husband) and limited to the session unless words of time generality are used. The commentary also explains that such suspension is considered a yamin (oath) because it strengthens the commitment, supported by a hadith condemning swearing by divorce. Words like متى (whenever) or كلما (every time) make the delegation unrestricted in time, while words like إن (if) or كيف (how) do not.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of authority to the wife to divorce herself.
تمليك (tamlik): Ownership or empowerment; the wife owns the right to divorce.
توكيل (tawkil): Agency; one appoints another to act on his behalf.
يمين (yamin): An oath; suspension of divorce is considered an oath to strengthen commitment.
مجلس (majlis): The session in which the delegation occurs; the wife must divorce in that same session unless time-general words are used.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal nature of the husband saying to his wife 'طلقي نفسك' (Divorce yourself) according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) It is tawkil (agency), so the husband can revoke it.
B) It is tamlik (ownership), binding and restricted to the session.
C) It is a void statement because it is not explicit.
D) It is an oath that requires kaffarah if broken.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that 'Divorce yourself' is tamlik, binding and restricted to the session unless time-general words are used, because the wife acts for herself, not as an agent.
