Conditional Divorce Delegation: From Agency to Ownership (Tamlik)
Commentary on conditional divorce delegation to a third party

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband tells a third party 'Divorce her if you wish' and explain the legal reasoning that transforms it into tamlik.

Core Idea
When the husband conditions divorce on the agent's wish, the delegation becomes binding ownership (tamlik), restricted to the session.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between pure agency (tawkil) and ownership-like delegation (tamlik) in divorce matters. In this lesson, we examine a key scenario: the husband says to a third person 'Divorce her if you wish.' The matn declares this is restricted and cannot be revoked. The commentary reveals the legal reasoning behind this ruling, drawing an analogy to tamlik and clarifying the precise wording required for the divorce to take effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The husband's statement 'Divorce her if you wish' is a conditional delegation. According to the matn, it is restricted (to the session) and cannot be revoked.
2
Understand the transformation from tawkil to tamlik
Conditioning the divorce on the agent's wish changes the nature from agency to tamlik. The agent now acts based on his own wish, like an owner.
This is the key legal reasoning (talil).
3
Identify the key consequence of tamlik
Tamlik is binding and restricted to the session. The agent must act in the same session and cannot revoke later.
4
Distinguish between different phrasings and responses
If the husband says 'She is divorced if you wish' and the agent says 'I wished', divorce occurs. But if he says 'Divorce her if you wish', the agent must say 'I did' or similar, not just 'I wished'.
Also note: 'Divorce her if she wishes' makes the agent wait for her wish.
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Given a scenario, determine whether the divorce is valid based on the wording and timing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وفي : طلقها إن شئت يتقيد ولا يرجع) : [ ١ ] قوله : يتقيد ؛ لأن طلقها وإن كان توكيلاً بنفسه، لكن لما علقه بمشيئته صار في حكم التمليك، فإنّ المالك هو الذي يتصرّف بمشيئته ، فصار حكمه حكم التمليك من التقيد بالمجلس، ، وعدم صـ صحة الرجوع . عنه كما مر. [٢] قوله : يتقيد ... الخ ؛ وكذا لو قال له : طلقها إن شاءت، فلا يصير وكيلاً ما لم تشأ ، فإن شاءت في مجلس علمها طلقها في مجلسه لا غير. كذا في «الخانية». [٣] قوله : لأنه علقه ؛ أي الزوج عَلّق تطليقها بمشيئة الوكيل، فصار في معنى التمليكات لا توكيلاً محضاً، فلو قال في جوابه شئت، لا يقع الطلاق ؛ لأنَّ الزوج أمره بتطليقها إن شاء، ولم يوجد التطليق بقوله : شئت. ولو قال الزوج : هي طالق إن شئت فقال : شئت وقع لوجود الشرط، ولو قال : طلقها إن شئت، فقال : فعلتُ وقـع ؛ لأنه كناية عن قوله : طلقت. كذا في «المحيط».
(And regarding: 'Divorce her if you wish' it is restricted and cannot be revoked). [1] His saying: 'it is restricted' because 'divorce her' is by itself agency, but when he conditioned it on his wish it becomes in the ruling of tamlik (ownership), for the owner is the one who acts according to his wish. So its ruling becomes the ruling of tamlik in terms of restriction to the session and invalidity of revocation from it, as passed. [2] His saying: 'it is restricted...etc.' – similarly if he said to him: 'Divorce her if she wishes', then he does not become an agent until she wishes. If she wishes in the session of his knowledge, he divorces her in that session only. Thus in al-Khaniyyah. [3] His saying: 'because he conditioned it' – i.e., the husband conditioned the divorce on the agent's wish, so it becomes in the meaning of tamlikat, not pure agency. So if he says in response 'I wished', the divorce does not occur because the husband ordered him to divorce her if he wishes, and divorce did not occur by his saying 'I wished'. And if the husband says 'She is divorced if you wish', and he says 'I wished', it occurs due to the existence of the condition. And if he says 'Divorce her if you wish', and he says 'I did', it occurs because it is a kinayah (allusion) for his saying 'I divorced'. Thus in al-Muhit.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on divorce delegation, now addressing a scenario where the husband tells a third party 'Divorce her if you wish' (طلقها إن شئت). The matn states it is restricted (to the session) and cannot be revoked. The commentary explains that although the basic command 'Divorce her' is tawkil (agency), conditioning it on the agent's wish transforms it into tamlik (ownership-like), because the agent now acts according to his own wish, similar to the owner. Consequently, it becomes binding and restricted to the session. The commentary further distinguishes between different phrasings: if the husband says 'Divorce her if she wishes' (إن شاءت), the agent does not become an agent until she wishes, and then he must divorce in that same session. If the husband says 'She is divorced if you wish' (هي طالق إن شئت) and the agent says 'I wished', the divorce occurs because the condition is fulfilled. But if the husband says 'Divorce her if you wish' (طلقها إن شئت) and the agent says 'I wished', the divorce does not occur because 'I wished' is not the divorce itself; rather the agent must say 'I did' (فعلت) as a kinayah for 'I divorced'.

Key Terms
تمليكات (tamlikat): Matters of ownership; here the conditional delegation is treated like tamlik.
كناية (kinayah): An indirect expression that implies divorce; here 'فعلت' (I did) is a kinayah for 'I divorced'.
توكيل (tawkil): Agency; here distinguished from tamlik in terms of revocability and session restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi passage, if the husband says 'Divorce her if you wish' and the agent says 'I wished', what is the ruling?
A) The divorce occurs because the condition is fulfilled.
B) The divorce does not occur because 'I wished' is not the divorce pronouncement.
C) The divorce occurs but is revocable.
D) The delegation becomes invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, 'طلقها إن شئت' transforms conditional agency into tamlik, making the divorce binding and restricted to the session, and requiring the agent to actually perform the divorce (not just express wish).

Delegation of Divorce: The Ruling on 'If You Wish'
Ruling with proof on delegation of divorce with conditional wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a husband delegates divorce to a third party using the phrase 'if you wish', and distinguish between tamlik and tawkil.

Core Idea
When the husband says 'divorce if you wish', the delegation becomes tamlik — tied to the session and irrevocable.

In Hanafi fiqh, the wording of delegation matters. A simple agency (tawkil) is not tied to the session, but when the husband makes the divorce conditional on the agent's will, it transforms into a conferral of ownership (tamlik). This lesson explains the reasoning behind this rule and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and the issue
The husband says to a third party: 'Divorce my wife if you wish.' What is the legal effect?
Focus on the conditional wording 'if you wish'.
2
Recognize the default
Unconditional agency (tawkil) is not session-bound and can be revoked by the husband.
This is the baseline for comparison.
3
Identify the transformative condition
The phrase 'if you wish' attaches the divorce to the agent's will, changing the nature of delegation.
The condition transforms tawkil into tamlik.
4
Apply the talil (reasoning)
Because the agent acts by his own will, he is like an owner. In Hanafi law, tamlik ties the authority to the session and makes it irrevocable.
The ruling is derived by analogy to the case where the husband tells his wife 'divorce yourself'.
5
Conclude the ruling
The agent's authority is confined to the session and the husband cannot revoke it. The agent must actually pronounce the divorce for it to take effect.
Simply saying 'I wished' is insufficient.

Source Text

And in it: 'Divorce her if you wish' is tied (to the session) and cannot be revoked. i.e., if he said to someone: 'Divorce my wife if you wish', it is tied to the session because he attached it to his will, so it becomes a conferral of ownership (tamlik) not agency (tawkil), so it is tied to the session and cannot be revoked from it as in 'divorce yourself'. (Likewise if he said to him: 'Divorce her if she wishes', he does not become an agent unless she wishes; if she wishes in his council of knowledge, he divorces her in his council only. Thus in al-Khaniyah.) He said: 'is tied' because the divorce, even if it is agency in itself, but when he attached it to his will it becomes in the ruling of conferral of ownership; for the owner is the one who acts at his will, so its ruling became the ruling of conferral of ownership in being tied to the session and lack of validity of revocation from it as passed. He said: 'is tied... etc. ; likewise if he said to him: 'Divorce her if she wishes', he does not become an agent unless she wishes; if she wishes in his council of knowledge, he divorces her in his council only. Thus in al-Khaniyah. He said: 'because he attached it'; meaning the husband attached the divorce to the will of the agent, so it became in the meaning of conferrals of ownership, not pure agency. Thus in al-Muhit.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof (talil) explaining the legal effect of a husband's statement to a third party: 'Divorce my wife if you wish.' The commentator clarifies that the phrase 'is tied' means the delegation is confined to the immediate session of the utterance and cannot be later revoked by the husband. The reasoning (talil) is that by attaching the divorce to the will of the agent, the husband has effectively turned the agency (tawkil) into a conferral of ownership (tamlik). In Hanafi law, an owner acts by his own will and is limited to the session; similarly, the agent now acts as an owner. The passage also distinguishes between this case and the case where the husband directly says 'She is divorced if you wish', where saying 'I wished' triggers the divorce because the condition is fulfilled. However, in the delegated case, the agent must actually perform the divorce; saying 'I wished' alone is insufficient. The text references authoritative Hanafi works: al-Khaniyah (a fatwa collection) and al-Muhit (a commentary). The authority weight here is from later fatwa collections (mutakhkhirin), not the core Zahir al-Riwayah. The Hanafi position is clear: the delegation is binding and limited to the session.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of the power to divorce to the wife or a third party.
تمليك (tamlīk): Conferral of ownership; the wife or agent owns the power to divorce.
توكيل (tawkīl): Agency; the agent acts on behalf of the husband without owning the power.

D) The divorce occurs automatically if the agent says 'I wished'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband delegates divorce with the condition 'if you wish', it becomes a tamlik that is tied to the session and cannot be revoked, and the agent must actually pronounce the divorce.

Delegated Divorce: Deviating from the Commanded Number
Ruling and intra-Hanafi dispute on deviating from the number commanded in delegated divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife divorces less or more than the number commanded in delegated divorce, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute over exceeding the number.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, doing less is valid; doing more is disputed: Abu Hanifa says nothing falls, Sahibayn say one falls.

This lesson explores a nuanced area of Hanafi family law: when a husband delegates the right to divorce to his wife by specifying a number (e.g., 'divorce yourself once'), what happens if she pronounces a different number? The agreed rule is that if she divorces fewer than the commanded number, that lesser number falls. But if she exceeds the commanded number, the Hanafi imams differ. This intra-madhhab dispute hinges on whether the excess is considered a mere addition or a contradictory act.

Guided Path
1
Understand the agreed ruling on doing less
The passage first states that if the husband commands a number and the wife divorces less, that lesser number falls. The reason is that owning the maximum includes owning what is less. For example, if he says 'Divorce yourself three' and she does one, one divorce occurs.
This is uncontroversial within the school.
2
Identify the disputed case: doing more
The controversy arises when the wife exceeds the commanded number. For instance, he says 'divorce yourself one' and she pronounces three. Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn give different rulings.
Focus on the scenario with 'one' vs 'three'.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's argument
Abu Hanifa holds that no divorce falls because the delegation was specifically for one divorce. Three is not an increase of one but a distinct, composite number. Performing three is an act contrary to the delegation, not a performance of it.
He distinguishes between 'excess' (ziyada) and 'opposite' (didd).
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's argument
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad analogize to the case where a husband divorces more than three. There, only three fall and the excess is void. Similarly, the wife performed what she owned (one) plus an increase (two), so one falls and the extra two are void.
This qiyas treats the wife's action as the commanded act with an invalid addition.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa the marriage remains intact; under the Sahibayn one divorce occurs (revocable if it was a first or second). Later Hanafi fatwa often prefers the Sahibayn's view to avoid the risk of no divorce at all.
The passage does not explicitly state the mufta bihi, but later practice leans toward the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ولو أمرت بالبائن (۳) ، أو الرجعي (4) فعكست (٥) ، فعكست (٥) ، يقع ما أمر به (۱). وبه علم أنه لو طلقت ثلاثاً في صورة تفويض الثلاث يقع الثلاث بالطريق الأولى، سواء كانت متفرّقة أو بلفظ واحد. His statement: 'one'; and likewise if she divorced two, and likewise if he said: 'Divorce yourself two' and she divorced one. In summary: when the husband restricts his speech by a number and she divorces less than it, that lesser number occurs. That is because when she owned the occurrence of three for example, she owned the occurrence of what is less than it, so she may pronounce whatever she wishes, either according to the number mentioned by the delegator, or less than it. 'One, and nothing falls in its opposite': i.e., if he said to her: 'Divorce yourself one' and she divorced three, nothing falls according to Abu Hanifa; because he delegated to her the occurrence of one intentionally, not within three, and according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) one falls. Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of deviating from the commanded number in delegated divorce. The text first states the agreed ruling: if the husband commands 'Divorce yourself three' and she divorces one (or two), that lesser number falls because she owns all numbers up to three. The controversy arises in the opposite case: if he commands 'Divorce yourself one' and she divorces three. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, nothing falls because she was delegated specifically to perform one divorce, not three; by doing three, she did something contrary to the delegation. However, according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), one divorce falls because she performed what was delegated along with an additional two, which are void by analogy to a husband divorcing more than three. The commentary provides the reasoning: the Sahibayn analogize to the husband who divorces four times — only three fall. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: three is a composite number, fundamentally different from one; performing three is an act that contradicts the delegation. Thus, no divorce falls. The passage also notes that if she was delegated three and she divorces three, it falls by primary reasoning. The authority weight is from the Zahir al-Riwayah insofar as the dispute is recorded, but the mufta bihi position is not explicitly stated here.

Key Terms
عدد (ʿadad): Number of divorces delegated, e.g., one, two, or three.
زيادة (ziyāda): Excess number of divorces beyond what was commanded.
ضد (ḍidd): Opposite or contradictory; used by Abu Hanifa to argue that three is the opposite of one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Three divorces fall.
B) One divorce falls.
C) No divorce falls.
D) The marriage is annulled.

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, doing less than commanded is valid, but doing more is disputed: Abu Hanifa holds nothing falls, the Sahibayn hold the commanded number falls; later fatwa often adopts the Sahibayn's view.

Extra Description Void in Delegated Divorce
Ruling on reversal of type in delegated divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife reverses the type of delegated divorce and explain why the extra description is void.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, an added description contradicting the command is void; the original commanded divorce falls.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq) where the husband commands a revocable divorce but the wife pronounces an irrevocable one. The Hanafi ruling, grounded in the principle that an extra description (waṣf) that goes beyond the command is disregarded, is applied to affirm the commanded divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's command
The husband says: 'Divorce yourself one raj'i divorce.' The command includes both the number and the type.
The command is clear: one revocable divorce.
2
Identify the wife's response
The wife says: 'I divorce myself one bain divorce.' She changes the type from raj'i to bain.
She performs the divorce but alters the description.
3
Isolate the core and the description
The core is the act of divorce itself; the type (raj'i or bain) is a description attached to it.
The wife performed the core act (one divorce) but added a different description.
4
Apply the Hanafi principle
When a commanded act is performed with an extra description that contradicts the command, the description is void (laghw) and the original commanded act remains valid.
This principle ensures that the husband's command is not nullified by the wife's addition.
5
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, a raj'i divorce falls, not a bain. The wife's act is valid as commanded, and the extra description is ignored.
The ruling is sahih (valid) and the commanded type takes effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣]قوله : وقع ما أمر به الزوج ; فلو قال : طلّقي نفسك واحدة رجعية، فقالت : طلقت نفسي واحدة بائنة، تقع رجعيّة ; لأنها أتت بالأصل مع زيادة وصف، فيلغو الوصف ويبقى الأصل. ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر» (١ : ٤١٤).
[3] His statement: 'What the husband commanded falls'; so if he said: 'Divorce yourself one raj'i' and she said: 'I divorced myself one bain', a raj'i falls because she came with the original (divorce) with an increase in description, so the description is void and the original remains. See: Majma' al-Anhur (1:414).
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with reasoning (talil) regarding the reversal of the type of divorce (bain vs raj'i) in a delegated divorce. The base ruling from the matn states: if the husband commands a specific type (e.g., one raj'i divorce) and the wife performs the reverse (e.g., one bain), what the husband commanded falls. The commentator explains that the wife has performed the essential act (one divorce) while adding an extra description (bain instead of raj'i). In Hanafi law, when a description is added to what was commanded, the description is void and the original commanded act remains valid. Therefore, a raj'i divorce falls. The authority referenced is Majma' al-Anhur, a later Hanafi fiqh work, so the weight is from mutakhkhirin sources. The ruling is agreed upon within the Hanafi school; no dispute is mentioned.

Key Terms
بائن (bāʾin): Irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage.
رجعي (rajʿī): Revocable divorce; the husband can return to his wife during the waiting period without a new contract.
وصف (waṣf): Description or attribute (e.g., bain or raj'i) that can be voided if added beyond command.

Delegated Divorce: When the Wife Exceeds the Number
Explanation of the ruling when wife exceeds delegated number of divorces

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on delegated divorce when the wife exceeds the number, and evaluate the reasoning of Abu Hanifa versus the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
When a wife exceeds the delegated number of divorces, the Hanafi masters differ: only if the delegated number is conceptually present does it fall.

Delegated divorce (tafwid) is a unique institution where the husband authorizes his wife to pronounce divorce. But what happens when she goes beyond what he authorized? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two chief students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on whether any divorce occurs when the wife gives three when told one. Understanding this disagreement reveals deep principles about intention, agency, and the nature of numbers in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and positions
The husband delegates one divorce to the wife. She gives three. There are two positions: Abu Hanifa says nothing occurs; Sahibayn say one occurs.
The passage also covers the opposite case (bain vs raj'i) where the type is reversed, but that is less contested.
2
Explain Abu Hanifa's reasoning
Abu Hanifa focuses on the husband's intention (qasd): he delegated the intention of one divorce, not the one that might be included within three. The wife's act of giving three is the opposite of one, so nothing occurs.
Key term: 'qasd al-wahidah la fi dhimn al-thalath'.
3
Explain the Sahibayn's reasoning
They analogize to the case where a husband divorces more than three: three fall, increase is null. Here the wife owned one, and by giving three she included one, so one falls and the increase is null.
Their dalil is qiyas on the husband's divorce of excess.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's response
Three is a composite number, one is simple. Giving three is not an act of giving one with excess; it is a fundamentally different act. Analogies fail because the husband's case involves ownership of three, not one.
This highlights the importance of the nature of the delegated authority.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, no divorce occurs; marriage continues. Under Sahibayn, one revocable divorce occurs; husband may take her back during idda.
Later Hanafi practice often follows Sahibayn, but this passage supports Abu Hanifa.

Source Text

فواحدة، ولا يقع شيءٌ في عكسه ، ولو أمرت بالبائن، أو الرجعي فعكست ، يقع ما أمر به. فواحدة) ، ولا يقع شيءٌ في عكسه ) : أي لو قال لها : طلقي نفسك واحدة، فطلقت ثلاثاً، لا يقع شي (٢) عند أبي حنيفة ؛ لأنه فوض إليها إيقاع الواحدة قصدا ، لا في ضمن الثَّلاث، وعندهما تقع" واحدة. (ولو أمرت بالبائن (۳) ، أو الرجعي (4) فعكست (٥) ، فعكست (٥) ، يقع ما أمر به (۱). وبه علم أنه لو طلقت ثلاثاً في صورة تفويض الثلاث يقع الثلاث بالطريق الأولى، سواء كانت متفرّقة أو بلفظ واحد. [١] قوله : لأنه فوّض ؛ هو بصيغة المعروفِ من التفويض ، ففاعله الضمير الراجع إلى الزوج، وإليه يرجع ضمير ؛ لأنه ومفعوله إيقاعُ الواحدة، ويحتمل أن يكون بصيغة المجهول، فإيقاعُ الواحدة نائب مناب فاعله، وضمير «لأنه» للشأن.
[٢]قوله: تقع واحدة ؛ لأنها أتت بما ملكته مع زيادة، فتلغو الزيادة، ويقع ما فوّضه إليها، ونظيره ما إذا طلق الزوج أزيد من الثلاث، فإنّه يقع الثلاث وتلغو الزيادة. والجواب عنه من قبل أبي حنيفة الله : أن الثلاثَ عدد مغاير للواحد، فإنّه عدد مركب من ثلاث وحدات، ولا تركيب في الواحد، فكان إتيانها بالزيادة إتياناً بما يضاد ما فوضه إليها فلا يقعُ شيء، بخلاف المسألة الأولى، فإنّ هناك ملكت الثلاث، والواحد موجود في الثلاث، أما هاهنا لم تملك الثلاث، وما أتت بما فوّض إليها.
One, and nothing occurs in its opposite. And if she was commanded to give a revocable (birraj'i) and she reversed, what was commanded occurs. (One) and nothing occurs in its opposite: meaning if he said to her: 'Divorce yourself once' and she gave three divorces, nothing occurs according to Abu Hanifa because he delegated to her the intention (qasd) of one divorce, not within the three. And according to them (the Sahibayn) one divorce occurs. (And if she was commanded to give an irrevocable (ba'in) or revocable (raj'i) and she reversed, what was commanded occurs). And from this it is known that if she gave three divorces in the case of delegation of three, the three occur by primary precedence, whether they were separated or with one utterance. [1] His saying: 'because he delegated' is in the active voice of tafwid, so its agent is the pronoun referring to the husband, and the pronoun 'because he' refers to him; its object is the occurrence of one. And it is possible it is in the passive voice, so 'occurrence of one' stands in place of its agent, and the pronoun 'because he' is for the matter. [2] His saying: 'one occurs' because she performed what she owned with an increase, so the increase is nullified, and what he delegated to her occurs. And its analogy is when the husband divorces more than three, then three occur and the increase is nullified. The response from Abu Hanifa is that three is a number different from one, for it is a number composed of three units, and there is no composition in one. So her coming with the increase is coming with what opposes what he delegated to her, so nothing occurs. Contrary to the first case: there she owned the three, and the one exists in the three. But here she does not own the three, and she did not perform what he delegated to her.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning delegated divorce (tafwid). The base text (matn) states two rulings: (1) If a husband tells his wife 'divorce yourself once' and she gives three divorces, nothing occurs according to Abu Hanifa, but according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) one divorce occurs. (2) If he tells her to give a revocable (raj'i) divorce and she gives an irrevocable (ba'in) one, or vice versa, what he commanded occurs. The commentary first explains the dispute on the number. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the husband delegated the intention (qasd) of one divorce, not the one that is included within three. The wife, by giving three, did not perform the delegated act but its opposite, so nothing falls. The Sahibayn analogize to the case where the husband himself divorces more than three: three fall and the increase is null. The commentator (the author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya) then refutes this analogy: three is a completely different number from one, so giving three does not include the delegated one. He distinguishes this from the case where the husband delegated three and she gave three – there it occurs because she owned the three and performed exactly what was delegated. It also notes the corollary: if the husband delegated three divorces and she gave three, they occur by primary precedence, whether separated or in one utterance. The footnotes clarify grammatical points about the verb 'fawwada'.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): The act of a husband delegating the power of divorce to his wife, giving her the authority to pronounce talaq on herself.
بائن (bāʾin): An irrevocable divorce that severs the marriage immediately without the possibility of revocability (raj'a) during the waiting period.
رجعي (rajʿī): A revocable divorce that allows the husband to take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) One
C) None
D) It depends on her intention

Key Takeaway
When a wife exceeds the delegated number of divorces, the occurrence of divorce depends on whether the excess constitutes a different act or a mere increase, a point of legitimate ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and his disciples.

Delegated Divorce: Reversing the Type (Ba'in vs Raj'i)
Ruling when wife reverses the type of delegated divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a wife reverses the type of delegated divorce and explain why the added description is nullified based on the distinction between essential and descriptive opposition.

Core Idea
If the wife reverses the type of delegated divorce, the divorce occurs as commanded; the added description is ignored.

In delegated divorce (tafwid), the husband authorizes his wife to divorce herself. But what happens if she changes the type of divorce he specified? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling when a wife revocable when told irrevocable, or vice versa, and the logical reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is delegated divorce where the wife reverses the type (ba'in vs raj'i). State the ruling from the passage.
The ruling is that the divorce occurs as commanded by the husband.
2
Examine the two scenarios
Consider the two cases: (1) husband says raj'i, wife says ba'in; (2) husband says ba'in, wife says raj'i. Both result in the husband's command taking effect.
The wife's added description is nullified.
3
Understand the reasoning: origin vs description
The wife performed the origin (divorce) with an additional description. The description is nullified, and the origin remains as commanded.
The husband specified the type, so the wife's role is limited to effecting the divorce, not changing it.
4
Distinguish essential vs descriptive opposition
The hypothetical objection argues that ba'in and raj'i are opposites, so nothing should occur. The reply clarifies that the opposition is descriptive (wasfi), not essential (dhati), unlike the case of one vs three divorces.
Essential opposition invalidates; descriptive opposition does not.
5
Apply the distinction to the one vs three case
If husband delegates one divorce and wife gives three, essential opposition causes no divorce to occur. This contrasts with the ba'in/raj'i case.
This comparison reinforces the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أمرت بالبائن، أو الرجعي فعكست، يقع ما أمر به. [٣]قوله : وقع ما أمر به الزوج ; فلو قال : طلّقي نفسك واحدة رجعية، فقالت : طلقت نفسي واحدة بائنة، تقع رجعيّة ; لأنها أتت بالأصل مع زيادة وصف، فيلغو الوصف ويبقى الأصل، ولو قال: طلقي نفسك واحدة بائنة، فقالت : نفسي واحدة رجعية، تقع بائنة، ويكون قولها : رجعيّة لغواً ; لأنّ الزوج لما عين صفةَ المفوّض إليها فحاجتها بعد ذلك إنّما هو في اختيار إيقاع الأصل دون تعيين الوصف. فإن قلت : من المعلوم أنّ بين الطلاق البائن والطلاق الرجعي تضاداً وتبايناً، فينبغي أن لا يقع شيء إذا عكست الأمر على ما مرّ في صورة تطليقها الثلاث إذا فوض إليها الواحد. قلت : ليس بينهما تضاد وتباين ذاتي بل وصفي، بخلاف الواحد والثلاث، فإنّ بينهما تبايناً ذاتياً فافترقا.
And if she was commanded to give a ba'in (irrevocable) or raj'i (revocable) divorce and she reversed, what was commanded occurs. [3] His saying: 'What the husband commanded occurs.' For example, if he said: 'Divorce yourself one revocable divorce,' and she said: 'I divorced myself one irrevocable divorce,' a revocable divorce occurs because she performed the origin with an additional description, so the description is nullified and the origin remains. And if he said: 'Divorce yourself one irrevocable divorce,' and she said: 'I divorced myself one revocable divorce,' an irrevocable divorce occurs, and her saying 'revocable' is null because the husband specified the description of what was delegated to her, so her need after that is only to choose to effect the origin without specifying the description. If you say: It is known that between ba'in and raj'i there is opposition and difference, so nothing should occur if she reversed the command, as in the case of her giving three when he delegated one. I say: Between them there is not essential opposition (tadadd dhāti) but rather descriptive (waṣfī), contrary to the case of one and three, for between them there is essential opposition, so they differ.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage states the ruling when a wife reverses the type of divorce she was commanded to give. The base ruling is: if the husband tells her to give a revocable (raj'i) divorce and she gives an irrevocable (ba'in) one, what the husband commanded occurs—a revocable divorce—because she added an extra description. Similarly, if commanded to give ba'in and she gives raj'i, a ba'in divorce occurs. The commentator notes that the wife's role is only to choose whether to effect the divorce, not to alter the type. A hypothetical objection is raised: since ba'in and raj'i are opposite, leaving should be similar to the case of delegating one and getting three. The commentator replies that the opposition between ba'in and raj'i is only descriptive (wasfi), not essential (dhāti). The type of divorce is an attribute of the act, not the act itself. Therefore, the wife's act is considered the same origin with an additional description, which is ignored. This contrasts with the essential opposition between one and three divorces.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): The act of a husband delegating the power of divorce to his wife.
بائن (bāʾin): An irrevocable divorce.
رجعي (rajʿī): A revocable divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No divorce occurs
B) An irrevocable divorce occurs
C) A revocable divorce occurs
D) Two divorces occur

Key Takeaway
When a wife reverses the type of delegated divorce, the description is nullified and the divorce falls as the husband commanded, because the opposition between ba'in and raj'i is descriptive, not essential.

When Wife Exceeds in Conditional Delegated Divorce: The Qasdiyya vs. Implicit One
Intra-Hanafi dispute on exceeding the number in conditional delegated divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when the wife exceeds the number in a conditional delegated divorce, and explain the legal reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
Exceeding the delegated number voids the condition; but dispute hinges on whether the base number is included in the excess.

In a conditional delegated divorce, the husband may say 'Divorce yourself once if you wish'. If the wife instead gives three divorces, does any divorce occur? Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ on whether her wish for three includes a wish for one. This lesson examines their reasoning and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Identify the exact wording: husband says 'Divorce yourself once if you wish' and wife says 'I divorce myself three times'.
This is the opposite of the earlier case where husband says three and wife gives one.
2
State the dispute
Abu Hanifa holds that no divorce occurs; the Sahibayn hold that one revocable divorce occurs.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's reasoning
The husband delegated a specific intentional (qasdiyya) divorce, not an implicit one. Her wish for three does not constitute a wish for that specific one.
The condition is not fulfilled because the wish must match the delegated act exactly.
4
Analyze the Sahibayn's reasoning
The wife owns one divorce and her wish for three necessarily includes a wish for one. The excess is ignored and one divorce takes effect.
Her general wish to divorce encompasses the delegated number.
5
Consider the practical consequence
According to Abu Hanifa, the marriage remains intact; according to the Sahibayn, one revocable divorce occurs.
The difference affects the legal status of the marriage.

And it does not occur in: 'Divorce yourself three times if you wish if she gives one', and its opposite: meaning if he said to her 'Divorce yourself once if you wish' and she gave three, it does not occur. So in the first, nothing occurs because the intent is 'if you wish the three', and the wish for three did not exist. And in the second, nothing occurs according to Abu Hanifa because the intent is 'divorce yourself one intentional (qasdiyya) if you wish', and the wish for one intentionally did not exist. [1] His saying: 'and did not exist' – it is said in al-ʿInāya: 'Indeed the condition must have a consequence; either what is conditioned upon or its like is estimated as later. [2] His saying: 'and in the second' – i.e. when she gave three in the case where the husband said to her 'Divorce yourself once if you wish.' Commentary
This passage deals with a conditional delegated divorce. The rule is that if the husband says 'Divorce yourself three if you wish' and she gives one, nothing occurs because her wish was for one, not three. The opposite: if he says 'Divorce yourself once if you wish' and she gives three, there is a dispute. Abu Hanifa says nothing occurs because the husband intended a single intentional divorce, not an implicit one. Sahibayn say one occurs because she wished for three, which includes the one. The commentary explains that the husband's delegation was for a specific, independent one, not one that is part of three. The footnote from al-ʿInāya clarifies that the condition 'if you wish' attaches to the number mentioned. In the first scenario, the condition is attached to three, so her wish for one is not relevant. In the second, the condition is attached to one; her wishing for three does not fulfill the condition for one intentionally. Ultimately, the dispute mirrors the earlier case without condition.

Key Terms
مشيئة (mashīʾa): Will or wish; the condition upon which the delegated divorce depends. In this context, it refers to the wife's choice to effect the divorce.
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of divorce.

Delegation of Divorce: Wife Differences Number
Intra-Hanafi dispute on delegation of divorce number.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on delegation of divorce when the wife performs a different number, and apply the distinction between intentional (qasdiyyah) and implicit (dimniyyah) wish.

Core Idea
The wife's wish must match the husband's delegated intention; implicit inclusion does not satisfy a condition for an exclusive single.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about tafwid (delegation of divorce) when the husband specifies a number and condition, but the wife performs a different number. The core issue is whether the wife's wish for three implicitly includes a wish for one, or whether the husband intended an exclusive, intentional wish. Two scenarios are analyzed, and the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) are contrasted, revealing a key distinction between qasdiyyah (intentional) and dimniyyah (implicit) intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, the husband says 'divorce yourself three if you wish' and she divorces once. Second, he says 'divorce yourself one if you wish' and she divorces three. Note the ruling for each scenario according to the text.
The first scenario is uncontested: nothing occurs. The second is disputed.
2
State the dispute in the second scenario
Abu Hanifa rules that no divorce occurs; the Sahibayn rule that one divorce occurs. Understand the evidence each side uses.
3
Analyze the husbands intention
According to Abu Hanifa, the husband intended her to wish for an exclusive single divorce (qasdiyyah), not an implicit one (dimniyyah). Her wish for three only includes an implicit wish for one, which does not fulfill the condition.
The Sahibayn argue that her wish for three necessarily includes the wish for one, so the extra is void.
4
Differentiate between descriptive and essential contradiction
The text contrasts this with a case where the husband specifies ba'in but the wife says raj'i. There, the divorce falls as ba'in because the contradiction is descriptive, not essential. For number (one vs three), the contradiction is essential, leading to the dispute.
This shows that ba'in and raj'i are not fundamentally opposite in the same way.
5
Identify the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: if the wife performs three after delegation of one, Abu Hanifa says the marriage continues (no divorce), while the Sahibayn say one divorce falls, making the marriage potentially revocable or irrevocable depending on context.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يقع في : طلقي نفسك ثلاثاً إن شئت لو طلقت واحدة، وعكسه) : أي لو قال لها : طلقي نفسك واحدة إن شئت فطلقت ثلاثاً، لا يقع، ففي الأَوَّل لا يقع شيء ; لأنَّ المراد إن شئت الثلاث، ولم توجد مشيئة الثلاث، وفي الثانية لا يقع شيء عند أبي حنيفة ; لأنَّ المراد طلقي نفسك واحدة قصديَّةً إن شئت ، ولم توجد مشيئة الواحدة قصداً، وعندهما تقع واحدة. طلقت الوصف ويبقى الأصل، ولو قال: طلقي نفسك واحدة بائنة، فقالت : نفسي واحدة رجعية، تقع بائنة، ويكون قولها : رجعيّة لغواً ; لأنّ الزوج لما عين صفةَ المفوّض إليها فحاجتها بعد ذلك إنّما هو في اختيار إيقاع الأصل دون تعيين الوصف. فإن قلت : من المعلوم أنّ بين الطلاق البائن والطلاق الرجعي تضاداً وتبايناً، فينبغي أن لا يقع شيء إذا عكست الأمر على ما مرّ في صورة تطليقها الثلاث إذا فوض إليها الواحد. قلت : ليس بينهما تضاد وتباين ذاتي بل وصفي، بخلاف الواحد والثلاث، فإنّ بينهما تبايناً ذاتياً فافترقا. قوله : ولم توجد ; قال في «العناية»: «فإنّ الشرط لا بد له من جزاء، فإما أن يكون المقدم عليه أو يقدر مثله متأخّراً، وعلى كلا التقديرين يتعلّق بمشيئة الثلاث، ولم توجد بمشيئة الواحدة). قوله : وفي الثانية ; أي إذا طلّقت ثلاثاً فيما قال لها الزوج : طلقي نفسك واحدة إن شئت. قوله: تقع واحدة ; لأنه ملكها الواحدة وعلّقها على مشيئتها، وقد شاءت الثلاث، ومشيئتها متضمنة لمشيئة الواحد مع الزيادة، فتلغو الزيادة وتبقى الواحد. والجواب عن ذلك من قبله : إنه إنّما فوّض إليها الواحدة القصدية المنفردة عن غيرها، لا الواحدة الضمنية، فتلغو مشيئة الواحد الضمنية، ولا يقع شيء ، وهذا كله إذا طلقت ثلاثاً بكلمة واحدة. انتهى من العناية» (٤ : ١٠٢ - ١٠٣). حتى يكون الواحدة
It does not occur in: 'Divorce yourself three times if you wish' if she divorced once, and its reverse. Meaning: if he said to her 'Divorce yourself once if you wish' and she divorced three, it does not occur. In the first case nothing occurs because the intended meaning is 'if you wish three' and the wish for three did not occur. In the second case nothing occurs according to Abu Hanifa because the intended meaning is 'divorce yourself one intentional divorce if you wish' and the intentional wish for one did not occur, while according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) one divorce occurs. The description is dropped and the original remains. If he said: 'Divorce yourself one ba'in divorce' and she said 'myself one raj'i divorce', it falls as ba'in, and her saying raj'i is void because when the husband specified the description of the delegated matter, her need after that is only to choose the occurrence of the original without specifying the description. If you say: It is known that between ba'in and raj'i there is contradiction and opposition, so nothing should occur if she reverses it as previously in the case of her divorcing three when he delegated one. I say: There is no essential contradiction and opposition between them but rather descriptive, unlike one and three for there is essential opposition. He said: 'and it did not occur' – he said in al-'Inayah: 'The condition must have a consequence; either it is what is advanced or its equivalent is estimated later, and in both estimations it is attached to the wish for three, and it did not occur with the wish for one.' He said: 'and in the second' – i.e., if she divorced three when the husband said to her 'divorce yourself once if you wish.' He said: 'one divorce occurs' because he gave her ownership of one and attached it to her wish, and she wished for three, and her wish includes the wish for one with an increase, so the increase is void and one remains. The answer from before: He only delegated to her the intentional one exclusive of others, not the implicit one, so the implicit wish for one is void and nothing occurs. All this is if she divorced three with one word. (End of al-'Inayah 4:102-103). So that the one occurs.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute from the commentary on al-Wiqayah. The issue: when the husband delegates divorce with a condition ('if you wish') specifying a number, and the wife performs a different number. Two scenarios: (1) Husband says 'divorce yourself three if you wish', she divorces once; nothing occurs because her wish was not for three, but for one. (2) Husband says 'divorce yourself once if you wish', she divorces three; Abu Hanifa says nothing occurs because he intended her to wish for a single, intentional divorce, and her wish was for three (implicitly including one) but not intentional; the Sahibayn say one divorce occurs because her wish includes one. The commentary then explains why the description (ba'in vs raj'i) does not cause contradiction like the number: descriptive vs essential. It quotes al-'Inayah to clarify the conditional reasoning. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated, but the text presents Abu Hanifa's view as the first position. The passage concludes that if she performs three with one word, the ruling stands.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of the right to divorce to the wife by the husband.
مشيئة (mashi'ah): Wish or will; the condition upon which the delegated divorce depends.
بائن (ba'in): Irrevocable divorce; the marriage ends immediately without possibility of return except new contract.
رجعي (raj'i): Revocable divorce; the husband can take back the wife during the waiting period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband tells his wife: 'Divorce yourself once if you wish,' and she says 'I divorce myself three times,' what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) Three divorces occur
B) One divorce occurs
C) No divorce occurs
D) It depends on her intention

Key Takeaway
In delegation of divorce, the wife's wish must match the husband's intended number and intentionality; implicit inclusion does not suffice for an exclusive condition.

Conditional Divorce: Wife's Response Must Be Unconditional
Ruling on wife's conditional response in delegated/conditional talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for the wife's wish in a conditional divorce and apply the ruling to determine whether the divorce occurs.

Core Idea
In conditional divorce, the wife's wish must be immediate and unconditional; a conditional response invalidates the delegation.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on a common scenario in conditional divorce: the husband makes the talaq conditional on the wife's immediate wish, and she responds with a conditional statement. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly applying the principles of ta'liq and tafwid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's statement and its condition
The husband says 'You are divorced if you wish,' making the occurrence of divorce dependent on the wife's immediate, unconditional wish.
The husband's statement is a form of ta'liq (conditional suspension).
2
Analyze the wife's response
The wife responds with a conditional statement, e.g., 'I wish if you wish,' which makes her own wish contingent on the husband's wish.
The wife's wish is not immediate; it is conditional on another event.
3
Determine if the condition is fulfilled
The condition requires the wife's wish to exist at the moment of her statement, not to be dependent on a future event. Since her wish is contingent, the condition is not met.
The husband's subsequent statement 'I wish' does not cure the defect because the wife's wish was not immediate.
4
Apply the ruling
Because the condition is not fulfilled, the divorce does not occur. The delegated power (tafwid) is invalidated by the wife's conditional response.
The same reasoning applies if the wife conditions her wish on anyone else's wish (e.g., father).
5
Contrast with explicit number statement
If the husband delegates one divorce and the wife says 'I divorced myself one and one and one,' only one divorce falls because the number was not explicitly stated; the implicit wish is void, and the extra numbers are ignored.
This is by agreement (ittifaqan).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا في : أنت طالق إن شئت، فقالت : شئت إن شئت، فقال : شئت ; لأنه علق الطلاق بمشيئتها الموجودة في الحال، ولم يوجد ذلك ; لأنها علقت وجود مشيئتها بوجود مشيئته، ولا علم لها بوجود مشيئته ; أما لو قالت في جواب طلقي واحدة إن شئت : طلقت نفسي واحدة وواحدة وواحدة وقعت واحدة اتفاقاً ; لأنّها لم تتعرّض للعدد لفظاً، الضمنية، فتلغو الزائدة، وتبقى الواحدة. كذا في «البحر». قوله : ولا في أنت طالق.... الخ ; يعني : إذا قال الزوج : أنت طالق إن شئت ، معلّقاً الطلاق ; أي إيقاعه على مشيئة المرأة. فإن قالت في جوابها شئت فقط ، يقع الطلاق ; لكون مفعول المشيئة حينئذ هو ما جعل جزاء في كلامه، فبوجود الشرط ينزل الجزاء، وكذا إذا قالت : شئت طلاقي. وبالجملة كل لفظ يدل على وجود مشيئتها في الحال إذا وقع جواباً للطلاق المعلق بالمشيئة يقع به الطلاق ، وإن قالت : شئت إن شئت أنت، فإن لم يقل الزوج في جوابه شيئاً، أو قال : لم أشأ لا يقع الطلاق ; لأنه علق وقوعه بمشيئتها ، وقد علقت مشيئتها بمشيئة الطلاق، ولم توجد مشيئة الزوج ، فلم يوجد المعلق عليها : أي مشيئة المرأة، فلم يوجد ما علق بها. وإن قال بعد قولها : شئت إن شئت أنت شئت أنا، لا يقع الطلاق أيضاً; لأنّ الزوج علّق الطلاق بمشيئة المرأة المنجزة ; أي الموجودة في الحال، ولم توجد ذلك ; لأنها علقت مشيئتها على مشيئة الزوج ، فلم يوجد الشرط، والإتيان بالمعلقة اشتغال بما لا يعني ، فيبطل به التفويض، ويخرج الأمر من يدها. قوله : علّق ; بصيغة المجهول، فما بعده نائب مناب فاعله، وضمير «لأنه» للشأن، أو بصيغة المعروف، فما بعده مفعوله، وفاعله مرجع ضمير لأنه وهو الزوج. قوله : ولا علم لها ; أي لا تعلم هي مشيئة الزوج الطلاق في الحال، وقول الزوج : شئت، لا يدلّ عليه على ما سيأتي. قوله : مشيئته ; ولهذا لا يقع فيما إذا قالت : شئت إن شاء أبي، أو شئت إن شاء فلان.
And not in: 'You are divorced if you wish,' and she said: 'I wish if you wish,' and he said: 'I wish.' Because he made divorce conditional on her wish present at the moment, and that did not occur because she made the existence of her wish conditional on the existence of his wish, and she does not know of the existence of his wish. As for if she said in response to 'divorce yourself one if you wish': 'I divorced myself one and one and one,' one divorce occurs by agreement because she did not mention the number verbally; the implicit (wish) is void, the extra is void, and one remains. So in al-Bahr. He said: 'And not in: You are divorced...' meaning: if the husband says: 'You are divorced if you wish,' making the divorce conditional on the wife's wish. If she says in response only 'I wish,' the divorce falls because the object of the wish then is what he made the consequence in his speech, so with the existence of the condition the consequence occurs. Likewise if she says: 'I wish my divorce.' In summary, any word that indicates the existence of her wish at the moment, if it occurs as a response to the divorce conditional on the wish, the divorce falls by it. And if she says: 'I wish if you wish,' and the husband does not say anything in response, or says 'I did not wish,' the divorce does not fall because he made its occurrence conditional on her wish, and she made her wish conditional on the husband's wish, and the husband's wish did not exist, so what was conditional upon it (i.e., the wife's wish) did not exist, so what was conditional upon it did not occur. And if he says after her saying 'I wish if you wish' - 'I wish,' the divorce also does not fall because the husband made divorce conditional on the immediate wish of the wife, i.e., present at the moment, and that did not exist because she made her wish conditional on the husband's wish, so the condition did not exist, and producing the conditional (statement) is occupation with what does not concern, so the delegation is invalidated and the matter leaves her hand. He said: 'made conditional' in passive voice, and 'liannahu' refers to the matter, or in active voice, and its pronoun refers to the husband. He said: 'and she does not know' meaning she does not know the husband's wish for divorce at the moment, and the husband's saying 'I wish' does not indicate it as will come. He said: 'his wish'; therefore it does not fall if she says 'I wish if my father wishes' or 'I wish if so-and-so wishes.'
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage explains a specific scenario in conditional divorce. The husband says 'You are divorced if you wish,' making the occurrence of divorce dependent on the wife's immediate wish. If she responds with a conditional statement (e.g., 'I wish if you wish'), the divorce does not fall because her wish is not immediate but contingent on another's wish. Even if the husband later says 'I wish,' it does not suffice because the condition required her immediate unconditional wish. The passage also discusses the case where the wife says 'I divorced myself one and one and one' in response to a delegation of one; it falls as one by agreement because she did not state the number expressly. The commentary from the sharh clarifies that any response indicating immediate wish works, but a conditional response invalidates the delegation. This ruling is foundational for understanding how conditions operate in delegated talaq.

Key Terms
معلق (mu'allaq): Conditional; a divorce whose occurrence depends on a future condition (here, the wife's wish).
منجزة (munajjazah): Immediate or absolute; the condition must be instant and not contingent on another event.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The divorce occurs as soon as she says 'I wish'
B) The divorce occurs when the husband also says 'I wish'
C) No divorce occurs
D) It becomes a delegation and she can divorce later

Key Takeaway
The wife's wish in a conditional divorce must be immediate and unconditional; any conditional response invalidates the delegation and no divorce occurs.

The Distinction Between Mashiyyah and Iradah in Talaq
Explains rulings on uttering 'shi'tu' in conditional divorce and the linguistic distinction between mashiyyah and iradah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on uttering 'shi'tu' vs 'aradtu' in talaq and explain the linguistic basis for the distinction.

Core Idea
Mashiyyah implies creation; iradah is mere desire. That is why 'shi'tu talaqaki' effects talaq but 'aradtu talaqaki' does not.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, we explore a nuanced ruling in the chapter of tafwid. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the words 'shi'tu' (I wished) and 'aradtu' (I desired) when used in divorce. This distinction hinges on the derivational meaning of mashiyyah from 'shay'' (thing/existent), versus iradah as simple desire. The text also presents a significant intra-Hanafi dispute about whether this linguistic distinction holds, with some theologians and lexicographers arguing they are synonymous.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional divorce scenario
The husband says 'you are divorced if you wish', the wife responds 'I wish if you wish'. The wife's present will is missing, so talaq does not occur.
2
Analyze the husband's statement 'shi'tu' without mention of talaq
If the husband says 'shi'tu' alone, even with intention, talaq does not occur because the word 'talaq' is not mentioned and intention cannot supply it.
3
Examine the case 'shi'tu talaqaki' with intention
Most Hanafi jurists rule that talaq occurs because mashiyyah is derived from 'shay'' (existent), so it implies bringing the divorce into existence.
4
Contrast with 'aradtu talaqaki'
Iradah means desire, not creation, so saying 'aradtu talaqaki' does not effect talaq even with intention.
5
Consider the intra-Hanafi dispute
Some scholars (e.g., from al-Binayah) argue that mashiyyah and iradah are synonymous based on Quranic usage, while the majority distinguishes them for humans but equates them for Allah.
The relied-upon fatwa position follows the majority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق باب التفويض والأمر باليد والمشيئة وإن نوى الطلاق
وذلك لأنَّ قولَهُ أنت طالق إنشاء، فهو إيقاع في الحال، لكن بشرط مشيئتها ، فمشيئتها لا بُدَّ من وجودِها في الحال، ولم يوجد ذلك"، (وإن نوى الطلاق) : أي إن نوى الطلاق بقوله : شئت.
قال في «الهداية : لأنه ليس في كلام المرأةِ ذِكْرُ الطَّلاق ؛ ليصير الزوج شائياً طلاقها ، والنية لا تعمل في غير المذكور حتى لو قال : شئت طلاقك، يقع إذا نوى
قوله : وذلك ؛ بيان لوجود كون الطلاق معلّقاً . على مشيئتها الموجودة في الحال، ودفع لما يتوهّم أنّ المشيئة المفهومة من قوله : إن شئت، التي علق عليها طلاقها، ليست مقيدة في كلامه، بكونها موجودة في الحال، فمن أين هذا التخصيص. وحاصل الكلام أن قوله : أنت طالق إنشاء شرعاً ، وإن كان خبراً لغة، والانشاء وضع للإنشاء الحالي لا الاستقبالي ؛ ولذا صَرَّحوا بأنه لا ينعقد البيع وغيره بصيغة المستقبل إلا إذا دلت قرينة على كونها بمعنى الحال، كما مر في موضعه، ولما كان الإنشاء إيقاعاً في الحال وقد علقه بمشيئتها علم قطعاً أن المراد هو المشيئة الموجودة في الحال لا مطلقاً، ولا المشيئة الاستقبالية.
قوله : ولم يوجد ذلك ؛ أي وجود مشيئة المرأة الحالي ؛ لأنها علّقت مشيئتها بمشيئته، وهي غير معلومة لها حالاً.
قوله : وإن ؛ الواو وصليّة ؛ أي وإن نوى الزوج بقوله : شئتُ الطلاق، فلا يقع الطلاق به، نواه أو لم ينوه.
قوله : قال في «الهداية : أي في تعليل عدم وقوع الطلاق مبتدأ بقوله : «شئت»، وحاصل تعليله : إن شئت ليس بصريح في الطلاق، وليس الطلاق مذكوراً أيضاً أو محذوفاً ؛ لأنَّ قوله : «شئت»، جواب لقولها: «شئتُ إن شئت»، ولا أثر للطلاق في كلامها، فلا يكون له أثر في كلامه والنية لا تعتبر في غير المذكور، بـل عملها في تعيين أحد محتملي المذكور حكماً أو حقيقة.
قوله : إذا نوى ؛ فيه بحث وهو أنه قال : شئتُ طلاقك، فأتى بلفظ صريح الطلاق، فينبغي أن لا يحتاج إلى النية. لأنه إيقاع مبتدأ ؛ لأنَّ المشيئة تنبئ عن الوجود وأجيب عنه : بأن قوله : شئت طلاقك، قد يقصد به وجوده ملكا، وقد يقصد به وجوده إيقاعا، فلا بد من النية ؛ لتعيين جهة الوجود وقوعا. كذا في «العناية»).
قوله : ايقاع مبتدأ ؛ أي لا تعلّق له بما مضى من قوله : أنت طالق إن شئت، فإنه قد بطل بعدم وجود مشيئتها المنجزة كما مرّ.
قوله : لأن المشيئة ... الخ ؛ اعلم أن أكثر الفقهاء منهم : صاحب «الهداية» قالوا بوقوع الطلاق بقوله : شئتُ طلاقك عند النية، وعدم وقوعه بقوله : أردتُ طلاقك، وبينوا وجه الفرق : بأن المشيئة في الأصل مأخوذة من الشيء، وهو اسم الموجود فكان قوله شئت بمعنى أوجدتُ الطلاق، وإيجاده إيقاعه، بخلاف الإرادة، فإنّها لغة : الطلب، وطلب الشيء لا يستلزم إيقاعه.
وردّ عليهم بأن أهل الكلامَ صرّحوا في بحث الصفات الإلهية : أن إرادته ومشيئته واحدة، وأجاب عنه في «الفوائد الظهيرية» و«العناية»: «إنّه يجوز أن تكون بينهما تفرقة بالنسبة إلى العباد والتسوية بالنسبة إلى الله ؛ لأنّ ما يطلبه تعالى يوجد لا محالة ، كما يوجد ما شاء بخلاف العباد.
وفيه بحث، إذ لا دليل على هذه التفرقة لغة وقال في «البناية»: «قال شيخي العلامة : ما ذكره يشير إلى أنّ الإيجاد هو المعنى الأصلي للمشيئة، وليس كذلك، فإنّ المشيئة مفسرة في أكثر كتب اللغة بالإرادة لا بالايجاد ومستعملة في القرآن والحديث، وفي تراكيب الناس بمعنى الإرادة دون الإيجاد ، قال الله تعالى : وَيَغْفِرُ مَا دُونَ ذَلِكَ لِمَن يشَاءُ ، وقال : يُدْخِلُ مَن يَشَاءُ فِي رَحْمَتِهِ ، وقال : إن شئت أن تقوم فقم، وإن شئت أن تقعد فاقعد
Book of Talaq, Chapter of Tafwid and Command by Hand and Will, and if he intended divorce. That is because his statement 'you are divorced' is a creation (insha), so it is a realization (iqa') at the moment, but conditioned upon her will. Her will must necessarily exist at the moment, and it did not exist. (And if he intended divorce): meaning if he intended divorce by his statement 'I wished'. He said in al-Hidayah: Because there is no mention of divorce in the woman's speech, so that the husband becomes a 'sha'i' of her divorce, and intention does not work in what is not mentioned. Even if he said: 'I wished your divorce', it occurs if he intended. Statement: 'that is' is an explanation that the divorce is suspended upon her will existing at the moment, and repelling the misconception that the will understood from his statement 'if you wish' is not restricted in his speech to existing at the moment. The summary is that his statement 'you are divorced' is a creation (insha) legally, although it is a statement (khabar) linguistically, and creation is placed for immediate creation not future; therefore they stated that sale and other contracts do not conclude with future tense unless a context indicates present meaning. Since creation is an immediate realization and he suspended it upon her will, it is definitively known that what is intended is the will existing at the moment, not absolutely nor future will. Statement: 'and it did not exist' means the existence of the woman's present will, because she suspended her will upon his will, and it is not known to her at the moment. Statement: 'and if (wa in)': the waw is conjunctive; meaning if the husband intended divorce by saying 'I wished divorce', divorce does not occur by it, whether he intended or not. Statement: 'he said in al-Hidayah': meaning in the explanation of the non-occurrence of divorce beginning with his saying 'I wish', and the summary of his explanation: 'I wish' is not explicit in divorce, and divorce is not mentioned nor implied, because his saying 'I wish' is a response to her saying 'I wish if you wish', and there is no trace of divorce in her speech, so there is no trace in his speech, and intention is not considered in what is not mentioned, rather its work is in specifying one of the two possibilities of what is mentioned, legally or literally. Statement: 'if he intended' contains a discussion, that he said 'I wished your divorce', so he uttered an explicit word of divorce, so it should not need intention. Because it is an independent realization, since 'mashiyyah' indicates existence. And it was answered: that his saying 'I wished your divorce' may intend its existence as ownership (milkan) or its existence as realization (iqa'an), so intention is necessary to specify the direction of existence as occurrence. This is in al-Inayah. Statement: 'an independent realization' means it has no connection to what preceded from his saying 'you are divorced if you wish', for it has become void due to the non-existence of her realized will. Statement: 'because mashiyyah' etc.: Know that most jurists, including the author of al-Hidayah, said that divorce occurs by his saying 'I wished your divorce' with intention, and does not occur by his saying 'I desired your divorce' (aradtu). They explained the difference: that mashiyyah is originally derived from 'shay'' (thing), which is the name of the existent, so his saying 'shi'tu' means 'I brought the divorce into existence', and bringing it into existence is its realization, unlike iradah (desire), which linguistically means seeking, and seeking a thing does not entail its realization. They were refuted by the fact that the theologians (ahl al-kalam) stated in the discussion of divine attributes that His will (iradah) and His volition (mashiyyah) are one. It was answered in al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah and al-Inayah that it is possible that there is a distinction between them with respect to servants (humans) and equality with respect to Allah, because what He seeks necessarily exists, just as what He wills exists, unlike servants. There is discussion in this, as there is no linguistic evidence for this distinction. It was said in al-Binayah: 'My teacher, the scholar, said: What he mentioned indicates that bringing into existence is the original meaning of mashiyyah, but it is not so, for mashiyyah is explained in most dictionaries as iradah (will), not as bringing into existence, and it is used in the Quran and hadith and in people's speech to mean iradah, not bringing into existence. Allah says: 'and He forgives what is less than that for whom He wills' (4:48), and says: 'He admits whom He wills into His mercy' (42:8), and says: 'If you wish to stand, stand; if you wish to sit, sit.'
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and ruling commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the topic of tafwid (delegation) in divorce. It addresses the legal implications of uttering 'shi'tu' (I wished) in the context of a conditional divorce. The text begins by restating the rule from the matn: if a husband says to his wife 'you are divorced if you wish', then her statement 'I wish' (shi'tu) does not effect divorce because her present will was not existent at the time of his utterance (since she conditionally deferred to his will). The commentary from al-Hidayah clarifies that the husband's statement 'you are divorced' is a creative act (insha) that requires immediate realization, but it is conditioned on her will existing at the moment. Since she responded with 'I wish' but did not actually will immediately (because she tied her will to his), the condition fails. Then the text discusses the scenario where the husband says 'I wished' (shi'tu) without explicitly mentioning divorce. Even if he intended divorce, it does not occur because the word 'shi'tu' does not mention divorce and is merely a response to her statement; intention cannot supply an omitted element. However, if he says 'shi'tu talaqaki' (I wished your divorce) and intends divorce, it does occur. The commentary explains that because 'mashiyyah' is derived from 'thing' (shay'), which is an existent, uttering 'shi'tu' implies bringing into existence. But the act of bringing divorce into existence could be either as ownership (malikan) or as realization (iqa'an), so intention is needed to specify. The text then presents a significant intra-Hanafi dispute about the linguistic meaning of 'mashiyyah' as distinct from 'iradah' (will/desire). The majority of Hanafi jurists, including the author of al-Hidayah, hold that 'mashiyyah' carries the sense of creation/existence, while 'iradah' is mere desire. Therefore, saying 'shi'tu talaqaki' with intention effects talaq, but 'aradtu talaqaki' does not. This position is challenged by theologians (mutakallimun) who assert that in divine attributes, mashiyyah and iradah are synonymous. The response from al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah and al-Inayah attempts a distinction: for humans, mashiyyah may imply creation while iradah does not, but for Allah they are identical because His desire necessarily results in existence. The commentator in al-Binayah questions this distinction, citing linguistic evidence from dictionaries and Quranic usage that mashiyyah is consistently used to mean iradah (will), not creation. This nuanced discussion illustrates the methodological interplay between linguistic analysis and legal reasoning (talil) in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashiyyah): In this text, mashiyyah is defined by the majority of Hanafi jurists as a term derived from 'shay" (thing), implying existence and creation. Thus 'shi'tu' means 'I brought into existence.' Some scholars argue it is synonymous with iradah (will/desire).
الإرادة (al-iradah): In this text, iradah is defined as linguistic 'talab' (seeking/desiring) without necessarily bringing into existence. Saying 'aradtu talaqaki' does not effect talaq because mere desire does not create the act.
التفويض (al-tafwid): Tafwid in the context of this chapter refers to the husband delegating the decision of divorce to his wife, typically by saying 'anti taliq in shi'ti' (you are divorced if you wish).
إن شئت (in shi'ti): The conditional phrase 'if you wish' used by the husband to delegate the choice to his wife. The wife's wish must be present and immediate for talaq to occur.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a husband says to his wife 'shi'tu talaqaki' (I wished your divorce) with the intention of divorcing her, what is the ruling?
A) Talaq does not occur because 'shi'tu' is not explicit.
B) Talaq occurs because 'mashiyyah' implies bringing into existence.
C) Talaq occurs only if the wife also wishes.
D) Talaq occurs without need for intention because 'shi'tu talaqaki' is explicit.

Key Takeaway
Mashiyyah and iradah are linguistically distinct in Hanafi fiqh: mashiyyah implies existence (creation) so 'shi'tu talaqaki' effects talaq with intention, while iradah is mere desire so 'aradtu talaqaki' does not.

The Need for Intention When Saying 'Shi'tu' Without Explicit Divorce
Expansion of al-Hidayah's ruling on 'shi'tu' and intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on saying 'shi'tu' without mentioning divorce and explain why intention is required, deriving from the distinction between mashi'a and irada.

Core Idea
When 'shi'tu' alone is said, intention is needed because the word can refer either to the conditional divorce or an initiating declaration.

In the context of conditional divorce, the phrase 'shi'tu' (I wished) can be ambiguous. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya ala Sharh al-Wiqaya examines whether simply saying 'shi'tu' effects divorce when the word 'divorce' is not explicitly mentioned. The Hanafi position requires intention due to the linguistic possibility that 'shi'tu' may imply bringing into existence, unlike 'aradtu'. We'll explore the reasoning and the intra-Hanafi debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The husband says 'You are divorced if you wish', the wife says 'I wished if you wished', and the husband says 'I wished'. The question is whether the husband's 'shi'tu' effects divorce.
The context is a conditional divorce where the husband's will is invoked.
2
Understand the implicit divorce
The commentator argues that divorce is implicit (muqaddar) in the context, so intention can operate. The author of al-Hidayah initially held that it does not occur because divorce is not explicitly mentioned.
The base text from al-Hidayah is being expanded here.
3
Analyze the linguistic distinction
Some Hanafis differentiate between mashi'a (which can mean 'bring into existence') and irada (which cannot). Therefore, saying 'shi'tu' requires intention, but 'aradtu' does not effect divorce even with intention.
This distinction is crucial for the ruling.
4
Examine the counter-argument
The author of Ghayat al-Bayan argues that the distinction is weak; lexicographers treat them as synonyms. According to this view, 'aradtu talaqak' should also effect divorce.
This is a legitimate intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Determine the operative ruling
The mufta bihi is that saying 'shi'tu' alone requires intention; if he intends an initiating divorce, it occurs; if he intends the object of the conditional will, it does not.
This is the position relied upon for fatwa.

Book of Divorce, Chapter of Delegation and the Command by Hand and Will. I say: When the husband says: 'You are divorced if you wish', its meaning is 'if you wish your divorce'. Then she says: 'I wished if you wished', i.e., 'I wished my divorce if you wished my divorce'. Then the husband says: 'I wished', i.e., 'I wished your divorce'. Since divorce is implicit, intention operates in it. It is possible to respond to it: that the implicit divorce is the object of the will. He elaborated, and its summary is that 'mashi'a' is the verbal noun of 'sha'a' meaning 'willed', and it is possible that 'shi'tu' means 'I brought into existence'. So on the view that existence is implied by this word, it needs intention, unlike 'irada' (intention), for it does not imply the meaning of bringing into existence, so divorce does not occur by it even if he intends, because intention does not encounter a locus, as in your saying: 'I desired your divorce' and 'I loved your divorce'. (End) And in 'Ghayat al-Bayan': This distinction they made between 'irada' and 'mashi'a' is weak, because lexicographers like al-Jawhari and the author of 'al-Diwan' did not distinguish between them, and our companions explicitly stated in theological books that there is no difference according to Ahl al-Sunnah between 'irada' and 'mashi'a', and they interpreted 'irada' as one of the two determined things coming into existence, so it also indicates existence. Then divorce occurs by his saying 'shi'tu talaqak' by consensus, so it should occur by his saying 'aradtu talaqak' also, because they are synonymous, same in meaning. [1] His saying: 'I say...' etc.; this is an objection to what the author of al-Hidayah gave as the reason for divorce not occurring by his saying 'shi'tu' alone, namely that divorce is not mentioned in her speech so that the will could attach to it in his saying 'shi'tu'. Its summary: that divorce here, although not mentioned verbally, is implicit and mentioned in effect, so intention operates in it. [2] His saying: 'implicit'; for often the object of 'mashi'a' and 'irada' is omitted, relying on context, as in their saying when mentioning not doing an action: 'If I wished I would have done it', i.e., if I wished this action I would have committed it. So when divorce was implicit in the first speech, namely his saying 'you are divorced if you wish', and it was the object of the will, and her speech was a response to him, and his second speech was a response to hers, it must be the object of the will in her speech as well. So the husband's saying 'shi'tu' is like his saying 'shi'tu talaqak'. [3] His saying: 'it is possible...' etc.; the summary of the response: We concede that divorce is implicit in his saying 'shi'tu' and it has an object implied, which is divorce. But this divorce is the one that is made an object of the will, not the divorce that is made a condition of the will. The implication of that divorce does not necessitate occurrence, because divorce was suspended on her will—a will that existed—but that will did not exist. Rather, the woman conditioned its existence on the existence of his will, which is unknown to her. As for when he says: 'shi'tu al-talaq' and intends, it occurs, because this is an initiating declaration. He needed intention only because it is possible that by 'divorce' is meant the object of the will; if he intends this, it does not occur, and if he intends an initiating divorce, it occurs, so intention is necessary. But it is different from the divorce that is made a condition of the will. And there is discussion: it is not apparent that there is a difference between the two divorces. The most that can be said: the divorce that is the condition of the will is the one mentioned in his saying 'you are divorced if you wish', for its meaning is 'if you wish, you are divorced', so it is the condition of the will. If the woman said in response: 'shi'tu talaqi', it would also be a condition of the will, so it occurs due to the existence of the present will, unlike the implicit divorce in her saying 'shi'tu in shi'ta' and in his saying 'shi'tu', because it is the object of the will, not its condition. This is what was transmitted in 'Dhakhirat al-Uqba' from his teacher, and it is nothing. For what is implicit in the chapter of will is only the content of the condition, nothing else. So the meaning of His saying: 'If He willed, He would have guided you' is 'if Allah willed your guidance', so the object of the will in his first statement 'you are divorced if you wish' is the divorce that is the condition. And since her saying 'shi'tu in shi'ta' is a response to him, and his saying 'shi'tu' is a response to her, and the apparent rule is that the response matches what it is responding to, the object of the will in both is also that which is made the condition, nothing else. [1] His saying: 'And he only...' etc.; repelling a supposed objection. The summary of the objection: Since 'shi'tu al-talaq' is an initiating declaration, it should not need intention, yet they explicitly stated the need for it. [2] His saying: 'because it is possible...' etc.; its summary: since 'divorce' can mean the object of the will or initiating divorce, intention is needed. And there is discussion:

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling from al-Hidayah regarding the phrase 'you are divorced if you wish' and subsequent statements of 'I wished'. The commentator begins by stating the scenario: the husband says 'you are divorced if you wish', the wife says 'I wished if you wished', and the husband says 'I wished'. The issue is whether this constitutes a divorce. According to the author of al-Hidayah, the husband's saying 'shi'tu' alone does not effect divorce because the word 'divorce' is not mentioned in the wife's speech to which the husband's 'shi'tu' could refer. However, the commentator argues that divorce is implicit (muqaddar) in the context, and therefore intention (niyya) can operate. He then discusses the linguistic meaning of 'mashi'a' (will) versus 'irada' (intention). Some Hanafi scholars differentiate between them, saying 'mashi'a' can mean 'bringing into existence' whereas 'irada' cannot, so intention is needed for 'shi'tu' but not for 'aradtu'. However, the author of Ghayat al-Bayan considers this distinction weak, pointing out that lexicographers and Hanafi theologians treat them as synonyms. According to the relied-upon position, saying 'aradtu talaqak' should also require intention? Actually, the text says that by consensus, 'shi'tu talaqak' effects divorce, so 'aradtu talaqak' should effect it too if they are synonymous. The passage then goes into detailed analysis of whether the implied divorce is the object of the will or the condition of the will. The key ruling: if the husband says 'shi'tu' alone, intention is needed; if he intends the initiating divorce, it occurs; if he intends the object of the conditional will, it does not occur. The passage ends with a discussion that the implication in such contexts is always the content of the condition, not a separate object.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashi'a): The verbal noun of 'sha'a' meaning 'will' or 'wish'; in this context, it is argued to imply bringing into existence.
الإرادة (al-irada): Intention or will; in this context, it is argued not to imply bringing into existence.
النية (al-niyya): Intention in the heart; here, it is needed to determine which meaning of 'shi'tu' is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Saying 'shi'tu' without explicit mention of divorce requires intention to determine whether an initiating divorce is intended, due to the linguistic ambiguity of 'mashi'a'.
