The Husband's Statement 'Shia'tu' Requires Niyyah for Divorce to Occur
Explaining the ruling on 'shia'tu' and the need for intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the husband's statement 'shia'tu' to determine when a divorce takes effect, and explain the role of intention (niyyah) in disambiguating the statement.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'shia'tu' without intention, no divorce occurs; intention is required to distinguish the divorce as a new initiation from the object of the wife's will.

In conditional divorce scenarios, the wording of both parties matters. This passage from the Hanafi tradition clarifies what happens when a husband, responding to his wife's conditional statement 'I am divorced if I wish', simply says 'I wished'. Does this complete the condition and effect the divorce? The Hanafi answer is clear: it does not, unless the husband explicitly says 'I wished divorce' with the specific intention of initiating a fresh divorce. Let's see how the commentators derive this ruling from the language of the Quran and the structure of conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the statement
Consider the wife's conditional statement: 'You are divorced if I wish.' The husband responds 'shia'tu' (I wished). What is the legal effect?
Understand the context: the condition is the wife's will, not the husband's.
2
Distinguish between two types of divorce
The passage differentiates between divorce as the object of the will (maf'ul al-mashiah) and divorce as the consequence (jaza' al-mashiah). The husband's response is linked to the object, not a new divoce.
The object is what is willed; the consequence is what results from the will.
3
Apply the ruling on intention
When the husband says 'shia'tu al-talaq' with intention, it is a new initiation and divorce occurs. Without intention, it is assumed to refer to the object of the wife's will and does not occur.
Intention (niyyah) disambiguates the ambiguous statement.
4
Analyze the Quranic evidence
The verse 'and if He willed, He would guide you' (16:9) shows that the object of 'will' is the consequence (guidance), not the act of willing. This supports the view that the husband's 'shia'tu' refers to the consequence, not a separate divorce.
Quranic usage is key to the Hanafi argument.
5
Evaluate the counterargument
The author rejects a distinction proposed by some scholars who argued that the husband's 'shia'tu' could be a separate divorce. The commentator dismisses this, showing that in all cases the object is the consequence.
The rejected view is from Dhakhirat al-'Uqba.

[٢] قوله : لأنه يمكن ... الخ ; حاصله : أنه لَمّا احتمل الطلاق الذي هو مفعولُ المشيئة والطلاق الابتدائي احتيج إلى النية، وفيه بحث : (۱) النحل : من الآية.
And when the husband says: 'I wished' an object is estimated for it, which is divorce. So this is the divorce that is made the object of the will, not the divorce that is made the consequence of the will. And the estimation of that divorce does not necessitate occurrence, because divorce is suspended on her will, the will is present, and that will did not occur. Rather, the woman suspended its existence on the existence of his will, and that is unknown to her. As for if he says: 'I wished divorce' and intends, it occurs, because this is a new initiation. And he only needs intention because it is possible that by 'divorce' is meant what is the object of the will. So if he intends this, it does not occur; if he intends an initial divorce, it occurs. So intention is necessary. 'I wished', but it is not the divorce that is made the consequence of the will. And there is investigation: that no difference appears between the two divorces. The most that can be said is that the divorce which is the consequence of the will is the one mentioned in his saying: 'You are divorced if you wish', because its estimation is: 'If you wish, you are divorced', so it is a consequence of the will. If the woman said in response: 'I wished my divorce', it would also be a consequence of the will, so it would occur due to the existence of the present will, unlike the estimated divorce in her saying: 'I wished if you wish' and in his saying: 'I wished', because it is the object of the will not its consequence. This is what is transmitted in Dhakhirat al-ʿUqba from his teacher, and it is nothing. Indeed, the estimated in the matter of will is only the content of the consequence, nothing else. So the meaning of His statement: 'and if He willed, He would guide you' is: if God willed your guidance. So the object of the will in his first statement 'You are divorced if you wish' is the divorce which is the consequence. (1) And since her saying 'I wished if you wish' is a response to him, and his saying 'I wished' is a response to her saying, and the apparent is that the response corresponds to what it responds to, the object of the will in both is also that which is made the consequence, nothing else. [1] His statement: 'And only...' etc. is a repelling of an anticipated objection. The formulation of the objection: since 'I wished divorce' is a new initiation, he should not need intention, whereas they explicitly stated the need for it. [2] His statement: 'Because it is possible...' etc. its summary: since it is possible that the divorce is the object of the will or an initial divorce, intention is needed. And there is investigation: (1) An-Nahl from the verse.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school concerning the husband's response of 'shia'tu' (I wished) in the context of conditional divorce. The commentator expands on the text, explaining the distinction between divorce as the object of the will (مفعول المشيئة) and divorce as the consequence of the will (جزاء المشيئة). The author first establishes that when the husband says 'shia'tu' without specifying, an object is implied, which is divorce. However, this divorce is considered the object of the wife's will (since she said 'if I wish'), not an independent divorce. Therefore, it does not take effect because the wife's actual will (the condition) has not occurred. In contrast, if the husband says 'shia'tu al-talaq' (I wished divorce) with the intention of initiating a new divorce, it does take effect because it is a fresh declaration. The need for intention arises because the word 'talaq' might be interpreted as the object of the will (from the earlier condition) rather than a new divorce. The passage then discusses a debate about the distinction between these two types of divorce. Some scholars argue that in the conditional phrase 'You are divorced if you wish', the estimated divorce is the consequence, so the wife's saying 'I wish my divorce' would also be a consequence and take effect. But the author rebuts this, citing that in the Quranic usage 'if He willed, He would guide you', the object of the will is the consequence (guidance), not the act of willing. Therefore, in the husband's original statement 'You are divorced if you wish', the object of his will is the divorce that is the consequence. The author concludes that both in the wife's response 'I wished if you wish' and the husband's 'shia'tu', the object is the consequence, not a separate divorce. The commentary then addresses an anticipated objection: why require intention if 'shia'tu al-talaq' is a new initiation? The answer is that it is ambiguous. The passage is central to understanding the Hanafi position on the interaction of wills in conditional divorce.

Key Terms
المشيئة (al-mashī’ah): Will or wish; in this context, the wife's conditional will upon which divorce depends.
مفعول المشيئة (maf‘ūl al-mashī’ah): The object of the will; that which is willed. Here, it refers to the divorce that is the thing desired by the will.
جزاء المشيئة (jazā’ al-mashī’ah): The consequence of the will; the result that follows if the will is fulfilled. In conditional divorce, it is the divorce that takes place when the condition (the wife's will) is met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a husband says 'shia'tu' (I wished) in response to his wife's statement 'I am divorced if I wish', what is the ruling according to this passage?
A) It counts as a divorce immediately.
B) It does not count unless he says 'shia'tu al-talaq' with the intention of initiating a new divorce.
C) It counts only if said in the same sitting.
D) It is invalid in all cases.

Key Takeaway
When a husband says 'shia'tu' without specifying 'al-talaq' and without intention, no divorce occurs; intention is required to create a new divorce.

Conditional Divorce with Temporal Terms Remains Open Beyond the Session
Ruling with proof: Conditional divorce using 'mata' and 'idha' is not limited to the session.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband uses 'mata shi'ti' or 'idha shi'ti' in conditional divorce and explain why the offer is not limited to the session.

Core Idea
Words like 'whenever you wish' make the divorce offer valid at any future time, not just the sitting.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary addresses a common scenario in conditional divorce: when a husband says 'You are divorced whenever you wish,' must the wife decide immediately? The commentator clarifies that the word 'mata' indicates generality of time, so the offer remains open even after initial rejection. This ruling has important practical consequences for the timing of the wife's acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The husband says 'You are divorced whenever you wish' (mata shi'ti) or similar temporal phrases. The question is whether the wife must accept in the same session or can accept later.
Contrast with 'in shi'ti' (if you wish) without a temporal word, which is limited to the session.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The delegation does not revert by her rejection; it remains valid beyond the session. The wife can accept at any future time.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is linguistic: 'mata' and 'mata ma' are adverbial nouns used for generality of times. The husband's statement is equivalent to 'at any time you wish'.
The dalil is zanni in transmission but clear in meaning.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Because 'mata' inherently indicates an open-ended time range, the condition is not confined to the present session. Therefore, the wife's will can be exercised at any moment.
This reasoning distinguishes it from default offer rules.
5
Address the potential objection
Some may argue that all offers expire after the session. The Hanafi response is that the temporal word overrides the default; the delegation explicitly indicates generality.
The commentary also mentions that intention (niyya) still matters, but the linguistic generality is decisive.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school concerning conditional divorce where the husband says words like 'idha shi'ti' (if you wish) or 'mata shi'ti' (whenever you wish). The commentary expands on the matn, explaining three sub-issues. First, if the wife's conditional will is attached to a non-existent thing (e.g., she says 'I wish if my father wishes'), the divorce does not occur because her present will (al-mashi'ah al-munjazah) is not actualized. However, if she attaches it to an existing condition (e.g., 'I wish if the sky is above us'), the divorce occurs because the condition is met. Second, the passage discusses that when the husband uses words like 'idha' or 'mata', the offer is not restricted to the immediate session (majlis). Even if the wife rejects it initially, she can still accept later. This is because 'mata' indicates generality of time. In contrast, if the husband had used 'in shi'ti' (if you wish) without a temporal word, the offer would be limited to the session. The commentator notes that this difference arises from linguistic analysis: 'mata' and 'mata ma' are adverbial nouns denoting general time, while 'idha' and 'idha ma' also indicate generality but may have nuances. The passage also addresses an objection that such a view is based on distinguishing between the two divorces (object vs. consequence), but the commentator argues that this doesn't hold because the same principle applies whether initially or in response, and the need for intention remains. The ruling is important for understanding the scope of delegation in divorce.

Key Terms
تعليق بمعدوم (ta‘līq bi-ma‘dūm): Suspension of divorce on a non-existent condition, i.e., a condition that has not yet occurred or is impossible.
المشيئة المنجزة (al-mashī’ah al-munjazah): The present, actualized will; the wife's immediate wish that must exist for the conditional divorce to take effect.
الاقتصار على المجلس (al-iqtiṣār ‘alā al-majlis): Limitation of the offer to the same sitting or session. The opposite is that the offer remains open after the session ends.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The divorce occurs because 'mata' indicates generality of time and the offer remains open.
C) The divorce occurs only if she says 'I wish' in the same session.
D) The divorce is invalid because the husband did not specify a time.

Key Takeaway
When a husband uses time-general words like 'mata' or 'idha' in conditional divorce, the wife's option is not limited to the immediate session, and her rejection does not cancel the offer.

Conditional Wish in Tafwid: Nonexistent vs. Existing Conditions
Ruling on conditional wish in tafwid delegation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife attaches her conditional wish (mashi'a) to a nonexistent or existing matter in a delegated divorce and identify the categories of nonexistent.

Core Idea
A conditional wish in tafwid only effects divorce if attached to an existing matter; otherwise, no divorce occurs.

In the chapter on tafwid (delegation of divorce), a key distinction arises based on whether the wife's conditional wish is attached to a nonexistent or an existing matter. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies that a wish conditioned on something nonexistent does not trigger divorce, while a wish conditioned on something existing is immediate (munajjiz) and effects divorce. The commentary expands on the types of 'nonexistent' to prevent misunderstanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation scenario
Recognize that the husband has delegated the power of divorce to the wife, e.g., by saying 'You are divorced if you wish.'
2
Examine the wife's response
The wife must express a wish (mashi'a). However, she may attach a condition to her wish, e.g., 'I wish if X.'
3
Determine whether the condition exists
Check whether the condition X is existent (past or present) or nonexistent at the time of utterance. Nonexistent includes possible, impossible, unknown, etc.
4
Apply the ruling
If X is existent, the wish is immediate (munajjiz) and divorce occurs. If X is nonexistent, the wish is not actualized, so no divorce occurs.
5
Recognize the breadth of 'nonexistent'
Note that the commentator includes all types: nonexistent in reality, impossible, conceptually nonexistent, or unknown to the wife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب التفويض والأمر باليد والمشيئة وكذا كل تعليق بمعدوم، ويقع لو علقت بموجود. [١] قوله : وكذا ... الخ؛ أي إذا قال الزوج : أنت طالق إن شئت، فعلقت في جوابه مشيئتها بأمر معدوم لا يقع الطلاق؛ لأنه علق الطلاق بمشيئتها المنجزة، ولم يوجد، وفي إطلاق المعدوم إشارة إلى تعميمه المعدوم الممكن، والمعدوم المحال، والمعدوم النفس الأمري، والمعدوم بحسب علمها. بخلاف ما إذا علقتها بأمر موجود ماض أو حال، كقولها: شئت إن كانت السماء فوقنا، وشئت إن كان كذا الأمر قد مضى، فإنه حينئذ يقع الطلاق لوجود المشيئة المنجزة، فإن التعليق بشرط كائن تنجيز.
Chapter of Delegation and the Matter of Hand and Will. And similarly, every conditional [wish] attached to a nonexistent [thing] [does not effect divorce], and it falls if attached to an existing [thing]. [1] Statement: 'And similarly... to the end'; meaning that if the husband says 'You are divorced if you wish,' and she in response attaches her wish to a nonexistent matter, the divorce does not occur; because he attached the divorce to her actual wish, and it did not exist. And the mention of 'nonexistent' includes the possible nonexistent, the impossible nonexistent, the nonexistent in itself, and the nonexistent to her knowledge. Contrary to if she attaches it to an existing matter, past or present, such as saying 'I wish if the sky is above us' or 'I wish if such-and-such matter has passed,' then the divorce occurs due to the existence of the actual wish, because the attachment by a condition that is existing is an immediate [delegation].
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the chapter on tafwid (delegation of divorce). The matn states that if the husband says 'You are divorced if you wish,' and the wife responds by attaching her wish to a nonexistent condition (e.g., 'I wish if my father comes' when he is not coming), the divorce does not take effect, because her wish was not actualized. However, if she attaches her wish to an existing condition (e.g., 'I wish if the sky is above the earth'), the divorce occurs immediately because the delegation becomes actualized. The commentator explains that the term 'nonexistent' is broad and includes various categories: the possible (but not yet existent), the impossible (such as a contradiction), the conceptually nonexistent, and that which she does not know. This ruling emphasizes that in Hanafi law, for a conditional wish to effect divorce, the condition must be existent at the time of the wish; otherwise, the divorce does not occur.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation of the power to divorce from the husband to the wife.
مشيئة (mashi'a): The wife's expressed wish or will to effect the delegated divorce.
تعليق (ta'liq): Attaching the divorce to a condition (here, the wife's wish conditioned on something).
منجز (munajjiz): Immediate, actualized; a wish that is not conditional on future events.
معدوم (ma'dum): Nonexistent; can refer to possible, impossible, conceptually nonexistent, or unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A conditional wish in tafwid only effects divorce if the condition is existent at the time of the wish; otherwise, no divorce occurs.

Scope of Delegation with General Time Words: Rejection Does Not Revoke
Ruling on scope of delegation with general time words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tafwid using 'idha' and 'mata' and explain why the wife's rejection does not revoke the delegation.

Core Idea
When the husband uses 'idha' or 'mata' for delegation, the wife's initial refusal does not revoke her option.

This passage addresses the scope of delegation (tafwid) in divorce. The matn rules that if the husband says 'You are divorced if/whenever you wish' using general time words, the wife's rejection before she expresses her wish does not cancel the delegation. The commentary explains the linguistic reasoning behind this ruling and the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over the interpretation of 'idha'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expressions and the ruling
The matn states that using 'idha shi'ti', 'idha ma shi'ti', 'mata shi'ti', or 'mata ma shi'ti', the delegation is not revoked by the wife's rejection. It continues until she actually wishes.
2
Understand the default limitation to the session
Normally, if delegation is expressed without words of generality, it is limited to the session (majlis). The wife must accept immediately. The use of general time words extends the option beyond the session.
The default is that delegation expires with the session unless stated otherwise.
3
Analyze the linguistic difference between 'mata' and 'idha'
'Mata' is an adverbial noun meaning 'whenever', implying generality of time. The Sahibayn treat 'idha' similarly. Abu Hanifa normally treats 'idha' as conditional (like 'in'), but in this context ambiguity is resolved in favor of generality.
The passage notes that 'idha' can function both as a conditional and as a temporal adverb.
4
Evaluate the dispute and its resolution
Because 'idha' is ambiguous, we apply the default principle that delegation continues unless there is certainty of limitation. Thus, the mufta bihi follows the Sahibayn's view that 'idha' is like 'mata'.
The default principle: al-asl adam al-qat' (the default is no cutting off).
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
If the wife rejects initially, the delegation remains. She can later wish and effect divorce. This contrasts with expressions like 'in shi'ti' which would limit to the session.
The thamarah al-khilaf: limitation vs. continuity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : أنت طالق إذا شئت، أو إذا ما شئت، أو متى شئت، أو متى ما شئت لا يرتد الأمر بردها. فساده أما أولاً : فلأنّ هذا الوجه مبني على إثباتِ الفرق بين الطلاقين، وقد عرفت وأما ثانياً : فلأنه لا يتمشى فيما لو قال ابتداء : شئت الطلاق، مع أنه أيضاً محتاج إلى النية أيضاً ، فالوجه في اشتراط النيّة هو أحد الوجهين اللذين ذكرناهما سابقاً. [٢] قوله : لا يرتد الأمر بردّها ؛ يعني في هذه الصور لا يقتصر التفويض على المجلس ولو ردّت لم يرتد ، بل يبقى له الخيار بعد الردّ أيضاً ؛ لأنه لم يملكها مطلقاً، ولا في وقت خاص، بل في الوقت الذي شاءت ، فلا يعتبر بردّها قبل المشيئة. والوجه في ذلك : أنّ كلمة «متى» و «متى ما» من أسماء الظروف تستعمل لعموم الأوقات، فكأنه قال : في أي وقت شئت، فلا يقتصر على المجلس، فإنّ الاقتصار عليه إنما هو إذا لم يأت في تفويضه بما يدلّ على العموم، وأما كلمة «إذا» و«إذا ما» فهما مثل: «متى» و «متى ما» عندهما ، فحكمهما حكمهما ، سواء بسواء، نعم هما عند أبي حنيفة للشرط مثل : «إن»؛ ولذا قال في قوله : «إذا لم أطلقك فأنت طالق»، إنّه لا يقع الطلاق إلا في آخر العمر. فيردّ عليه : أنّه لِمَ لم يجعلهما في بحث المشيئة مثل : «إن»، حتى يتقيد التفويض به بالمجلس. ويجاب عنه على ما في «التوضيح» و«التلويح» وغيرهما : إنّ «إذا» لما كانت مستعملة في كلامهم تارة للشرط وتارة للظرف كمتى لاحظنا كل من استعماليه ، وحكمنا على حبسه ، ففي قوله : «إذا لم أطلقك فأنت طالق»، إن حمل على «متى» يقع الطلاق في الحال، وإن حمل على «إن» يقع عند الموت، والأصل عدم الطلاق ، فلا يقع بالشك. فلذلك حكمنا أن حكمه هناك حكم «إن»، وهاهنا إن حمل على «إن» انقطع تعلق المشيئة بما وراء المجلس بعدما ثبت بكلمةِ التفويض ، وإن حمل على «متى» لا ينقطع ، والأصلُ عدم الانقطاع ، فلا ينقطع بالشك، فلذلك قلنا : إنّه مثل «متى»، وقد مر نبد مما يتعلق بهذا البحث سابقاً.
And in [the expression] 'You are divorced if you wish, or if ever you wish, or whenever you wish, or whenever you wish,' the matter is not revoked by her rejection. [A objection:] Its invalidity: Firstly, because this view is based on establishing a difference between the two divorces, and you have known... Secondly, because it does not apply when he says initially 'I wish divorce,' while it also requires intention, so the correct view in requiring intention is one of the two views we mentioned earlier. [2] Statement: 'The matter is not revoked by her rejection'; meaning in these forms the delegation is not limited to the session, and if she rejects it is not revoked, rather he retains the option after rejection as well; because he did not grant her ownership absolutely, nor at a specific time, but at the time she wishes, so her rejection before the wish is not considered. The reasoning is that the word 'mata' and 'mata ma' are among the adverbial nouns used for the generality of times, so it is as if he said 'at any time you wish', so it is not limited to the session. Limitation to the session only applies when he does not include in his delegation something indicating generality. As for the word 'idha' and 'idha ma', they are like 'mata' and 'mata ma' according to them (the two Sahibayn), so their ruling is the same. However, according to Abu Hanifa, they are for condition like 'in'; thus he said in his statement 'If I do not divorce you, then you are divorced' that the divorce only occurs at the end of life. An objection is raised: Why did he not treat them in the context of wish like 'in' so that the delegation would be restricted to the session? It is answered according to what is in 'al-Tawdih' and 'al-Talwih' etc.: Since 'idha' is used in their speech sometimes for condition and sometimes for adverb like 'mata', we considered both usages and ruled upon its ambiguity. So in his statement 'If I do not divorce you...' if interpreted as 'mata' the divorce occurs immediately, if interpreted as 'in' it occurs at death, and the default is no divorce, so it does not occur due to doubt. Therefore we ruled that its ruling there is like 'in'. Here, if interpreted as 'in' the connection of the wish beyond the session is cut off after being established by the word of delegation, and if interpreted as 'mata' it is not cut off, and the default is no cutting off, so it is not cut off due to doubt. Thus we say it is like 'mata'. And there has passed some of what relates to this discussion previously.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage deals with the scope of delegation when the husband uses expressions indicating general time. The matn first states that when the husband says 'You are divorced if you wish' using words like 'idha' or 'mata', the wife's rejection before she expresses her wish does not revoke the husband's delegation; it remains until she actually wishes. The commentator then explains that words like 'mata' (whenever) are adverbial nouns implying generality of time, so the delegation is not confined to the session of the offer. In contrast, if the husband used 'in' (if), the delegation would be limited to the session. There is a difference of opinion between Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the word 'idha'. According to Abu Hanifa, 'idha' is primarily used for condition (like 'in'), whereas the two Sahibayn consider it similar to 'mata' for time. In the context of delegation (mashi'a), the commentators note that since the word is ambiguous, we apply the default principle: if it can be interpreted as either, we choose the interpretation that preserves the continuity of the delegation (i.e., not limited to the session), because the default is that delegation continues unless there is certainty of limitation. Thus, in this context, 'idha' is treated like 'mata'.

Key Terms
إذا (idha): A particle used both for condition (like 'if') and for temporal adverb (like 'when'). In this text, its interpretation is disputed.
متى (mata): Temporal adverb meaning 'whenever', implying generality of time.
مجلس (majlis): The session or meeting in which the delegation is offered; limitation to the session means the wife must accept in that same gathering.
رد (radd): Rejection; the wife's refusal of the delegation before she expresses her wish.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'You are divorced whenever you wish' (using متى شئت) and the wife initially says 'I don't want it,' what is the ruling?
A) The delegation is revoked and she cannot later divorce herself.
B) The delegation remains valid and she can still divorce herself later.
C) The husband must renew the offer for it to be valid.
D) The wife's rejection ends the matter permanently.

Key Takeaway
Delegation with general time words like 'idha' and 'mata' continues beyond the session, and the wife's rejection does not revoke it.

Delegated Divorce Wording: One Divorce vs. Multiple with 'Kullama'
Ruling on number of delegated divorces based on wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings for 'mata shi't' and 'kullama shi't' in delegated divorce and determine the number of divorces the wife can effect in each case.

Core Idea
With 'mata shi't' she gets one divorce; with 'kullama shi't' she gets multiple, but sequentially.

This passage clarifies a critical distinction in delegated divorce (tafwid al-talaq). The ruling depends on the wording used by the husband. Words indicating general time but not repeated actions yield a single divorce, while the word 'kullama' implies repetition, allowing multiple sequential divorces. Understanding this nuance is essential for correct fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two key phrases
The passage contrasts 'mata shi't' (when you wish) with 'kullama shi't' (whenever you wish). Both delegate the power to divorce to the wife, but the number of divorces differs.
2
Analyze the linguistic implication
'Mata' and similar words indicate generality of time (any time) but not repetition of actions. 'Kullama' indicates both generality of time and repetition of action (every time).
This is the 'illa (reason) for the different rulings.
3
Apply the ruling to each phrase
With 'mata', the wife can divorce only once at any time she chooses. With 'kullama', she can divorce repeatedly, one after another.
For a free woman, up to three divorces; for a slave, up to two.
4
Note the restriction on simultaneity
With 'kullama', she cannot effect all three divorces at once. The phrase implies successive actions, not simultaneous.
If she says 'I divorce myself three times', only one divorce occurs.
5
Consider the status of the wife
The maximum number of divorces depends on whether she is free (three) or a slave (two). This is important for applying the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتطلق متى شاءت واحدةً لا غير، وفي : كُلما شئت لها إيقاع واحدة، ثمّ وثم، لا الثلاث جميعاً [١] قوله : واحدة؛ يعني في هذه الصور لا يكون الطلاق مقتصراً على المجلس، بل يقع عليها في أي وقت شاءت، لكن لا يقع إلا واحد لا أزيد منه، فإنّ هذه الكلمات إنما وضعت لعموم الأزمان، ولا دلالة لها على عموم الأفعال، فتملك التطليق في أي زمان شاءت، ولا تملك تطليقاً بعد تطليق، بخلاف: «كلما شئت»، فإنّ «كلما» يوجب تكرّر الأفعال كما يفيد عموم الأزمان، فلها أن تطلق نفسها واحدة بعد واحدة حتى تطلق نفسها ثلاثا إن كانت حرّة. [٢] قوله : لا الثلاث جميعاً؛ أي ليس لها في قوله : «أنت طالق كلما شئت» أن تطلق نفسها ثلاثا إن كانت حرّة.
And she divorces when she wishes once only. And in: 'Whenever you wish' she has the power to effect one [divorce], then [another], then [another], not all three at once. [1] Statement: 'once'; meaning in these forms the divorce is not restricted to the session, but it occurs upon her at any time she wishes, but it occurs only once, not more, because these words are only for the generality of times, and they do not indicate the generality of actions. So she owns the power to divorce at any time she wishes, but she does not own a divorce after a divorce. Contrary to 'kullama shi't' (whenever you wish), because 'kullama' necessitates repetition of actions as it indicates generality of times, so she can divorce herself one after another until she divorces herself three times if she is a free woman. [2] Statement: 'not all three at once'; meaning that in his statement 'You are divorced whenever you wish' she does not have the power to divorce herself three times at once, even if she is a free woman.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage clarifies the number of divorces a woman can effect under different delegation phrases. When the husband says 'You are divorced when you wish' using words like 'mata' or 'idha', the wife can divorce herself only once, because these words indicate general time but not repeated actions. She can choose any time but only one divorce. However, if he says 'kullama shi't' (whenever you wish), the word 'kullama' implies repetition of the action (every time she wishes), so she can divorce herself one at a time, and if she is free (not a slave), she can do so up to three times, but she cannot do all three at once because the phrase implies successive actions, not simultaneous. The commentator emphasizes that with 'mata' she owns only one divorce, while with 'kullama' she owns multiple but must be done sequentially.

Key Terms
كلما (kullama): A word meaning 'every time' or 'whenever', implying repetition of the action.
التطليق (al-tatliq): The act of divorcing; here, the wife's exercise of the delegated power.
حرّة (hurrah): A free woman as opposed to a slave; a free woman can be divorced up to three times (for a valid revocable divorce).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) She can effect one divorce, then another, up to three, but not all three at once.

Key Takeaway
The number of divorces in delegated divorce depends on the wording: 'mata' yields one, 'kullama' yields multiple sequential divorces.

Delegation of Divorce: Rulings for Temporal, Place, and Conditional Phrases
The passage explains rulings for tafwid using different Arabic phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the legal effects of different phrases used in delegation of divorce (tafwid) and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the word 'idha'.

Core Idea
In delegation of divorce, wording determines scope: 'mata' authorizes one divorce, 'kullama' sequential divorces, and 'idha' triggers a dispute over whether it is conditional or temporal.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies how a husband's delegation of divorce to his wife is interpreted based on the Arabic phrase he uses. The commentary distinguishes between temporal words like 'mata' and 'kullama', place words like 'haythu' and 'ayna', and the conditional 'idha'. Each phrase yields a different scope of the wife's authority. The passage also addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn over the nature of 'idha' and its application in both conditional divorce and delegation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegation phrase
Determine which word the husband used: mata, kullama, idha, haythu, ayna, or kayfa. Each has a distinct legal effect.
The husband must use clear wording; the wife's authority is limited by the phrase.
2
Classify the phrase type
Is it temporal (mata, kullama), place-based (haythu, ayna), or conditional (idha)? The classification determines the scope of the wife's power.
Kullama implies repetition of both time and action; mata implies time only.
3
Apply the default Hanafi rule
For mata: one divorce at any time. For kullama: one divorce after another, up to three. For haythu/ayna/kayfa: restricted to the council. For idha in delegation: treated like mata to preserve the delegation.
The exception: if the husband uses idha in a conditional divorce clause, Abu Hanifa treats it as conditional (divorce at death), while Sahibayn treat it as temporal (immediate).
4
Analyze the ikhtilaf over idha
In the statement 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced' (idha lam utalliqka...), Abu Hanifa holds the divorce occurs at death; Sahibayn hold it is immediate. The rationale: 'idha' can be conditional or temporal; the original is absence of divorce, so doubt prevents it (Abu Hanifa) or favors immediate (Sahibayn).
In delegation, the same word 'idha' is interpreted as temporal to avoid cutting the wife's authority after the council.
5
Note practical consequences
The difference affects timing: in conditional divorce, Abu Hanifa delays until death; Sahibayn trigger immediately. In delegation, all three treat 'idha' like 'mata' to maintain the wife's ongoing power.
The commentary explains why the ikhtilaf does not carry over to delegation: the default is continuation, not restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتطلق متى شاءت واحدةً لا غير ، وفي : كُلما شئت لها إيقاع واحدة، ثمّ وثم ، لا الثلاث جميعاً لأنه ملكها الطلاق في الوقت الذي شاءَت ، فلم يكن تمليكاً قبل المشيئة حتّى يرتد بالرد ، وتطلق متى شاءت واحدة لا غير. وفي : كُلّما شئت لها إيقاع واحدة ، ثم وثم) ؛ لأن كلمة : كلما ؛ تعم الأفعال كما تَعُمُّ الأزمان، (لا الثلاث " جميعاً مثل: «متى» و «متى ما عندهما ، فحكمهما حكمهما ، سواء بسواء، نعم هما عند أبي حنيفة الا الله للشرط مثل : «إن»؛ ولذا قال في قوله : «إذا لم أطلقك فأنت طالق»، إنّه لا يقع الطلاق إلا في آخر العمر. فيردّ عليه : أنّه لِمَ لم يجعلهما في بحث المشيئة مثل : «إن»، حتى يتقيد التفويض به بالمجلس. ويجاب عنه علــي مـا في «التوضيح» و«التلويح» وغيرهما : إنّ «إذا» لما كانت مستعملة في كلامهم تارة للشرط كان وتارة للظرف كمتى لاحظنا كلا. من استعماليه ، وحكمنا على حبسه ، ففي قوله : «إذا لم أطلقك فأنت طالق»، إن حمل على «متى» يقع الطلاق في الحال، وإن حمل على «إن» يقع عند الموت، والأصل عدم الطلاق ، فلا يقع بالشك. فلذلك حكمنا أن حكمه هناك حكم «إن»، وهاهنا إن حمل على «إن انقطع تعلق المشيئة بما وراء المجلس بعدما ثبت بكلمةِ التفويض ، وإن حمل على «متى» لا ينقطع ، والأصلُ عدم الانقطاع ، فلا ينقطع بالشك، فلذلك قلنا : إنّه مثل «متى»، وقد مر نبد مما يتعلق بهذا البحث سابقاً. [١]قوله : واحدة ؛ يعني في هذه الصور لا يكون الطلاق مقتصراً على المجلس، بل يقع عليها في أي وقت شاءت ، لكن لا يقعُ إلا واحد لا أزيد منه ، فإنّ هذه الكلمات إنما وضعت لعموم الأزمان، ولا دلالة لها على عموم الأفعال، فتملك التطليق في أي زمان شاءت، ولا تملك تطليقاً بعد تطليق ، بخلاف: «كلما شئت»، فإنّ «كلما» يوجبُ تكرّر الأفعال كما يفيد عموم الأزمان، فلها أن تطلق نفسها واحدة بعد واحدة حتى تطلق نفسها ثلاثا إن كانت حرّة. [٢]قوله : لا الثلاث جميعاً ؛ أي ليس لها في قوله : «أنت طالق كلّما شئت» أن تطلق نفسها ثلاثاً معاً، بأن تقول : طلقت نفسي ثلاثاً أو تقول : طلقت نفسي واحدة وواحدة وواحدة ؛ لأنّ كلمة كلّما إنّما تثبت عموم الأفراد لا عموم الاجتماع، فلا تملك الاجتماع ، نعم تملك إيقاع الثلاث متفرقاً في أزمنة متفرقة. [1]قوله : ولا التطليق . الخ؛ يعني لها إذا قال زوجها : أنت طالق كلّما شئت أن تطلق نفسها بعد زوج آخر بأن نكحت بزوج آخر بعد انقضاء العدة من طلاقِ الأول، ثم عادت إلى الزوج الأول بعد طلاق الثاني أو موته وانقضاء عدته، فليس لها حينئذ أن تطلق بكلمته السابقة ؛ وذلك لأن التعليق ينصرف إلى الملك القائم، ولا يتجاوز العموم عنه ، والملك بعد زوج آخر ملك مستحدث. كذا في «العناية)) (۳) [٢] قوله : يتقيّد بالمجلس ؛ وذلك لأنّ «حيث) و «أين» للمكان، ولا تعلّق للطلاق به؛ ولذا كان قوله : أنت طالق بمكة أو في مكة تنجيز ، أو قد يستعملان للشرط مجازاً ، فيقومان مقام «أن»، فيتقيد التفويض به في المجلس. فإن قلت : لَمَّا لم يكن الطلاق متعلّقاً بالمكان، وكان أصل وضعهما للمكان، فلم جعلا للشرط ؟ قلت : لأنّ الظرف قد يستعمل مجازاً في الشرط، فحملا عليه حذراً عن إلغائهما بالكلية، وإنّما حمل على أن دون غيره من كلماتِ الشرط ؛ لكونه أصلاً في الشرط. كذا في «الفتح». [٣]قوله : وفي كيف شئت ... الخ ؛ اعلم أنّ كيف في الأصل للاستفهام : أي
And she divorces, whenever she wishes, one divorce only, not more. And in [the phrase] 'whenever you wish', she has the power to effect one divorce, then another, then another, not all three together. Because he granted her ownership of divorce at the time she wished, so it was not an ownership before the wish such that it could be rejected by refusal. And she divorces, whenever she wishes, one divorce only. And in [the phrase] 'whenever you wish', she has the power to effect one divorce, then another, then another, because the word 'kullama' encompasses actions just as it encompasses times. (Not all three together). Like 'mata' and 'mata ma' according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad], so their ruling is the same as theirs, identically. Yes, both of them according to Abu Hanifa are for condition like 'in'. Hence he said regarding his statement: 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced', that the divorce does not occur except at the end of life. So it is objected against him: Why did he not make them in the discussion of delegation like 'in' so that delegation is restricted to the council? And it is answered as in 'al-Tawdih' and 'al-Talwih' and others: Because 'idha' is used in their speech sometimes for condition and sometimes as an adverb like 'mata'. We considered both of its usages and adjudicated based on its circumstance. So in his statement: 'If I do not divorce you, you are divorced', if it is taken as 'mata', divorce occurs immediately; if taken as 'in', it occurs at death. The original is the absence of divorce, so it does not occur due to doubt. Therefore we judged that its ruling there is the ruling of 'in'. And here, if taken as 'in', the connection of the delegation to beyond the council is cut after it was established by the word of delegation; and if taken as 'mata', it is not cut. The original is the lack of being cut, so it is not cut due to doubt. Therefore we said that it is like 'mata'. And a portion of what pertains to this discussion has passed earlier. [1] His statement: 'one' means that in these images the divorce is not restricted to the council; rather, it occurs upon her at any time she wishes, but only one divorce, not more than that. Because these words are placed for the generality of times, and they have no indication of the generality of actions. So she owns the divorce at any time she wishes, but she does not own a divorce after a divorce, unlike 'kullama shi'ta', for 'kullama' requires repetition of actions as it conveys generality of times. So she can divorce herself one after another until she divorces herself three times if she is free. [2] His statement: 'not all three together' means that she does not have the right, in his statement 'you are divorced whenever you wish', to divorce herself three times together by saying 'I divorce myself three times' or 'I divorce myself once and once and once', because the word 'kullama' establishes the generality of individuals, not the generality of simultaneous occurrence. So she does not have the right of simultaneous occurrence; rather, she has the right to effect three divorces separately in separate times. [1] His statement: 'nor divorce after another husband' etc. means that if her husband says to her 'you are divorced whenever you wish', she cannot divorce herself after another husband, i.e., if she marries another husband after completing the waiting period from the first divorce, then returns to the first husband after the second husband's divorce or death and completion of his waiting period, then she cannot divorce by his previous statement. Because the condition is directed to the existing marriage, and the generality does not extend beyond it, and the marriage after another husband is a newly created marriage. Thus in 'al-Inayah'. [2] His statement: 'is restricted to the council' because 'haythu' and 'ayna' are for place, and divorce has no connection to place. Hence, if he says 'you are divorced in Makkah' or 'in Makkah', it is immediate. Or they may be used metaphorically for condition, so they stand in place of 'an', and thus the delegation is restricted to the council. If you say: Since divorce is not connected to place, and their original usage is for place, why are they taken for condition? I say: Because the adverb may be used metaphorically for condition, so they are taken to it, cautious against completely cancelling them. And they are taken to 'an' rather than other conditional words because it is the original in condition. Thus in 'al-Fath'. [3] His statement: 'and in kayfa shi'ta' etc. Know that 'kayfa' originally is for interrogation, meaning...
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the rulings of delegating divorce to the wife using various temporal, place, and manner phrases. The author begins by stating the ruling for 'mata shi'ta' (when you wish): the wife can divorce herself once at any time, not limited to the council. The phrase 'kullama shi'ta' (whenever you wish) is different: it allows her to divorce one after another, up to three if she is free, but she cannot pronounce all three divorces at once. The reason is that 'kullama' conveys repetition of actions as well as times, so each divorce is a separate act. The text then compares 'mata' and 'mata ma'—according to Abu Hanifa, these function as conditional particles like 'in', while according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, they are temporal adverbs like 'mata'. This leads to a discussion of the word 'idha' in the husband's statement 'if I do not divorce you, you are divorced'. Abu Hanifa treats 'idha' as conditional, so the divorce occurs only at the end of his life if he never divorces her; the two companions treat it as a time adverb, making the divorce immediate. The commentator then responds to an objection: why not treat 'idha' in delegation as conditional, restricting it to the council? The answer distinguishes contexts: in a conditional divorce clause, the condition is uncertain, so ambiguity prevents the divorce; in delegation, the wife has been given the power, and treating 'idha' as conditional would cut off that power beyond the council, but since the original state is continuation, it is not cut off, so 'idha' is treated like 'mata'. The commentary then explains that for 'mata' and similar words, the wife divorces once only, not repeatedly. For 'kullama', she can repeat one after another. She cannot divorce after a second marriage because the delegation is tied to the existing marriage. For 'haythu shi'ta' and 'ayna shi'ta' (wherever you wish), the delegation is restricted to the council because these are place words; they are interpreted as conditional to avoid being meaningless, and the council is the only context where the condition can be fulfilled. For 'kayfa shi'ta' (how you wish), the ruling is the same as for place words: restricted to the council, because 'kayfa' is originally for interrogation, and here it is taken as conditional. Throughout, the commentary relies on standard Hanafi sources like al-Inayah, al-Fath, al-Tawdih, and al-Talwih.

Key Terms
التفويض (al-tafwid): Delegation of the power of divorce from husband to wife.
كلما (kullama): A word indicating repetition of both time and action; used here meaning 'whenever'.
متى (mata): A word meaning 'when', used as a time adverb; in delegation, it allows one divorce at any time.
إذا (idha): In this context, a word that can be conditional (like in) or temporal (like mata).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling for 'haythu shi'ta' (wherever you wish) in delegation of divorce?
A) The wife can divorce at any time and place.
B) The wife can divorce only within the council (majlis).
C) The wife cannot divorce at all because place is irrelevant.
D) The wife can divorce only once, like 'mata'.

Key Takeaway
The scope of a wife's authority in delegated divorce is determined by the precise Arabic phrase used, with 'mata' for one divorce, 'kullama' for sequential divorces, and place words restricted to the council.

Delegation Does Not Apply After a Second Marriage
Ruling that delegation is invalid after a second marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a delegation of divorce expires with the first marriage and apply it to a scenario involving remarriage.

Core Idea
A husband's delegation of divorce is tied to the current marriage; a new marriage is a separate ownership.

When a husband tells his wife 'You are divorced whenever you wish,' he delegates the power of divorce to her. But does that permission last forever, even after she remarries and returns to him? In this passage, the Hanafi school clarifies that the delegation is limited to the first marriage only. Once the marriage ends and a new marriage contract is formed, the former delegation no longer applies. Let's examine the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The matn states 'ولا التطليق بعد زوج آخر' – the wife does not have the right to divorce herself after a second marriage. Confirm that the ruling is about invalidity of divorce under the previous delegation.
The ruling is batil (ineffective), not haram or fasid.
2
Understand the Scenario
The scenario involves a wife who was given a general delegation, divorced, married another, then returned to the first husband after the second marriage ended. The delegation is the same verbal permission from the first marriage.
The delegation is a conditional statement (ta'liq), not an independent contract.
3
Analyze the Reason (Illah)
The reason given is that 'التعليق ينصرف إلى الملك القائم' – the conditional delegation refers only to the existing marriage ownership (milk qaim). A new marriage is a newly created ownership (milk mustahdath), so the delegation does not carry over.
This is a rational (dalil aqli) proof based on the nature of delegation.
4
Apply the Ruling
If a similar situation arises, conclude that the wife cannot use the old delegation. She would need a new delegation from the husband in the new marriage.
The husband could delegate anew if he wishes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا التطليق بعد زوج آخر) ؛ فقوله : ولا التطليق بالرفع عطف على الايقاع المضاف إلى الثلاث، تقديره : ليس لها ايقاعُ الثَّلاث جميعاً ، ولا التطليق. [1]قوله : ولا التطليق . الخ؛ يعني لها إذا قال زوجها : أنت طالق كلّما شئت أن تطلق نفسها بعد زوج آخر بأن نكحت بزوج آخر بعد انقضاء العدة من طلاقِ الأول، ثم عادت إلى الزوج الأول بعد طلاق الثاني أو موته وانقضاء عدته، فليس لها حينئذ أن تطلق بكلمته السابقة ؛ وذلك لأن التعليق ينصرف إلى الملك القائم، ولا يتجاوز العموم عنه ، والملك بعد زوج آخر ملك مستحدث. كذا في «العناية»
And no divorce after a second husband. So his statement: 'and no divorce' in nominative is coordinated with 'the effecting' added to 'three', its meaning: she does not have the effecting of all three together, and no divorce. [1] His statement: 'and no divorce', etc.; meaning, she has, when her husband said: 'You are divorced whenever you wish' that she may divorce herself after a second husband, such that she married another husband after completion of the waiting period from the first divorce, then returned to the first husband after the second husband's divorce or death and completion of his waiting period, then she does not have at that time to divorce with his previous statement; and that is because the conditional delegation refers to the existing ownership, and does not extend beyond it in generality, and the ownership after a second husband is a newly created ownership. Thus in al-Inayah.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage contains a ruling from the chapter on delegation (tawfeeḍ). The matn states: 'ولا التطليق بعد زوج آخر' meaning that the wife does not have the right to divorce herself after a second marriage. The commentator explains that the phrase 'ولا التطليق' is in the nominative case, connected to 'الإيقاع' in the previous line, and the meaning is that she does not have the power to effect all three divorces at once, nor does she have the power to effect divorce after a second marriage. The reason is that the husband's statement 'you are divorced whenever you wish' is a conditional delegation that attaches only to the current marriage (ملك قائم). When the marriage ends by divorce, and she marries another and then returns to the first husband, a new marriage contract is formed, which is a newly created ownership (ملك مستحدث). The original delegation does not extend to this new marriage because it was specifically directed to the first ownership. Therefore, she cannot use that previous permission to divorce herself in the new marriage.

Key Terms
التطليق (al-tatliq): The act of divorcing (the wife herself, in the context of delegation).
التعليق (al-ta'liq): A conditional statement or delegation; here, the husband's utterance delegating the power of divorce to the wife.
الملك القائم (al-milk al-qa'im): The existing marriage ownership (i.e., the current marital bond).
ملك مستحدث (milk mustahdath): A newly created ownership; here, the new marriage contract after remarriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the permission is general.
B) No, because the delegation expires with the first marriage.
C) Yes, if she did not use it before.
D) No, because the second husband's permission is needed.

Key Takeaway
A delegation of divorce is specific to the marriage in which it was given; it does not carry over to a subsequent marriage after remarriage.

Restriction of Delegation to the Council for Place Words
Ruling that 'wherever you wish' is restricted to the council

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband uses place words in talaq delegation and explain the legal reasoning for restricting the wife's option to the council.

Core Idea
Place words in delegation are restricted to the council because they are metaphorical conditions, not literal places.

In this passage, the commentator explains a subtle point of talaq delegation. When a husband says 'wherever you wish' (حيث شئت) or 'anywhere you wish' (أين شئت), the wife's choice is not unlimited; it is confined to the council where the words were spoken. This ruling is derived from the literal meaning of these words and the need to avoid nullification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The husband's delegation (tafwid) using place words. The ruling: the wife's option is restricted to the council.
The act is the husband's utterance, not the wife's choice.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The primary meaning of 'حيث' and 'أين' as place words, and the metaphorical use as condition.
The proof is rational (dalil aqli) based on lexical analysis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this prove the restriction? Since divorce has no connection to place, the words are interpreted metaphorically as condition, and to avoid nullification, the condition is linked to the council.
If the words were taken literally, they would be meaningless in the context of divorce.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Does any school disagree? The Hanafi position (mukhtalaf fih) contrasts with schools that may not impose the council restriction.
The text does not name opponents explicitly.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The Hanafi position as recorded in al-Fath and accepted by later scholars is the mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي : حيث شئت، وأين شئت، يتقيد بالمجلس) [٢] قوله : يتقيّد بالمجلس ؛ وذلك لأنّ «حيث) و «أين» للمكان، ولا تعلّق للطلاق به؛ ولذا كان قوله : أنت طالق بمكة أو في مكة تنجيز ، أو قد يستعملان للشرط مجازاً ، فيقومان مقام «أن»، فيتقيد التفويض به في المجلس. فإن قلت : لَمَّا لم يكن الطلاق متعلّقاً بالمكان، وكان أصل وضعهما للمكان، فلم جعلا للشرط ؟ قلت : لأنّ الظرف قد يستعمل مجازاً في الشرط، فحملا عليه حذراً عن إلغائهما بالكلية، وإنّما حمل على أن دون غيره من كلماتِ الشرط ؛ لكونه أصلاً في الشرط. كذا في «الفتح». (۱) حتى لو قامت من مجلسها فلا مشيئة لها ؛ لأنهما اسمان للمكان والطلاق لا تعلق له بالمكان فيلغو، ويجعل مجازا عن إن الشرطية بخلاف متى وإذا . ينظر : (الدر المنتقى»(١ : ٤١٥)
And in 'wherever you wish' and 'anywhere you wish,' it is restricted to the council. [2] His statement: 'it is restricted to the council'; and that is because 'حيث' and 'أين' are for place, and divorce has no connection to it; therefore his statement: 'You are divorced in Makkah' or 'in Makkah' is an immediate divorce, or they may be used for condition metaphorically, so they stand in the place of 'أن', so the delegation is restricted to it in the council. If you say: Since divorce is not related to place, and their original usage is for place, why were they made for condition? I say: Because a particle of place may be used metaphorically for condition, so they are carried upon it to avoid completely nullifying them, and they are carried upon 'أن' rather than other condition words because it is the original for condition. Thus in al-Fath. (1) Even if she leaves her council, she has no option; because they are two names for place and divorce has no connection to place, so it becomes void and is made metaphorical for 'أن' the conditional, unlike 'متى' and 'إذا'. See: al-Durr al-Muntaqa (1:415).
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling when a husband uses place words in delegation. The matn states that when he says 'wherever you wish' or 'anywhere you wish,' the wife's choice is restricted to the current council (majlis). The commentator explains that the words 'حيث' and 'أين' are primarily for location, and divorce is not inherently connected to place. If the husband says 'you are divorced in Makkah,' it is an immediate divorce, not a delegation. However, when used in the context of delegation, these words are taken metaphorically as conditional 'if' (أن). To avoid making them meaningless, they are interpreted as condition, but since they are place words, the condition is restricted to the council. If she leaves the council, she loses the option. This is different from words like 'متى' (whenever) and 'إذا' (if), which are not restricted.

Key Terms
حيث (haythu): A word indicating place; in this context, used metaphorically for condition, restricting the delegation to the council.
أين (ayna): A word indicating place; similar to 'حيث' in this ruling.
المجلس (al-majlis): The council or gathering where the husband's statement was made.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) She can choose even after leaving the council.
D) She must choose before the husband leaves.

Key Takeaway
When a husband uses place words in delegation, the wife's choice is restricted to the council to preserve the metaphorical meaning of the words.

The Delegation 'How You Wish': Navigating Intentions in Talaq
Detailed ruling on the delegation phrase 'how you wish' based on spousal intentions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on the delegation phrase 'how you wish' to scenarios with different intentions and determine the resulting mode of divorce.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'divorce yourself as you wish,' the wife chooses the mode, but if it conflicts with his hidden intention, both fall: a single revocable remains.

The phrase 'how you wish' (كيف شئت) in talaq delegation is a classic case illustrating the interplay between spousal intention and the limits of delegation. The Hanafi school provides a precise framework: the wife can decide the mode of divorce, but only if her choice aligns with the husband's unspoken intent. When they clash, the law defaults to the minimum — one revocable divorce — preserving the origin of the act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the delegated element
Determine whether the wife is delegated the act itself or only its manner (kayfiyyah). In 'how you wish,' the origin (asl) of divorce is already given; only the manner is delegated.
This distinction is crucial because if the origin were delegated, non-choice might prevent divorce entirely.
2
Check if the wife makes a choice
If the wife does not will any specific manner, the divorce falls as one revocable (the minimum). If she does choose, proceed to step 3.
The default is the least form of divorce.
3
Compare wife's choice with husband's intention
If the wife's chosen manner matches the husband's intention (e.g., both intend triple), the divorce occurs accordingly. If they conflict, proceed to step 4.
The husband's intention is often hidden; it becomes operative only when it matches or contradicts the wife's explicit choice.
4
Apply the conflict rule
When intentions conflict, both are set aside (yatasāqaṭān). The origin remains, and the divorce occurs in the least form: one revocable divorce.
This preserves the validity of the divorce while avoiding either party's desired extreme.
5
Understand the rationale (talīl)
Intention is needed here because the delegated matter is the 'state of divorce' (حال الطلاق), which varies between revocable, irrevocable, and count. Unlike delegations that only involve whether an act occurs, this requires specification.
The dalīl is a rational proof (dalīl aqlī): the diversity of the delegated matter necessitates intention for specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣]قوله : وفي كيف شئت ... الخ ؛ اعلم أنّ كيف في الأصل للاستفهام : أي السؤال عن الحال، يقال : كيف أصبحت أي ما كان حالك عند الصباح، وقد تجرد عن معنى الاستفهام، وتستعمل بمعنى الكيفية والحال كما في قولهم : أنت طالق كيف ، أيّ على أية كيفية وحال شئت. وقيل : إنها في الأصل بمنزلة : أي الاستفهاميّة ؛ لأنّ معنـى كـيـف شـئـت عـنـد الاستفهام : أي حال شئت فاستعيرت لأي الموصولة على معنى أنت طالق بأية كيفية شئت من الكيفيات. [١]قوله : تقع رجعية ؛ حاصله : أنه إذا قال الزوج : أنت طالق كيف شئت، فلا يخلو إما أن توجد مشيئتها لشيء من الكيفيات أو لم توجد، فإن لم توجد بأن لم تشأ شيئاً من كيفيات الطلاق تقعُ واحدة رجعيّة ؛ لكونها أقلّ وأدنى ؛ لأنَّ المفوض إليها لم يكن أصل الطلاق حتى لا يقع بعدم مشيئتها بل الكيفية، فإذا لم توجد مشيئتها وجد الأصل في ضمن الأقل الأدنى، وإن شاءت كيفية من الكيفيات، فإن وافقت مشيئتها نية الزوج يقعُ ذلك ثلاثاً كان أو واحدة بائنة كانت أو رجعية، وإن خالفت يتساقطان للتعارض، ويبقى الأصل، فيوجد في الأدنى الأقل. فإن قلت : لم احتاج هذا التفويض إلى نيّة الزوج دون سائر التفويضات؟ قلت : لأنّ المفوّض هاهنا حال الطلاق، وهو متنوّع بين البينونة والعدد، فيحتاجُ إلى النيّة لتعيين أحدهما ، بخلاف سائر التفويضات. [٢]قوله : أو بالقلب ... الخ ؛ أي العكس؛ بأن نوى ثلاثاً ونوت واحدة بائنة. [٣] قوله : وحاصله ... الخ ؛ قال الشارح له في تنقيح الأصول» وشرحه «التوضيح»: تطلق في أنت طالق كيف شئت، وتبقى الكيفية ؛ أي كونها رجعياً أو ...
[3] His statement: 'And in 'how you wish'...'; know that 'how' is originally for interrogation: i.e., asking about the state. It is said: 'How did you become?' i.e., what was your condition in the morning. And it may be stripped of the meaning of interrogation and used in the meaning of manner and state, as in their saying: 'You are divorced how', i.e., according to whatever manner and state you wish. And it is said: It is originally in the position of 'which' interrogative; because the meaning of 'how you wish' in interrogation is 'which state you wish,' so it is borrowed for 'which' relative with the meaning 'you are divorced with whatever manner you wish of manners.' [1] His statement: 'It falls as revocable'; its summary: When the husband says 'You are divorced how you wish,' there is no alternative but either her will for some manner exists or not. If it does not exist, such that she does not will any of the manners of divorce, it falls as one revocable; because it is the least and lowest. Since what was delegated to her was not the origin of divorce so that it would not occur in the absence of her will, but rather the manner. So when her will did not exist, the origin occurs within the least low. And if she wills a manner of the manners, then if her will agrees with the husband's intention, it occurs as such: whether triple, or a single irrevocable, or revocable. And if they conflict, both fall away due to contradiction, and the origin remains, so it occurs in the least low. If you say: Why did this delegation need the husband's intention unlike other delegations? I say: Because what is delegated here is the state of divorce, and it varies between irrevocability and count, so it needs intention to specify one of them, unlike other delegations. [2] His statement: 'or by the heart' etc.; i.e., the opposite; that he intended triple and she intended a single irrevocable. [3] His statement: 'and its summary' etc.; the commentator said in his 'Tanqih al-Usul' and its commentary 'al-Tawdih': She divorces in 'you are divorced how you wish', and the manner remains; i.e., whether it is revocable or...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed ruling on the phrase 'كيف شئت' (how you wish) in delegation. It first explains the linguistic origin of 'كيف' as interrogative about state, then its use as a relative meaning 'any manner you wish.' The core ruling is that the wife has the right to choose the manner (kayfiyyah) of divorce, not the origin. If she makes no choice, a single revocable divorce occurs as the minimum. If she chooses a specific manner (e.g., triple or irrevocable) and it matches the husband's intention, it takes effect accordingly. If they conflict, both are set aside, and the single revocable divorce applies. This is the view of Abu Hanifa. The reason intention is needed here is that the delegated matter is the state of divorce, which has multiple possibilities, unlike other delegations. The commentary then quotes from 'Tanqih al-Usul' and 'al-Tawdih' summarizing that the wife divorces and the manner remains subject to her choice, but with the interaction of the husband's intention.

Key Terms
كيف (kayfa): Originally interrogative about state; here used as a relative meaning 'any manner you wish'.
الكيفية (al-kayfiyyah): The manner or mode of divorce (revocable, irrevocable, triple, etc.).
المشيئة (al-mashi'ah): The wife's volition or choice regarding the mode of divorce.
النية (al-niyyah): The husband's intention regarding the mode of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Triple divorce occurs because the husband's intention controls.
B) A single revocable divorce occurs.
C) No divorce occurs because of conflicting intentions.
D) A single irrevocable divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the delegation 'how you wish' gives the wife control over the mode of divorce, but her choice is limited by the husband's intention, defaulting to a single revocable when they conflict.

6 min
Delegation of Divorce: Dispute over Origin vs. Quality in 'As You Wish'
States Hanafi ruling for tafwid using number words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on delegation using 'kam shi'ta', explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding whether the origin of divorce is delegated, and determine the practical outcome in a given scenario.

Core Idea
When husband says 'as many as you wish', wife may choose the number, but husband's intent also matters; if they conflict, only one revocable divorce falls.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya presents a subtle but crucial intra-Hanafi dispute about the scope of tafwid (delegation) in divorce. When a husband tells his wife 'كم شئت' (as many as you wish), does he delegate only the number (quality) or also the very existence of the divorce? The answer determines what happens if the wife chooses nothing—a practical difference that highlights the conceptual distinction between the origin (asl) and description (kayfiyya) of a legal act.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi positions
Identify the two views: (1) Abu Hanifa holds that only the quality (kayfiyya) of divorce is delegated; the origin (asl) remains with the husband. (2) The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that both quality and origin are delegated to the wife.
The text opens with the first view as the established ruling, then presents the Sahibayn's view.
2
Identify the opposing view's proof
The Sahibayn argue that delegating the quality necessarily includes delegating the origin because legal acts like divorce are known only through their descriptions; without a description, the act has no existence. Suspending the description suspends the act itself.
This is a rational (aqli) proof based on epistemology of legal acts.
3
Analyse Abu Hanifa's response
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between the origin and the description. The husband is the origin of divorce; the wife's delegation is derived from him. If the husband's and wife's intentions conflict, both drop, and the original one revocable divorce remains.
His response relies on the principle that when two conflicting intentions cancel out, the original (asl) prevails.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the wife chooses nothing, according to Abu Hanifa one revocable divorce falls (since the origin was not delegated), while according to the Sahibayn nothing falls (since the origin was delegated and she did not choose).
This is the key point for actual fatwa in such cases.
5
Note the unresolved tarjih
The passage does not explicitly state which view is mufta bihi, but Abu Hanifa's view is presented first and appears to be the basis of the discussion. Later works (al-Tawdih, al-Talwih) contain further analysis.
Students should be aware that this ikhtilaf remains a subject of scholarly discussion.

وفي : كم شئت، أو ما شئت، طلقت ما شاءت في مجلسها لا بعده أما إن شاءت ، فإن وافق مشيئته مشيئتها في البائن ، أو الثَّلاث وقع ما اتفقا عليه ، وإن خالفها تقع رجعية ؛ لأنه لا بُدَّ من اعتبار مشيئتها ؛ لأنَّ الزَّوجَ فوَّضَ إليها ، ولا بد أيضاً من اعتبار مشيئته ؛ لأنَّ مشيئتها مستفادة من الزوج ؛ فإذا تعارضا تساقطا، فبقي الأصل، أي الواحدة الرجعية، وإن لم توجد مشيئة الزوج تعتبر مشيئة المرأة في الكيفية، وأما عندهما فكما أن الكيفية مفوّضةٌ إليها ، فأصلُ) الطلاق مفوض إليها أيضاً. وفي : كم شئت، أو ما شئت، طَلَقَتْ ما شاءت في مجلسها لا بعده بأنها خفيفة أو غليظة مفوّضة إليها إن لم ينو الزوج ، وإن نوى فإن اتفقا فذلك، وإلا فرجعيّة، وهذا لأنه لَمَّا فوّض الكيفية إليها ، فإن لم ينو الزوج اعتبر نيتها ، وإن نوى الزوج، فإن اتفق نيتهما يقعُ ما نويا، وإن اختلفت فلا بُدَّ من اعتبار النيتين، أما نيتها فلأنه فوّض إليها ، وأمّا نيّته فلأنّ الزوج هو الأصل في إيقاع الطلاق، فإذا تعارضا سقطا فبقى أصل الطلاق وهو الرجعي. [١]قوله : وافق مشيئته ؛ بأن شاءت الثلاث وشاءَ هو أيضا الثلاث، أو شاء الواحد. (٢) قوله : فأصلُ الطلاق مفوّض ؛ فإن لم تشأ شيئاً لم يقع شيء ؛ وذلك لأنه فوض إليها كلّ حال حتى الرجعة فيلزم تفويض نفس الطلاق ضرورة أنه لا يكون بدون حال من الأحوال ووصف من الأوصاف. وتحقيقه على ما ذكره الأصوليون : أنّ ما لا يكون محسوساً كالتصرفات الشرعية من الطلاق والعتاق والنكاح والبيع وغيرها حاله وأصله سواء ؛ لأن وجوده لما لم يكن محسوساً كانت معرفة وجوده بآثاره ،وأوصافه، فافتقرت معرفة ثبوته إلى معرفة آثاره ووصفه كثبوت الملك في البيع والحلّ في النكاح والوصف أيضاً مفترقا إلى الأصل، فاستويا وصار تعليق الوصف تعليق الأصل. وللشارح له في «التوضيح» في توجيه مذهبهما مسلك آخر، قد ردّ عليه في «التلويح» بوجوه إن شئت الاطلاع فارجع إليه.
And regarding: 'As many as you wish' or 'Whatever you wish', she divorces what she wishes in her sitting not after it. If she wishes, and his wish agrees with her wish regarding the irrevocable or three divorces, what they agreed upon occurs. If his wish differs from hers, a revocable divorce occurs; because it is necessary to consider her wish since the husband delegated to her, and also necessary to consider his wish because her wish is derived from the husband; when they conflict, both drop, and the original remains, i.e., one revocable divorce. And if the husband's wish does not exist, the woman's wish regarding the quality is considered. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), just as the quality is delegated to her, the origin of divorce is also delegated to her. And regarding: 'As many as you wish' or 'Whatever you wish', she divorces what she wishes in her sitting not after it, whether light or heavy, delegated to her if the husband did not intend. If he intended, if they agree then that, otherwise revocable. This is because when he delegated the quality, if the husband did not intend, her intention is considered; if the husband intended, if their intentions agree, what they intended occurs, and if they differ, both intentions must be considered: her intention because he delegated to her, and his intention because the husband is the origin in effecting divorce. When they conflict, both drop and the origin of divorce, which is revocable, remains. [1] His saying: 'agrees with his wish' – as if she wishes three and he also wishes three, or he wishes one. (2) His saying: 'So the origin of divorce is delegated' – if she does not wish anything, nothing occurs; because he delegated to her every state including the revocable, so delegation of the very divorce necessarily follows, since it cannot exist without a state and description. Its verification according to the usuliyyun: What is not sensible, like legal acts of divorce, manumission, marriage, sale, etc., its state and origin are the same; because since its existence is not sensible, knowledge of its existence is through its effects and descriptions, so knowledge of its existence requires knowledge of its effects and description, like the establishment of ownership in sale and permissibility in marriage, and the description is also separate from the origin, so they are equal and suspending the description becomes suspending the origin. The commentator in 'al-Tawdih' has another approach in explaining their madhhab, which is refuted in 'al-Talwih' with several points; if you wish, refer to it.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the delegation (tafwid) of divorce using expressions like 'as many as you wish' (كم شئت) or 'whatever you wish' (ما شئت). The ruling is a ruling_statement with accompanying rational proof. The author first states the position of the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad? Actually here the text distinguishes 'A' (Abu Hanifa) and 'the two' (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). According to the first view (attributed to Abu Hanifa and the earlier text), when the husband says these words, the wife may choose the number of divorces (one, two, or three) but only in the sitting, and if her choice matches the husband's implicit intent? Actually the text says 'if his wish agrees with her wish' meaning if the husband also intended that number, it falls; if they differ, only one revocable divorce falls. If the husband had no specific intention, her choice governs the quality (ba'in or raj'i?). The second view of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) is that the origin of divorce itself is delegated to her, so if she chooses nothing, nothing falls. The commentary then explains that the delegation of the quality (kayfiyya) necessarily includes delegation of the original divorce because legal acts are known through their descriptions; suspending the description suspends the act. The author notes that al-Tawdih and al-Talwih contain further discussion. This is a technical ruling concerning the validity and effect of such delegation.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwid): Delegation by the husband to the wife of the right to divorce herself or choose the number/quality of divorce.
كم شئت (kam shi'ta): Expression 'as many as you wish' used to delegate the number of divorces to the wife.
أصل الطلاق (asl al-talaq): The original existence of the divorce act itself, as opposed to its description (e.g., being one or three).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In tafwid with 'kam shi'ta', the delegation of quality does not necessarily delegate the origin of divorce; this distinction leads to different rulings if the wife chooses nothing.

Ruling on Wife Rejecting Delegation of Divorce
States ruling when wife rejects the tafwid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a wife rejects the delegation of divorce and identify what constitutes rejection.

Core Idea
A wife's rejection voids the delegation, whether explicit or implicit.

This lesson addresses the scenario where a husband delegates the power to divorce to his wife, but she rejects it. The text clarifies that rejection, in any form, cancels the delegation entirely. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly applying the rules of tafwid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The husband delegates divorce (tafwid). The wife rejects. The ruling: the delegation becomes void.
2
Recognize forms of rejection
Rejection can be explicit (saying 'I do not divorce') or implicit (showing disinterest by action).
3
Apply the ruling
Once rejection occurs, the wife's option to divorce is cancelled; the husband retains full control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن ردت ارتد . وفي : طلقي نفسك من ثلاث ما شئت، لها أن تطلق ما دونها لا ثلاثاً هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ لأنَّ من للتّبعيض، وعندهما : لها أن تطلق نفسها ثلاثاً، فتكون من للبيان.
And if she rejects, she rejects. And in: 'Divorce yourself from three whatever you wish', she may divorce less than three, not three, according to Abu Hanifa because 'min' is for partitive; and according to the two, she may divorce herself three, so 'min' is for explanation.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This short passage gives two rulings. First, if the wife rejects the delegation (e.g., by saying 'I do not divorce' or by acting with disinterest), her option is cancelled; the delegation becomes void. Second, the text then introduces a new scenario: the husband says 'Divorce yourself from three whatever you wish.' Here, according to Abu Hanifa, she can only divorce less than three (one or two) because the word 'min' (from) indicates partitive meaning, allowing only a part of the three. According to the Sahibayn, she can divorce three as well because 'min' here is for explanation (bayān), clarifying that the number three is the maximum. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute based on the interpretation of the particle 'min'.

The 'Min' Dispute: Partitive vs Explanatory in Tafwid Divorce
Intra-Hanafi dispute over the particle 'min' in tafwid expression

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the meaning of 'min' in the phrase 'min thalath' and derive the resulting rulings for the wife's ability to divorce.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa reads 'min' as partitive restricting to less than three; the Sahibayn read it as explanatory allowing up to three.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the interpretation of the particle 'min' in a tafwid scenario. When a husband says, 'Divorce yourself from three whatever you wish,' the meaning of 'min' determines how many divorces the wife can execute. Understanding this linguistic nuance is key to correctly applying the Hanafi rulings on delegated divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's wording
The phrase is 'طلقي نفسك من ثلاث ما شئت' (divorce yourself from three whatever you wish). The key term is 'min'.
2
Recognize the two interpretations
Abu Hanifa takes 'min' as partitive (tab'eed) – she can take a part of the three. The Sahibayn take it as explanatory (bayan) – it clarifies that three is the maximum.
3
Apply each view
Under Abu Hanifa: she may divorce less than three (one or two). Under Sahibayn: she may divorce up to three.
4
Note the practical difference
The thamarah is whether she can effect a triple divorce or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if she rejects, she rejects. And in: 'Divorce yourself from three whatever you wish', she may divorce less than three, not three, according to Abu Hanifa because 'min' is for partitive; and according to the two, she may divorce herself three, so 'min' is for explanation.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This short passage gives two rulings. First, if the wife rejects the delegation (e.g., by saying 'I do not divorce' or by acting with disinterest), her option is cancelled; the delegation becomes void. Second, the text then introduces a new scenario: the husband says 'Divorce yourself from three whatever you wish.' Here, according to Abu Hanifa, she can only divorce less than three (one or two) because the word 'min' (from) indicates partitive meaning, allowing only a part of the three. According to the Sahibayn, she can divorce three as well because 'min' here is for explanation (bayān), clarifying that the number three is the maximum. This is an intra-Hanafi dispute based on the interpretation of the particle 'min'.

Key Terms
من للتبعيض (min li al-tab'eed): The particle 'min' indicating a part of a whole, yielding the meaning 'some of three'.
من للبيان (min li al-bayan): The particle 'min' used to clarify or explain the preceding noun, here 'three' specifying the maximum.
